Sawm — Fasting
beginner
6 min
Definition and Conditions of Fasting
Defines fasting and states conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of fasting, identify the cause of its obligation, and list the conditions for obligation and validity.

Core Idea
Fasting is refraining with intention; accidental ingestion still nullifies the fast in the Hanafi school.

Fasting (sawm) is a foundational act of worship in Islam. The Hanafi school provides a precise definition that includes both intentional and unintentional nullifiers, distinguishing between conditions that make fasting obligatory and conditions that make it valid. This lesson clarifies the technical definition, the cause of obligation (the sighting of Ramadan), the four conditions for obligation, and the three conditions for validity, along with the ruling on accidental ingestion.

Guided Path
1
Parse the definition
Break down the elements of the Hanafi definition: refraining during daytime, from introducing anything into the stomach (or equivalent) and from sexual pleasure, with intention. Note that accidental introduction (e.g., water going down the throat during rinsing) also nullifies.
The definition is comprehensive: 'imsak' is more than just abstaining from food.
2
Identify the cause (sabab) of obligation
The cause for the obligation of fasting Ramadan is witnessing a part of it, i.e., seeing the new moon or completing 30 days. Each day individually has its own cause for obligation.
The cause triggers the obligation for the entire month, but each day is a separate act of worship.
3
Distinguish conditions of obligation from conditions of validity
Conditions of obligation (Islam, sanity, puberty, knowledge of obligation or being in Dar al-Islam) make fasting required. If any is missing, fasting is not obligatory. Conditions of validity (Islam, purity from menstruation/postpartum, intention) must be present for the fast to be valid. Knowledge of obligation is not required for validity if one is in Dar al-Islam.
Puberty is not a condition of validity; a child's fast is valid and rewarded.
4
Understand accidental nullification
In the Hanafi school, if water enters the throat unintentionally during rinsing, the fast is still nullified, similar to intentional acts. This is because the definition includes 'mistakenly' (khaṭa').
This differs from some other schools; it demonstrates the Hanafi emphasis on the actual occurrence.
5
Note the concise definition
The correct concise definition is: refraining from nullifiers with intention for Allah in its time.
This captures the essence: abstention plus intention.

Source Text

كتاب الصوم أحكام الصوم كتاب الصوم هو الإمساك نهارا عن إدخال شيء عمدا أو خطأ بطنا، أو ما له حكم الباطن، وعن مفعول لقوله: إدخال وهو الدماغ شهوة الفرج بنية من أهله، وسبب وجوب رمضان شهود جزء منه، وكل يوم لوجوب يعني افتراض صومه منه أدائه، وهو فرض أداء وقضاء على من اجتمع فيه أربعة أشياء: وتسمى شروط وجوب صوم رمضان الإسلام والعقل والبلوغ والعلم بالوجوب لمن أسلم بدار الحرب C فلا يجب على الكافر فلا يجب على مجنون فلا يجب على صبي سواء من يحترز عن هو الإمساك إلخ: اعلم أن النهار ضد الليل من الفجر الصادق إلى الغروب، وإطلاق الشيء يشمل مأكولاً عادةً أو غيره، والمخطئ من سبقه ماء المضمضة إلى حلقه، فحكمه حكم العمد في إفساد الصوم والإدخال في البطن مطلق، كان الفم أو الأنف أو من جراحة في الباطن، والإمساك عن شهوة الفرج يشمل الجماع والإنزال بعبث، فإن الصوم يفسد بهما، وإن لم تجب كفارة. قيد بالإدخال احترازاً عن دخول الغبار ونحوه من غير إدخال، وبكونه "عمداً أو خطأ" النسيان وبقوله: "من أهله" احترازاً عن الحائض والنفساء والكافر والمجنون وأهل الصوم هو الشخص المخصوص المجتمع فيه شروط الصحة الثلاث: وهي الإسلام والطهارة من الحيض والنفاس والنية والعلم بالوجوب إن كان بدار الحرب، أو الكون بدارنا، وإن لم يعلم بالوجوب فالإسلام والطهارة شرطا وجوب ،وصحة والعلم بالوجوب أو الكون في دارنا شرط الوجوب فقط، وأما البلوغ والإطاقة فليسا من شروط الصحة؛ لصحة صوم الصبي ويثاب عليه، ولصحة صوم من جن أو أغمي عليه بعد النية، والحد الصحيح المختصر للصوم هو الإمساك عن المفطرات المنوي لله تعالى بإذنه في وقته. وسبب إلخ : اعلم أن سبب وجوب رمضان شهود جزء من الشهر ليله أو نهاره، وكل يوم سبب وجوب أدائه؛ لأنها عبادات متفرقة كتفرق الصلوات في الأوقات، بل أشد لتخلل زمان لا يصلح للصوم أصلاً، وهو الليل، وجمع المصنف بينهما؛ لأنه لا منافاة، فشهود جزء منه سبب لكله، ثم كل يوم سبب لصومه، غاية الأمر أنه تكرر سبب وجوب صوم اليوم باعتبار خصوصه، ودخوله في ضمن غيره فتح القدير بتصرف: ٢٣٤/٢] سبب لوجوب إلخ فمن بلغ أو أسلم، يلزمه ما بقي . منه لا ما مضى. [مراقي الفلاح: ٦٣٣] والعلم بالوجوب إلخ : اعلم أن هذا الشرط الرابع مردّد بين شيئين، فلابد لافتراض صوم رمضان من أحدهما، إما العلم بالوجوب، أو الكون بدار الإسلام والأول: شرط لمن أسلم بدار الحرب وإنما يحصل له العلم الموجب للخطاب إذا أخبره عدلان أو رجل وامرأتان مستوران أو واحد عدل وعندهما لا تشترط العدالة ولا البلوغ والحرية، والثاني: أي الكون بدار الإسلام شرط لمن نشأ بدار الإسلام، فإنه لا عذر له بالجهل.
Book of Fasting. Rulings of Fasting. Book of Fasting. It is refraining during the day from introducing anything intentionally or mistakenly into the stomach, or something that has the ruling of the interior, and from the object of his saying: introduction, which is the brain, and from the pleasure of the private part with intention from its people. And the cause for the obligation of Ramadan is witnessing a part of it, and each day [is a cause] for the obligation of performing its fast, meaning the obligation of its fasting. And it is a duty of performance and make-up upon one in whom four things are combined: called the conditions for the obligation of fasting Ramadan: Islam, sanity, puberty, and knowledge of obligation for one who becomes Muslim in Dar al-Harb. So it is not obligatory upon the disbeliever, not obligatory upon an insane person, not obligatory upon a child, whether... [text: 'from' as a qualification]. 'It is refraining etc.': Know that the day is the opposite of the night from true dawn to sunset, and the absolute 'thing' includes what is usually eaten or other than that. And the mistaken one from whom the water of rinsing precedes to his throat, so his ruling is the ruling of intentional in terms of spoiling the fast. And introduction into the stomach is absolute, whether through the mouth, nose, or from a wound internally. And refraining from the pleasure of the private part includes intercourse and ejaculation through play, for the fast is spoiled by both, even if expiation is not obligatory. He restricted it by 'introduction' to exclude the entry of dust and the like without introduction, and by it being 'intentionally or mistakenly' to exclude forgetfulness, and by his saying 'from its people' to exclude the menstruating woman, the postpartum woman, the disbeliever, and the insane. And 'people of the fast' is the specific person in whom the three conditions of validity are combined: Islam, purity from menstruation and postpartum bleeding, and intention, and knowledge of obligation if in Dar al-Harb, or being in our land. Even if he does not know the obligation, Islam and purity are conditions for obligation and validity, and knowledge of obligation or being in our land is a condition for obligation only. As for puberty and ability, they are not conditions for validity, because the fast of a child is valid and he is rewarded for it, and the fast of one who becomes insane or faints after intention is valid. The correct and concise definition of fasting is: refraining from nullifiers with intention for Allah the Exalted with His permission in its time. 'And the cause etc.': Know that the cause for the obligation of Ramadan is witnessing a part of the month, its night or its day. And each day is a cause for the obligation of performing it, because they are separate acts of worship like the separation of prayers in times, rather more so due to the interposition of a time that is not suitable for fasting at all, which is the night. The author mentioned both because there is no contradiction. Witnessing a part is a cause for all of it, then each day is a cause for its fast, the net result being that the cause for the obligation of the day's fast is repeated by considering its specificity and its inclusion within the whole. [Fath al-Qadir with adaptation 2:234] 'A cause for obligation etc.': So whoever reaches puberty or becomes Muslim must observe what remains of it, not what has passed. [Maraqi al-Falah: 633] 'And knowledge of obligation etc.': Know that this fourth condition is between two things: either knowledge of obligation or being in Dar al-Islam. The first is a condition for one who becomes Muslim in Dar al-Harb. And he only obtains the knowledge that necessitates the address if two just men or a man and two concealed women or one just man inform him, while according to them [i.e., the two scholars?], justice, puberty, and freedom are not conditions. The second, i.e., being in Dar al-Islam, is a condition for one who grew up in Dar al-Islam, for he has no excuse of ignorance.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Fasting (Kitab al-Sawm) with a precise technical definition of fasting and its conditions. The author first defines sawm (fasting) as refraining during the daytime from intentionally or mistakenly introducing anything into the stomach (or what has the ruling of the interior) and from engaging in sexual desire with intention. The commentary explains that 'introduction' includes any entry via mouth, nose, or a wound, and that the mistake (like water entering the throat during rinsing) still nullifies the fast. Sexual pleasure includes both intercourse and ejaculation due to play, and both spoil the fast but not necessarily require expiation (kaffarah). The passage then clarifies that the cause (sabab) for the obligation of Ramadan is witnessing a part of it (shuhud juz' min al-shahr), meaning seeing the new moon or completing the month. Each day is individually a cause for its own fast. Fasting is a duty of performance (ada') and make-up (qada') upon one who has four conditions: Islam, sanity, puberty, and knowledge of obligation (for one in Dar al-Harb) or being in Dar al-Islam. Thus it is not obligatory upon a disbeliever, an insane person, or a child. The commentary distinguishes between conditions of obligation (shurut al-wujub) and conditions of validity (shurut al-sihhah). The conditions of validity are: Islam, purity from menstruation and postpartum bleeding, and intention. Knowledge of obligation or being in Dar al-Islam is a condition of obligation only, not validity. Puberty is not a condition of validity; a child's fast is valid and rewarded. The correct concise definition of fasting is: refraining from nullifiers (mufattirat) with intention for Allah in its proper time. The passage ends by discussing how knowledge of obligation is acquired: for one who becomes Muslim in Dar al-Harb, it requires two just men or one man and two women or one just man as witnesses; for one in Dar al-Islam, ignorance is no excuse.

Key Terms
الصوم (al-sawm): Refraining during the daytime from introducing anything into the stomach (or equivalent) and from sexual pleasure, with intention.
شروط الوجوب (shurut al-wujub): Conditions that make fasting obligatory: Islam, sanity, puberty, and knowledge of obligation or being in Dar al-Islam.
دار الإسلام (dar al-islam): A land where Islamic law is applied and Muslims are safe; for fasting, it eliminates the excuse of ignorance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition in this passage, what is the cause (sabab) for the obligation of fasting Ramadan?
A) The sighting of the new moon for each day.
B) Witnessing a part of the month of Ramadan, whether night or day.
C) The first day of Ramadan.
D) The arrival of the month of Shawwal.

Key Takeaway
Fasting is defined as abstention with intention; accidental nullifiers still break the fast, and the obligation depends on four conditions: Islam, sanity, puberty, and knowledge of the obligation (or being in Dar al-Islam).

Sawm — Fasting

The Six (and Eight) Categories of Fasting in the Hanafi School
Classifies fasting into six categories with expansion to eight

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six categories of fasting according to the Hanafi school, distinguish between fard and wajib, and explain how the six categories expand to eight when considering specified and unspecified forms.

Core Idea
Fasting is classified into six types; the key distinction is between fard (qat'i) and wajib (zanni), affecting obligation and compensation.

In this passage from Nur al-Idah, the author presents a systematic classification of fasting into six categories. This taxonomy is foundational for understanding the Hanafi rulings on obligation, compensation, and permissibility. The passage also highlights an intra-madhhab difference regarding expiation fasts, which are considered fard in the apparent view but wajib by some. We will walk through each category and its examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six categories
List the six terms: fard, wajib, sunnah, mandub, nafl, makruh. Note the Hanafi order and hierarchy.
Fard and wajib are the only obligatory types, with a subtle epistemological difference.
2
Distinguish fard from wajib
Fard is proven by qat'i (certain) evidence; wajib by zanni (speculative) evidence. Fard includes Ramadan and expiations/vows in the apparent view; wajib includes qada of a spoiled nafl fast.
This distinction has practical consequences: missing a fard requires immediate qada, while missing a wajib may have different compensations.
3
Expand to eight categories
Fard and wajib each have two subcategories: specified (mu'ayyan) and unspecified (ghayr mu'ayyan). For example, Ramadan is specified fard, qada of Ramadan is unspecified fard.
This expansion comes from the commentary; the matn only lists six.
4
Recognize the makruh category
Makruh fasts include days like the two Eids. The passage notes that such fasts are valid but sinful due to rejecting Allah's hospitality.
This shows that validity and reward are separate: a makruh fast is technically valid but carries sin.
5
Apply to examples
Test yourself: is the qada of a broken voluntary fast fard or wajib? (Answer: wajib). Is a vowed fast fard or wajib? (Answer: fard in the apparent view).
This reinforces the categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في صفة الصوم وتقسيمه ينقسم الصوم إلى ستة أقسام ،فرض وواجب ومسنون، ومندوب، ونفل، ومكروه، أما الفرض فهو صوم رمضان أداء وقضاء، وصوم الكفارات والمنذور في الأظهر، وأما الواجب فهو قضاء ما أفسده من صوم نفل . وهو عبارة عن تفريغ الذمة في وقته. (مراقي الفلاح الصحة فلا تجب على مريض وحائض ونفساء. النية: أراد بالنية النية في وقتها والوقت بالنسبة لأداء رمضان بعد الغروب إلى قبيل الضحوة، ففي أي جزء منه وجدت النية صح، وبالنسبة لقضائه الليل كله ولا تجزئ النية بعد طلوع الفجر. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٦٣٥] سقوط الواجب إلخ هو مقيد بما إذا لم يكن منهيا عنه، فإن كان منهيا كصوم يوم النحر فحكمه الصحة، والخروج عن العهدة والإثم بالإعراض عن ضيافة الله تعالى. ستة أقسام أي إجمالا، وبالتفصيل هي ثمانية؛ لأن الفرض إما معين وهو صوم رمضان أداء، أو غير معين: وهو صومه قضاء والواجب كذلك، فالمعين كالنذر المعين وغير المعين كالنذر المطلق، أفاده في "الدر". حاشية الطحطاوي: ٦٣٧] وصوم الكفارات مثل كفارة الظهار، وكفارة القتل خطأ، وكفارة اليمين، وكفارة جزاء الصيد، وفدية الأذى في الإحرام في الأظهر وقيل: إنه واجب؛ لأنه خص من آية ﴿وَلْيُوفُوا نُذُورَهُمْ (الحج: (۲۹) النذر بما ليس من جنسه واجب كعيادة المريض، فلم يبق قطعيا، وصار كخبر الواحد، وبمثله يثبت الوجوب لا الفرض [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٦٣٨] فهو قضاء إلخ: أي إذا صام أحد صوم نفل ثم وجب عليه قضاؤه، وهذا صوم واجب. أفسده،
Section on the description of fasting and its division: Fasting is divided into six categories: fard, wajib, sunnah, mandub, nafl, and makruh. As for fard, it is the fast of Ramadan (performance and make-up), and the fasts of expiations and vows in the most apparent view. As for wajib, it is making up what one spoils of a supererogatory fast... (commentary continues with explanations of categories and details).
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage classifies fasts into six main types, then expands that to eight when considering specified vs. unspecified. Fard includes Ramadan (both on time and qada), expiation fasts (e.g., zihar, accidental killing, oath-breaking, hunting compensation, and fidyah for harm during ihram in the apparent view), and vowed fasts. There is a difference of opinion whether expiation fasts are fard or wajib; the text says 'in the apparent view' they are fard. Wajib fasts are primarily the qada of a broken nafl fast—if one starts a voluntary fast and then invalidates it, making it up becomes obligatory. The commentary clarifies that fard and wajib each have specified and unspecified forms. The passage also notes that if a fast is prohibited (like on Eid), the ruling is validity but sin for rejecting Allah's hospitality.

Key Terms
الفرض (al-fard): An obligatory act proven by qat'i evidence; includes Ramadan and expiations/vows in the apparent view.
الواجب (al-wajib): An obligatory act proven by zanni evidence; here specifically the qada of a broken nafl fast.
النفل (al-nafl): Voluntary fasts beyond the obligatory; includes sunnah, mandub, and general voluntary fasts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what type of fast is the qada (make-up) of a broken supererogatory fast?
A) Fard
B) Wajib
C) Sunnah
D) Makruh

Key Takeaway
Fasting is classified into six categories in the Hanafi school; the crucial distinction is between fard (certain obligation) and wajib (speculative obligation), each with practical consequences for qada and compensation.

Sawm — Fasting
beginner
8 min
The Six Categories of Fasting in Hanafi Fiqh
Classification of fasting types with conditions and pillar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six categories of fasting, distinguish between farḍ and wājib, and identify the key conditions and pillar of a valid fast.

Core Idea
Fasting in Hanafi fiqh is not monolithic — it spans six distinct legal categories from obligatory to disliked, each with unique rules.

This passage from Nur al-Idah provides a complete taxonomy of fasting types in the Hanafi school. Understanding this classification is essential for determining the ruling of any fast: whether it is mandatory, recommended, or discouraged, and whether it requires intention, consecutiveness, or make-up.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching structure
The passage first lists conditions for fasting, then its pillar, then divides fasting into six types (farḍ, wājib, sunnah, mandūb, nafl, makrūh) with subcategories.
2
Define each type using the text
farḍ: Ramadan, expiations, vows (in the more apparent view). wājib: make-up of invalidated voluntary fast. sunnah: Ashura with 9th. mandūb: three white days, Mondays/Thursdays, six of Shawwal. nafl: other voluntary. makrūh: tanzīhī (Ashura alone) and taḥrīmī (Eids, Tashrīq, singling out Friday/Saturday).
3
Note the conditions for validity and pillar
Conditions: intention (niyyah), free from menstruation/postpartum bleeding, free from invalidators. Pillar: refraining from the two appetites (eating/drinking and sexual intercourse) from dawn to sunset.
4
Understand the Hanafi distinction between farḍ and wājib
farḍ is proven by definitive evidence (qaṭ‘ī), wājib by speculative evidence (ẓannī). This affects the obligation of make-up and the sin of omission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم
صفة الصوم وأقسامه
أو الكون بدار الإسلام ويشترط لوجوب أدائه الصحة من مرض وحيض ونفاس، والإقامة، ويشترط لصحة أدائه ثلاثة النية، والخلو عما ينافيه من حيض ونفاس، فلا يجب على مسافر وعما يفسده، ولا يشترط الخلو عن الجنابة وركنه الكف عن قضاء شهوتي البطن والفرج وما ألحق بهما، وحكمه سقوط الواجب عن الذمة، والثواب في الآخرة، والله أعلم.
فصل في صفة الصوم وتقسيمه
ينقسم الصوم إلى ستة أقسام، فرض وواجب ومسنون، ومندوب، ونفل، ومكروه، أما الفرض فهو صوم رمضان أداء وقضاء، وصوم الكفارات والمنذور في الأظهر، وأما الواجب فهو قضاء ما أفسده من صوم نفل. وهو عبارة عن تفريغ الذمة في وقته. (مراقي الفلاح) الصحة فلا تجب على مريض وحائض ونفساء. النية: أراد بالنية النية في وقتها والوقت بالنسبة لأداء رمضان بعد الغروب إلى قبيل الضحوة، ففي أي جزء منه وجدت النية صح، وبالنسبة لقضائه الليل كله ولا تجزئ النية بعد طلوع الفجر. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٦٣٥] سقوط الواجب إلخ هو مقيد بما إذا لم يكن منهيا عنه، فإن كان منهيا كصوم يوم النحر فحكمه الصحة، والخروج عن العهدة والإثم بالإعراض عن ضيافة الله تعالى. ستة أقسام أي إجمالا، وبالتفصيل هي ثمانية؛ لأن الفرض إما معين وهو صوم رمضان أداء، أو غير معين: وهو صومه قضاء والواجب كذلك، فالمعين كالنذر المعين وغير المعين كالنذر المطلق، أفاده في "الدر". حاشية الطحطاوي: ٦٣٧] وصوم الكفارات مثل كفارة الظهار، وكفارة القتل خطأ، وكفارة اليمين، وكفارة جزاء الصيد، وفدية الأذى في الإحرام في الأظهر وقيل: إنه واجب؛ لأنه خص من آية ﴿وَلْيُوفُوا نُذُورَهُمْ (الحج: (٢٩) النذر بما ليس من جنسه واجب كعيادة المريض، فلم يبق قطعيا، وصار كخبر الواحد، وبمثله يثبت الوجوب لا الفرض [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٦٣٨] فهو قضاء إلخ: أي إذا صام أحد صوم نفل ثم وجب عليه قضاؤه، وهذا صوم واجب.
وأما المسنون فهو صوم عاشوراء مع التاسع وأما المندوب: فهو صوم ثلاثة من كل شهر، ويندب كونها الأيام البيض وهي: الثالث عشر والرابع عشر والخامس عشر، وصوم يوم الاثنين والخميس، وصوم ست من شوال، ثم قيل: الأفضل وصلها، وقيل تفريقها وكل صوم ثبت طلبه والوعد عليه بالسنة، كصوم داود عليه السلام كان يصوم يوما ويفطر يوما، وهو أفضل الصيام، وأحبه إلى الله تعالى. وأما النفل: فهو سوى ذلك مما لم يثبت كراهيته وأما المكروه فهو قسمان: مكروه تنزيها، ومكروه تحريما، الأول: صوم عاشوراء منفردا عن التاسع، والثاني: صوم العيدين، وأيام التشريق، وكره إفراد يوم الجمعة وإفراد يوم السبت، ويوم النيروز أو المهرجان، ويندب كونها إلخ: أفاد أن صوم ثلاثة أيام من الشهر أيا كانت مندوب وكونها خصوص هذه الأيام مندوب آخر، فمن صام غيرها منه أتى بأحد المندوبين. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٦٣٩] الأيام البيض: سميت بذلك؛ لتكامل ضوء الهلال، وشدة البياض فيها مراقي الفلاح: ٦٣٩] فالمراد بياض ليلها. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٦٣٩] الأفضل وصلها : اعلم أن الصوم اللازم ثلاثة عشر قسما سبعة منها يجب فيها التتابع، وهي رمضان، وكفارة القتل، وكفارة اليمين وكفارة الظهار، وكفارة الإفطار في رمضان والنذر المعين وغير المعين إذا التزم فيه التتابع أو نواه إلا أن صوم كفارة القتل والظهار، والإفطار واليمين والنذر المطلق إذا ذكر فيه التتابع أو نواه إذا أفطر في خلاله استقبله واستأنفه، وصوم رمضان والنذر المعين لا يلزم فيهما الاستئناف بقطع التتابع، وستة لا يجب فيها التتابع وهي قضاء رمضان، وصوم المتعة، كفارة الحلق، وصوم جزاء الصيد، المطلق عن ذكر التتابع أو نيته، وصوم اليمين بأن قال: والله لأصومن شهرا. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٦٣٨] النيروز :أصله، نوروز، لكن لما لم يكن في أوزان العرب "فوعول" أبدلوا الواو ياء، وهو يوم في طرف الربيع وهو اليوم الذي تحل فيه الشمس ببرج الحمل. [مراقي الفلاح: ٦٤١] والمهرجان معرب مهرگان، وهو يوم في طرف الخريف [مراقي الفلاح: (٦٤١] والمراد منه: أول حلول الشمس في الميزان، وهذا اليوم والذي قبله عيدان للفرس. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٦٤١]
Book of Fasting. Description of fasting and its divisions. Or being in the abode of Islam. And for the obligation of its performance, health from illness, menstruation, and postpartum bleeding, and residence are conditions. And for the validity of its performance, three things are conditions: intention, being free from menstruation and postpartum bleeding, so it is not obligatory upon a traveller, and being free from what invalidates it. And being free from major ritual impurity is not a condition. Its pillar is refraining from fulfilling the two appetites of the stomach and the private parts and what is attached to them. Its ruling is the discharge of the obligation from the liability and reward in the Hereafter. And Allah knows best. Section on the description of fasting and its division. Fasting is divided into six categories: fard, wajib, sunnah, mandub, nafl, and makruh. As for fard, it is the fast of Ramadan as performance and makeup, and the fasts of expiations and the vowed fast in the more apparent view. As for wajib, it is making up what he invalidated of voluntary fast. It is the emptying of the liability in its time. (Maraqi al-Falah) Health: so it is not obligatory upon a sick person, menstruating woman, or woman in postpartum bleeding. Intention: he intended by intention the intention at its time, and the time for performing Ramadan is from after sunset to before the forenoon, so in any part of it the intention is found it is valid, and for its makeup the whole night, and intention after dawn is not sufficient. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 635] 'Discharge of the obligation etc.' is restricted to when it is not prohibited, for if it is prohibited like the fast of the day of Nahr, its ruling is validity, and exiting from responsibility and sin by turning away from the hospitality of Allah Almighty. Six categories i.e. in summary, and in detail they are eight because fard is either specific which is the fast of Ramadan as performance, or non-specific which is its makeup, and likewise wajib, so the specific is like a specific vow and the non-specific like an absolute vow, as stated in al-Durr. Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 637] And the fasts of expiations like the expiation of zihar, expiation of accidental killing, expiation of an oath, expiation of hunting compensation, and the ransom of harm in ihram in the more apparent view, and it is said it is wajib because it is specified from the verse 'And let them fulfill their vows' (al-Hajj 22:29) the vow which is not of its type of wajib like visiting a sick person, so it did not remain definitive and became like a solitary report, and by such wajib is established not fard [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 638] So it is makeup etc.: i.e. when a person fasts a voluntary fast and then its makeup becomes obligatory, and this is a wajib fast. As for sunnah, it is the fast of Ashura along with the ninth. As for mandub, it is the fast of three from each month, and it is recommended that they be the white days which are the thirteenth, fourteenth, and fifteenth, and the fast of Monday and Thursday, and the fast of six from Shawwal, then it is said: it is better to join them, and it is said to separate them. And every fast whose seeking and promise is established by the Sunnah, like the fast of Dawud peace be upon him; he used to fast one day and break one day, and it is the best of fasting and most beloved to Allah Almighty. As for nafl, it is other than that whose dislike has not been established. As for makruh, it is two types: makruh tanzihi and makruh tahrimi. The first: fasting Ashura alone without the ninth. The second: fasting the two Eids and the days of Tashriq, and it is disliked to single out Friday and to single out Saturday, and the day of Nayruz or Mihrajan. 'And it is recommended that they be etc.' indicates that fasting three days of the month whatever they are is mandub, and their being specifically these days is another mandub, so whoever fasts other than them has performed one of the two mandubs. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 639] The white days: they are named thus because of the completion of the moonlight and the intensity of whiteness in them. Maraqi al-Falah: 639] So the intended meaning is the whiteness of their nights. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 639] 'It is better to join them': Know that obligatory fasts are thirteen categories, seven of which require consecutiveness: Ramadan, expiation of killing, expiation of an oath, expiation of zihar, expiation of breaking fast in Ramadan, and a specific and non-specific vow if consecutiveness is committed or intended. However, the fast of expiation of killing, zihar, breaking fast, oath, and absolute vow if consecutiveness is mentioned or intended, if one breaks it during it, he restarts and resumes; and the fast of Ramadan and specific vow do not require restarting by breaking consecutiveness. And six in which consecutiveness is not required: makeup of Ramadan, the fast of tamattu', expiation of shaving, the fast of hunting compensation, the absolute (without mention of consecutiveness or intention), and the fast of an oath such as saying 'By Allah I will fast a month.' [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 638] Nayruz: its original is Nawruz, but since it is not among the measures of the Arabs 'faw'ul', they substituted the waw with ya, and it is a day at the onset of spring, the day when the sun enters the constellation of Aries. [Maraqi al-Falah: 641] And Mihrajan is an Arabicized form of Mihragan, and it is a day at the onset of autumn [Maraqi al-Falah: 641] and what is intended is the first entry of the sun into Libra, and this day and the one before it are two festivals of the Persians. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 641]
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition of the types of fasting in the Hanafi school. It begins by stating the conditions for the obligation of fasting: being resident in the Islamic abode, being healthy (not sick), and being free from menstruation and postpartum bleeding for women. For the validity of the fast, three conditions are listed: intention (niyyah), being free from menstruation and postpartum bleeding, and being free from anything that invalidates the fast. The pillar (rukn) of fasting is refraining from the two appetites — eating/drinking and sexual intercourse, and anything analogous. Then the passage divides fasting into six categories: farḍ, wājib, sunnah, mandūb, nafl, and makrūh. Farḍ includes the Ramadan fast (both in its time and as make-up) and expiatory and vowed fasts (in the more apparent view). Wājib is make-up for a voluntary fast that was invalidated — this is a distinct obligatory category below farḍ. Sunnah is exemplified by fasting on Ashura (10th Muharram) with the 9th. Mandūb includes fasting three days every month (preferably the white days: 13th, 14th, 15th), Mondays, Thursdays, and six days of Shawwal. Nafl is any voluntary fast not falling under the above categories. Makrūh is divided into tanzīhī (disliked but not sinful, e.g., fasting Ashura alone without the 9th) and taḥrīmī (prohibitively disliked, e.g., fasting on the two Eids and the days of Tashrīq). The commentary further explains that detailed categories reach eight, and discusses consecutiveness for obligatory fasts. The overall aim is to teach the precise gradations of fasting obligations and recommendations in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
فرض (farḍ): An obligation established by decisive evidence (qaṭ‘ī), such as the fast of Ramadan, expiatory fasts, and vowed fasts in the more apparent view.
واجب (wājib): An obligation proven by speculative evidence (ẓannī), such as making up a voluntary fast that was invalidated.
مسنون (masnūn): A recommended act that the Prophet consistently performed, such as fasting on Ashura with the ninth.
مندوب (mandūb): A recommended act that the Prophet did sometimes, such as fasting three days each month, Monday and Thursday, and six of Shawwal.
نفل (nafl): Voluntary acts of worship not confirmed as sunnah or mandūb, but without any dislikedness.
مكروه (makrūh): Disliked; divided into makrūh tanzīhī (lightly disliked) and makrūh taḥrīmī (prohibitively disliked). Examples: fasting Ashura alone (tanzīhī), fasting on Eid days (taḥrīmī).
الأيام البيض (al-ayyām al-bīḍ): The white days: the 13th, 14th, and 15th of each lunar month, so called because their nights are brightly lit by the full moon.
شهوتي البطن والفرج (shahwatay al-baṭn wa al-farj): The two appetites: the appetite of the stomach (eating and drinking) and the appetite of the private parts (sexual intercourse) — the essence of the fast is to refrain from satisfying these.
تتابع (tatābu‘): Consecutiveness: the requirement to fast days in a row without interruption for certain obligatory fasts such as Ramadan, expiations, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of fasting is considered wājib (obligatory) but not farḍ?
A) Fasting Ramadan
B) Fasting six days of Shawwal
C) Making up a voluntary fast that was invalidated
D) Fasting on Ashura with the 9th

Key Takeaway
Fasting in the Hanafi school is divided into six legal categories (farḍ, wājib, sunnah, mandūb, nafl, makrūh), each with distinct rulings and evidentiary weight.

Sawm — Fasting
beginner
8 min
Categories of Voluntary Fasts: Sunnah, Mandub, Nafl, and Disliked
Classifies types of voluntary fasts and their rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the five categories of voluntary fasting (sunnah, mandub, nafl, makruh tanzihi, makruh tahrimi) and identify examples for each as per the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Voluntary fasts are graded: sunnah, mandub, nafl, and two disliked levels — know the hierarchy to maximize reward.

This lesson from Nur al-Idah lays out the Hanafi classification of voluntary fasts. It lists the most recommended (sunnah), preferred (mandub), general supererogatory (nafl), and two types of disliked (makruh). Understanding this hierarchy helps you choose fasts that bring the most reward and avoid those that are discouraged.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories
Find and list all categories of fasting mentioned in the text: sunnah, mandub, nafl, makruh tanzihi, makruh tahrimi.
2
Note the examples for each
For each category, note the specific fasts given as examples: sunnah – Ashura with the 9th; mandub – three white days, Monday & Thursday, six of Shawwal; nafl – any other voluntary fast not disliked; makruh tanzihi – Ashura alone; makruh tahrimi – Eid days and Tashriq.
Singling out Friday or Saturday, or fasting on Nayruz/Mihrajan, is also disliked.
3
Understand the makruh distinction
The Hanafi school splits disliked into two levels: tanzihi (lesser) and tahrimi (prohibitive). Fasting Ashura alone is tanzihi; fasting on 'Eid or Tashriq is tahrimi.
Makruh tahrimi is closer to haram and should be strictly avoided.
4
Apply the classification
Given a new fast scenario, determine its category using the criteria from the text. For example, fasting on a random Wednesday for no particular reason would be nafl.
Consider whether a specific hadith recommends the fast or if it falls under a disliked category.

وصوم
Book of Fasting. Description of fasting and its divisions. As for the sunnah (recommended) fast, it is fasting on Ashura with the ninth (i.e., the tenth of Muharram). As for the mandub (preferred), it is fasting three days of every month, and it is preferred that they be the white days: the 13th, 14th, and 15th; fasting on Monday and Thursday; fasting six days of Shawwal. Then it was said: it is better to fast them consecutively, and it was said to separate them. And every fast for which seeking and promise is established by the Sunnah, like the fast of Dawud (peace be upon him): he used to fast one day and break fast one day, and it is the best of fasting and most beloved to Allah Almighty. As for the nafl (supererogatory), it is other than that for which dislike is not established. As for the makruh (disliked), it is of two types: makruh tanzihan (disliked as a matter of lesser offense) and makruh tahriman (disliked as a matter of prohibition). The first: fasting Ashura alone without the ninth. The second: fasting the two 'Eids (al-Fitr and al-Adha) and the days of Tashriq (the three days after the day of Nahr). And it is disliked to single out Friday or Saturday for fasting, and the day of Nayruz (Persian New Year) and Mihrajan (Persian festival). [Commentary] "And it is preferred that they be..." indicates that fasting three days of the month, whatever they are, is mandub, and specifically these days is another mandub, so whoever fasts other than them has performed one of the two mandub acts. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 639] The white days: they are so called because of the completeness of the moonlight and the intensity of whiteness therein. [Marqi al-Falah: 639] What is meant is the whiteness of their nights. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 639] "It is better to fast them consecutively": Know that obligatory fasts are thirteen types: seven of them require consecutiveness: Ramadan, expiation for killing, expiation for an oath, expiation for zihar, expiation for breaking fast in Ramadan, and a specified vow and unspecified vow if consecutiveness is stipulated or intended; except that the fast of expiation for killing, zihar, breaking fast, oath, and absolute vow, if consecutiveness is mentioned or intended, then if one breaks fast during it, he must restart and begin anew; Ramadan and a specified vow do not require restarting if consecutiveness is broken. Six do not require consecutiveness: making up missed Ramadan fasts, the fast of tamattu', expiation for shaving (hair), fast as compensation for hunting, absolute regarding mentioning consecutiveness or its intention, and the fast of an oath, such as saying: "By Allah, I will fast a month." [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 638] Nayruz: its origin is 'nuruz', but since 'faw'ul' is not among the patterns of the Arabs, they changed the waw to ya. It is a day at the beginning of spring, the day when the sun enters Aries. [Marqi al-Falah: 641] Mihrajan: an arabicized form of 'Mihragan', a day at the beginning of autumn. [Marqi al-Falah: 641] What is meant is the first entry of the sun into Libra. This day and the one before it are two festivals of the Persians. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 641] And fasting...
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the types of fasting according to the Hanafi school. It begins by describing the sunnah fast: fasting on 'Ashura (10th of Muharram) along with the 9th, which is recommended. The mandub (preferred) fast includes fasting three days of every month, preferably the 'white days' (13th, 14th, 15th), and also Mondays, Thursdays, and six days of Shawwal. The commentator notes that the phrase 'it is preferred that they be' indicates that fasting any three days is mandub, but specifically the white days is an additional virtue. The text then mentions the nafl (supererogatory) fast as anything not falling into the disliked category. The makruh (disliked) is divided into two: makruh tanzihi (lesser dislike, like fasting 'Ashura alone) and makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked, like fasting on 'Eid days and the days of Tashriq). It is also disliked to single out Friday or Saturday, or to fast on Nayruz and Mihrajan (Persian festivals), unless they coincide with one's habit. The commentary then elaborates on the thirteen types of obligatory fasts (al-sawm al-lazim), detailing which require consecutiveness (tataabu') and which do not. This is a useful summary for understanding the legal hierarchy of fasts.

Key Terms
المسنون (al-masnun): The recommended fast that is established by the Sunnah, such as 'Ashura with the ninth.
المندوب (al-mandub): A preferred act that is not as strongly emphasized as sunnah, such as fasting three days of any month.
النفل (al-nafl): Supererogatory act; here it refers to any voluntary fast that is not disliked.
المكروه (al-makruh): Disliked act, divided into two categories: tanzihan (lesser offense) and tahriman (prohibitive offense).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school establishes a clear hierarchy for voluntary fasts; knowing the categories helps you choose the most rewarding fasts and avoid disliked ones.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate

Obligatory Fasts: Types and Consecutiveness
Lists thirteen obligatory fasts and rules on consecutiveness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the thirteen types of obligatory fasts and identify which require consecutive fasting (tataabu') and which do not, according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Seven obligatory fasts require consecutiveness; six do not — know the rule to fulfill your obligations correctly.

The commentary on Nur al-Idah provides a comprehensive list of thirteen obligatory fasts in the Hanafi school. It explains which ones require consecutive fasting and what happens if consecutiveness is broken. This is essential knowledge for anyone who has to make up missed fasts, perform expiation, or fulfill vows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thirteen types
From the commentary, list all thirteen obligatory fasts: Ramadan, qada, expiation for killing, oath, zihar, breaking fast in Ramadan, tamattu', shaving, hunting compensation, and various vows.
The text specifically mentions seven requiring consecutiveness and six not.
2
Group by consecutiveness requirement
Separate the list into those that require tataabu' (consecutive days) and those that do not.
Requiring: Ramadan, expiations, specified vow; Not requiring: qada, tamattu', etc.
3
Note the restart rules
For some expiations, if consecutiveness is broken, one must restart the fast. For Ramadan and specified vow, restart is not required.
Expiations for killing, zihar, breaking fast, and oath: restart if broken.
4
Apply to a scenario
Given a situation (e.g., someone must fast two months for expiation and breaks after one month), determine if they must restart.
Use the categories to decide.

Book of Fasting. Description of fasting and its divisions. As for the sunnah (recommended) fast, it is fasting on Ashura with the ninth (i.e., the tenth of Muharram). As for the mandub (preferred), it is fasting three days of every month, and it is preferred that they be the white days: the 13th, 14th, and 15th; fasting on Monday and Thursday; fasting six days of Shawwal. Then it was said: it is better to fast them consecutively, and it was said to separate them. And every fast for which seeking and promise is established by the Sunnah, like the fast of Dawud (peace be upon him): he used to fast one day and break fast one day, and it is the best of fasting and most beloved to Allah Almighty. As for the nafl (supererogatory), it is other than that for which dislike is not established. As for the makruh (disliked), it is of two types: makruh tanzihan (disliked as a matter of lesser offense) and makruh tahriman (disliked as a matter of prohibition). The first: fasting Ashura alone without the ninth. The second: fasting the two 'Eids (al-Fitr and al-Adha) and the days of Tashriq (the three days after the day of Nahr). And it is disliked to single out Friday or Saturday for fasting, and the day of Nayruz (Persian New Year) and Mihrajan (Persian festival). [Commentary] "And it is preferred that they be..." indicates that fasting three days of the month, whatever they are, is mandub, and specifically these days is another mandub, so whoever fasts other than them has performed one of the two mandub acts. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 639] The white days: they are so called because of the completeness of the moonlight and the intensity of whiteness therein. [Marqi al-Falah: 639] What is meant is the whiteness of their nights. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 639] "It is better to fast them consecutively": Know that obligatory fasts are thirteen types: seven of them require consecutiveness: Ramadan, expiation for killing, expiation for an oath, expiation for zihar, expiation for breaking fast in Ramadan, and a specified vow and unspecified vow if consecutiveness is stipulated or intended; except that the fast of expiation for killing, zihar, breaking fast, oath, and absolute vow, if consecutiveness is mentioned or intended, then if one breaks fast during it, he must restart and begin anew; Ramadan and a specified vow do not require restarting if consecutiveness is broken. Six do not require consecutiveness: making up missed Ramadan fasts, the fast of tamattu', expiation for shaving (hair), fast as compensation for hunting, absolute regarding mentioning consecutiveness or its intention, and the fast of an oath, such as saying: "By Allah, I will fast a month." [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 638] Nayruz: its origin is 'nuruz', but since 'faw'ul' is not among the patterns of the Arabs, they changed the waw to ya. It is a day at the beginning of spring, the day when the sun enters Aries. [Marqi al-Falah: 641] Mihrajan: an arabicized form of 'Mihragan', a day at the beginning of autumn. [Marqi al-Falah: 641] What is meant is the first entry of the sun into Libra. This day and the one before it are two festivals of the Persians. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 641] And fasting...
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the types of fasting according to the Hanafi school. It begins by describing the sunnah fast: fasting on 'Ashura (10th of Muharram) along with the 9th, which is recommended. The mandub (preferred) fast includes fasting three days of every month, preferably the 'white days' (13th, 14th, 15th), and also Mondays, Thursdays, and six days of Shawwal. The commentator notes that the phrase 'it is preferred that they be' indicates that fasting any three days is mandub, but specifically the white days is an additional virtue. The text then mentions the nafl (supererogatory) fast as anything not falling into the disliked category. The makruh (disliked) is divided into two: makruh tanzihi (lesser dislike, like fasting 'Ashura alone) and makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked, like fasting on 'Eid days and the days of Tashriq). It is also disliked to single out Friday or Saturday, or to fast on Nayruz and Mihrajan (Persian festivals), unless they coincide with one's habit. The commentary then elaborates on the thirteen types of obligatory fasts (al-sawm al-lazim), detailing which require consecutiveness (tataabu') and which do not. This is a useful summary for understanding the legal hierarchy of fasts.

Key Terms
التتابع (al-tataabu'): Consecutive fasting without interruption.
الاستئناف (al-isti'naf): Restarting the entire fast from the beginning due to a break in consecutiveness.
النذر المعين (al-nadhr al-mu'ayyan): A vow to fast on specific days (e.g., 'I vow to fast on Monday').

Key Takeaway
Seven obligatory fasts require consecutive days, six do not; for five of the seven, breaking consecutiveness forces a restart.

Fasts Allowing Daytime Intention
Explains which fasts allow daytime intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which three categories of fasts allow intention to be made during the day (before midday) and which require it at night.

Core Idea
Ramadan, specified vows, and nafl fasts allow intention until midday; other obligatory fasts require night intention.

The Hanafi school provides flexibility for certain fasts. While the strict rule requires intention at night for all obligatory fasts, three categories — Ramadan, specified vows, and supererogatory fasts — allow the intention to be made in the daytime. This lesson explains the boundaries and the correct time window.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three fast types
From the text: Ramadan (ada'), specified vow (nazr mu'ayyan), and nafl. These do not require tabayyut or ta'yin.
Remember: nafl includes all non-obligatory fasts (sunnah, mandub, makruh).
2
Recognize the default rule
All other obligatory fasts (qada, kaffarah, etc.) require intention at night and specification.
The text implies this from the exception.
3
Understand the time window
For the three categories, intention can be made from the night until before midday (al-asahh: before duha al-kubra, the forenoon). al-Quduri's statement allowing until noon is less sound.
The correction from al-Tahtawi is important: after duha al-kubra, intention is not valid.
4
Apply with a scenario
If you wake up late on a Ramadan day without night intention, you can still fast if you intend before midday and have not eaten/drunk.
This applies even for a specified vow or nafl fast.

Section on what requires the intention to be made at night (tabayyut) and specified (ta'yin), and what does not require. As for the category that does not require specification of intention nor making it at night: it is performing (the fast of) Ramadan, a specified vow, and supererogatory (nafl) fasts. So it is valid with an intention (made) from the night until before midday according to the most sound opinion. Specified and made at night except that it coincides; i.e., if fasting on the day of Nayruz or other coinciding with one's habit, it is not disliked. For example, if a man regularly fasts on Monday, and fasts according to his habit, it is not disliked. Making intention at night means that intention must be from the night or what is in its ruling, which is concurrent with dawn; indeed that is the principle. And an absolute unspecified intention is not valid. Then know that intention from the night is sufficient for every fast on condition of not retracting from it. Even if one intends at night to fast tomorrow, then resolves to break the fast, he does not become a faster. So if he breaks the fast, there is nothing upon him if it is not Ramadan, but if time passes, it does not suffice him because that intention was nullified by retraction. And if a faster intends to break the fast, he does not break it until he eats. (al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, with modification) The specified vow, etc., like his saying: 'By Allah, I must fast on Thursday of this week.' So if he makes an absolute intention during its night or day up to before midday, it is valid and he is thereby discharged of the vow. (Marqi al-Falah: 642) Nafl: what is meant by nafl is other than obligatory (fard) and wajib, whether it is sunnah, mandub, or makruh. So it is valid with intention, etc. [i.e., all three of these.] (Marqi al-Falah: 642) Know that the reality of intention is his resolve, determined in his heart, to fast tomorrow. And no Muslim is free from this in the nights of the month of Ramadan except rarely, like if he is a sinful, licentious person, or asleep from sunset or before it until dawn, or unconscious. And verbal utterance is not a condition, though pronouncing it is the practice (sunnah) of the shuyukh. (Marqi al-Falah and Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 642) 'Until before' etc.: what is meant is that from the night until this time is the timeframe for intention. So whenever it occurs in any part of this time, the fast is valid. If one intends to fast during the day, intending that he is fasting from the beginning (of the day), even if he intends before noon that he is fasting from the moment he intended from the start of the day, he does not become a faster. Rather it is permissible before the forenoon (duha) if nothing contrary to fasting (like eating, drinking, or intercourse, even forgetfully) occurred before it. If that occurred after dawn, it is not permissible. (Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 642) 'According to the most sound opinion' is a caveat against the apparent meaning of al-Quduri's statement, which is his saying: 'What is between it, i.e., dawn and noon.' Its apparent meaning indicates that if it occurs before noon and after the forenoon (al-dahwa al-kubra), it is valid, but it is not so. (Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 643) Not from...
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This is a detailed ruling on the conditions of intention (niyyah) for fasting. The passage clarifies which fasts require the intention to be made at night (tabayyut) and specified (ta'yin), and which do not. The Hanafi position is that for Ramadan, a specified vow, and nafl (supererogatory) fasts, the intention can be made from the night until before midday (i.e., before the time of duha al-kubra, roughly midday). However, the most sound opinion is that it must be before the forenoon. The text then explains that an absolute intention (without specifying the type of fast) is not valid. It also discusses the effect of changing one's mind after intending: if one intends at night but then decides to break, the intention is nullified; if one intends to break but does not eat, he is still fasting. The commentary by al-Tahtawi clarifies al-Quduri's statement, showing that the time for intention extends to just before the forenoon, not up to noon. The passage also notes that verbal utterance of intention is not required, though it is a recommended practice. The overall ruling: for non-obligatory fasts, intention can be made in the daytime as long as no invalidating action occurred.

Key Terms
تبييت النية (tabayyut al-niyyah): Making the intention at night, i.e., before the break of dawn.
تعيين النية (ta'yin al-niyyah): Specifying the intention for a particular type of fast (e.g., Ramadan, vow, nafl).
النفل (al-nafl): Supererogatory acts; here specifically voluntary fasts that are not obligatory (fard or wajib).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) A supererogatory (nafl) fast
D) An expiation fast (kaffarah)

Key Takeaway
Ramadan, specified vows, and nafl fasts allow intention to be made during the daytime up to before midday; for all other obligatory fasts, intention must be at night and specified.

Conditions of Intention (Niyyah) for Fasting: What Requires Specification and Overnight Intention
Classification of fasts by intention requirements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify Hanafi fasts into two categories based on intention requirements and apply the ruling to scenarios involving intention for Ramadan, qada, kaffara, vow, and nafl, including the internal dispute regarding the sick person.

Core Idea
Intention type and timing vary by fast: some require absolute intention before noon, others need specification and overnight intention.

Intention (niyyah) is an essential condition for the validity of fasting, but the Hanafi school distinguishes between fasts that require an absolute intention (mutlaq) made before half the day and those that demand both specification (ta'yin) and overnight intention (tabyit). This lesson unpacks the classification from Nur al-Idah and clarifies the three opinions about the sick person who mistakenly intends another obligatory fast in Ramadan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of fasts
The passage divides fasts into two categories: (1) those valid with absolute intention before half the day, and (2) those requiring specification and overnight intention. List each category's members from the text.
Category 1 includes Ramadan, specified vow (al-nadhr al-mu'ayyan), and nafl. Category 2 includes qada of Ramadan, qada of broken nafl, all expiatory fasts, and absolute vow (al-nadhr al-mutlaq).
2
Determine the effect of intending another wajib in Ramadan
Note the two scenarios: for a healthy resident, intending another wajib still counts for Ramadan. For a traveller, it falls as the intended wajib. This distinction hinges on the strength of the obligation in context.
The traveller's fast is not as strongly linked to Ramadan because the traveller may not be obliged to fast at all; thus his intention is taken at face value.
3
Analyze the internal ikhtilaf on the sick person
The passage presents three positions: (1) falls for Ramadan (Fakhr al-Islam, Shams al-A'imma), (2) falls for what he intended (most mashayikh, author of al-Hidayah), (3) detailed view depending on harm. Identify the mufta bihi (operative opinion) and the reasoning behind each.
The second opinion is strongly relied upon in later Hanafi fatwa. The detail opinion adds nuance but is not preferred.
4
Apply the rules to a new scenario
Given a scenario (e.g., a healthy resident intends a kaffara during Ramadan but does not specify), determine which category the fast falls into and whether the intention is valid.
Apply the classification: if the fast falls under category 1, absolute intention suffices; if under category 2, both ta'yin and tabyit are required.

ما يشترط تبييت النية فيه.
كتاب الصوم ونصف النهار : من طلوع الفجر إلى وقت الضحوة الكبرى، ويصح أيضا بمطلق النية، وبنية النفل ولو كان مسافرا أو مريضا في الأصح، ويصح أداء رمضان بنية واجب آخر لمن كان صحيحا مقيما، بخلاف المسافر، فإنه يقع عما نواه من الواجب، واختلف الترجيح في المريض إذا نوى واجبا آخر في رمضان، ولا يصح المنذور المعين زمانه بنية واجب غيره، بل يقع عما نواه من الواجب فيه. الناذر لا يسقط
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو ما يشترط فيه تعيين النية وتبييتها فهو قضاء رمضان، وقضاء ما أفسده من نفل، وصوم الكفارات بأنواعها، والمنذور المطلق كقوله: إن شفى الله مريضي فعلي صوم يوم، فحصل الشفاء.
وقت الضحوة: اعلم أن ساعة الزوال نصف النهار ، وهو من طلوع الشمس إلى غروبها، ووقت أداء الصوم من طلوع الفجر إلى غروب الشمس، ونصفه وقت الضحوة الكبرى، فتشترط النية قبلها لتتحقق النية في الأكثر . الكفاية بتغيير: ۲۳۷/۲] ويصح أي كل من أداء رمضان والنذر المعين والنفل [مراقي الفلاح: ٦٤٢] بمطلق النية : أي من غير تقييد بوصف من كونه فرضا أو نفلا.
في الأصح: اعلم أن في النفل عنه روايتين: أصحهما عدم صحة ما ينوي، ووقوعه عن فرض الوقت، فعلم بهذا أن المسافر يصح صومه عن رمضان مطلق النية، وبنية النفل على الأصح فيهما مع وجود الروايتين فيهما، وأما المريض إذا نوى واجبا آخر أو نفلا ففيه ثلاثة أقوال: فقيل: يقع عن رمضان؛ لأنه لما صام التحق بالصحيح، واختاره فخر الإسلام وشمس الأئمة. وقيل: يقع عما نوى كالمسافر، واختاره صاحب "الهداية" وأكثر المشايخ. وقيل: بأنه ظاهر الرواية، وينبغي أن يقع عن رمضان في النفل على الصحيح كالمسافر. وقيل بالتفصيل بين أن يضره الصوم، فتتعلق الرخصة بخوف الزيادة، فيصير كالمسافر ويقع عما نوى، وبين أن لا يضره الصوم كفساد الهضم، فتتعلق الرخصة بحقيقة، فيقع عن فرض الوقت. [البحر الرائق: ٤١٤/٢] بنية واجب آخر كما إذا نوى في رمضان عن كفارة يمين وجبت عليه، أو قضاء رمضان. الترجيح إلخ: أي وقع الاختلاف فيما بينهم في الراجح من الأقوال، فرجح بعضهم قولا وبعضهم قولا آخر، كما بيناه آنفا.
بأنواعها : ككفارة اليمين وصوم التمتع والقران. [مراقي الفلاح: ٦٤٥]
What requires overnight intention. Book of Fasting: Half of the day is from dawn to the time of the great forenoon (al-duha al-kubra). It is also valid with an absolute intention, and with the intention of supererogatory even if one is a traveller or ill according to the most correct. Performing Ramadan is valid with the intention of another obligatory fast for one who is healthy and resident, contrary to the traveller, for it falls as what he intended of the obligatory, and the preferred opinion differs in the ill person if he intends another obligatory fast in Ramadan. A vowed fast with specified time is not valid with the intention of another obligatory, but rather falls as what he intended of the obligatory. The one who vows does not drop it. As for the second category: what requires specification of intention and its overnighting is the make-up of Ramadan, the make-up of what he spoiled of supererogatory, the fast of expiations of all types, and the absolute vow such as saying: If Allah cures my patient, upon me is a fast of a day, then cure occurs. Time of forenoon: Know that the moment of zenith is half of the day, and it is from sunrise to sunset. The time of performing the fast is from dawn to sunset, and its half is the time of the great forenoon, so intention is required before it so that intention is realized in the majority. Al-Kifaya with modification: 237/2. It is valid—i.e., each of performing Ramadan, the specified vow, and supererogatory [Maraqi al-Falah: 642]—with absolute intention: i.e., without restricting with a description of being obligatory or supererogatory. In the most correct: Know that concerning supererogatory there are two narrations: the more correct of them is the invalidity of what he intends, and that it falls as the duty of the time. From this it is understood that the traveller's fast for Ramadan is valid with absolute intention, and with the intention of supererogatory according to the most correct in both with the existence of two narrations in them. As for the ill person if he intends another obligatory or supererogatory, there are three sayings: It is said: it falls for Ramadan because when he fasted he joins the healthy, and Fakhr al-Islam and Shams al-A'imma chose this. And it is said: it falls as what he intended like the traveller, and the author of al-Hidayah and most of the mashayikh chose this. And it is said: it is the apparent of the narration, and it should fall for Ramadan in supererogatory on the correct like the traveller. And it is said with detail between the case that fasting harms him, so the concession is attached to fear of increase, so he becomes like the traveller and falls as what he intended, and the case that it does not harm him like indigestion, so the concession is attached to its reality, so it falls for the duty of the time. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 414/2] With the intention of another obligatory, as when he intends in Ramadan for a sworn expiation that became obligatory upon him, or the make-up of Ramadan. The preferred opinion etc.: i.e., difference occurred among them regarding the most correct of the sayings, some preferred one saying and others another saying, as we explained earlier. Of all types: like the expiation of the oath, the fast of tamattu' and qiran. [Maraqi al-Falah: 645]
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah discusses the conditions of intention (niyyah) for fasting, particularly what fasts require specification (ta'yin) and overnight (tabyit) intention. The passage is a ruling statement with some internal ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school. The author first explains that the time for intention extends from dawn until half the day, i.e., the time of al-duha al-kubra. He then states that Ramadan, specified vows (al-nadhr al-mu'ayyan), and supererogatory fasts (nafl) are valid with an absolute intention (mutlaq al-niyyah), meaning without specifying whether it is fard or nafl. Even if one intends a different obligatory fast while fasting Ramadan—provided one is healthy and resident—it still counts for Ramadan. However, a traveller's fast in Ramadan is only valid if he intends that specific Ramadan fast; if he intends another wajib fast, it falls as that other wajib. The author notes an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sick person: three opinions exist. The first (chosen by Fakhr al-Islam and Shams al-A'imma) says it falls for Ramadan. The second (chosen by the author of al-Hidayah and many mashayikh) says it falls for what he intended. The third details: if fasting harms him, he is like a traveller and it falls for what he intended; if it does not harm him, it falls for the duty of the time (Ramadan). The second category of fasts—require both specification and overnight intention—are: make-up of Ramadan, make-up of a broken nafl fast, expiatory fasts (kaffarat) of all types, and absolute vows (al-nadhr al-mutlaq) where no time is specified. The passage also clarifies that the time for intention is before half the day (i.e., before al-duha al-kubra). The commentary from later sources (al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and Maraqi al-Falah) is interwoven to explain the three opinions and the details of absolute intention. The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah but includes later tarjih. The practical outcome: a healthy resident fasting Ramadan must intend it, but if he intends another wajib by mistake, it still counts for Ramadan. A traveller must intend Ramadan specifically, otherwise his fast counts for what he intended.

Key Terms
تبييت النية (tabyit al-niyyah): Making the intention at night (before dawn) for the fast of the following day.
مطلق النية (mutlaq al-niyyah): An intention that does not specify the type of fast (e.g., not stating whether it is fard or nafl).
المنذور المعين زمانه (al-nadhr al-mu'ayyan zamanuhu): A vow specific to a particular day or time.
المنذور المطلق (al-nadhr al-mutlaq): A vow that is not fixed to a specific day (e.g., 'I vow to fast a day').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following fasts is valid with an absolute intention (mutlaq al-niyyah) before half the day according to the Hanafi school?
A) Make-up (qada) of Ramadan
B) Expiation (kaffara) for an oath
C) A specified vow (al-nadhr al-mu'ayyan)
D) An absolute vow (al-nadhr al-mutlaq)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school splits fasts into two categories: those valid with absolute intention before noon (Ramadan, specified vow, nafl) and those requiring both specification and overnight intention (qada, kaffara, absolute vow).

The Obligation of Seeking the Crescent and the Mufti's Practice on Yawm al-Shakk
Statements on crescent seeking and mufti's Day of Doubt practice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for seeking the crescent and explain the mufti's instruction on the Day of Doubt.

Core Idea
Seeking the crescent is a communal obligation; on the Day of Doubt the public waits and breaks while the mufti fasts secretly.

Knowing when Ramadan begins is a personal duty, but the community must assist by sighting the crescent. The Hanafi school teaches that this search is a collective obligation. On the Day of Doubt, the mufti balances caution and practicality, ordering the public to delay then break, while he himself fasts in secret to avoid any risk.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Locate the act being ruled: seeking the crescent (talab al-hilal) on the night of the 30th of Sha'ban and on the sunset of the 29th of Ramadan. Determine its hukm level from the text: fard kifayah (communal obligation).
Fard kifayah means if some members fulfill it, the rest are absolved.
2
Understand the reason for the ruling
The text says 'because it is a means to the obligation.' Identify that the personal obligation is fasting Ramadan. Seeking the crescent is a necessary preparatory act, hence it takes the same obligation level communally.
This is a classic Hanafi principle: wasa'il (means) follow the ruling of the maqsud (end).
3
Distinguish the two contexts
Note that the obligation applies to two occasions: (1) night of 30th Sha'ban for starting Ramadan, (2) sunset of 29th Ramadan for Shawwal. Both are fard kifayah.
4
Analyze the mufti's instruction to the public
On the Day of Doubt (yawm al-shakk), if the crescent was not sighted, the mufti orders the general public to wait (talammuth) until the time of intention passes (mid-morning). If still unclear, they must break their fast.
Waiting prevents them from fasting without certainty, which would be invalid if it turns out not to be Ramadan.
5
Contrast with the mufti's own practice
The mufti and qadi themselves fast on that day, but with a nafl intention and secretly. This is precautionary: if the day was actually Ramadan, their fast counts; if not, it counts as nafl. They do not publicize it to avoid confusion.
This practice is based on a narration about Abu Yusuf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يثبت به الهلال: اعلم أنه يفترض كفاية التماس الهلال ليلة الثلاثين من شعبان؛ لأنه يتوصل به إلى الفرض، وكذا التماس هلال شوال في غروب التاسع والعشرين من رمضان. ويأمر المفتي العامة بالتلوم يوم الشك، ثم بالإفطار إذا ذهب وقت النية، ولم يتبين الحال ويصوم فيه المفتي والقاضي، على نية النفل
It is established by the crescent: Know that it is a communal obligation (fard kifayah) to seek the crescent on the night of the thirtieth of Sha'ban, because it is a means to the obligation. Likewise, seeking the crescent of Shawwal at the sunset of the twenty-ninth of Ramadan. And the mufti orders the general public to wait (talammuth) on the Day of Doubt, then to break the fast if the time of intention passes and the situation has not become clear. And the mufti and the qadi fast on it with the intention of nafl.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage explains two related matters: the obligation of seeking the crescent (talab al-hilal) and the practical instruction for the Day of Doubt. First, seeking the crescent on the night of the 30th of Sha'ban (i.e., the night before the Day of Doubt) is a fard kifayah (communal obligation) because it enables the community to determine the start of Ramadan, which is a personal obligation. Similarly, seeking the crescent of Shawwal at sunset on the 29th of Ramadan is also a fard kifayah. Second, on the Day of Doubt itself, if the crescent was not sighted the previous night, the mufti (the jurist who issues fatwas) instructs the general public to 'wait' (talammuth) — that is, to postpone fasting until the time of intention (which is mid-morning, approximately the time of الضحوة الكبرى, when the sun is well up) passes. If by that time the situation has not become clear (i.e., no news arrives that the crescent was sighted elsewhere), then the public should break their fast and not fast that day. However, the mufti and the qadi themselves fast on that day secretly with the intention of nafl, as a precaution in case it is actually Ramadan. They do not publicize their fast to avoid confusion and so that the public does not accuse them of sin. This is based on a story narrated by Asad ibn Amr about Abu Yusuf al-Qadi, who fasted on Yawm al-Shakk while outwardly advising people to break, demonstrating the practice of the early Hanafi imams.

Key Terms
التماس الهلال (Talab al-Hilal): The act of searching for the crescent moon on the night of the 30th of Sha'ban or the 30th of Ramadan to determine the start of Ramadan or Shawwal.
التلوم (Talammuth): Waiting for the time of intention (approximately mid-morning) on the Day of Doubt without deciding to fast or not, before it becomes clear whether it is Ramadan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What should the mufti tell the general public on Yawm al-Shakk if the crescent was not sighted the previous night?
A) To fast with the intention of Ramadan
B) To wait until the time of intention passes and then break their fast
C) To fast secretly
D) To seek the crescent again

Key Takeaway
Seeking the crescent is fard kifayah; on the Day of Doubt the mufti orders the public to wait and break while he himself fasts secretly with a nafl intention.

Ruling for One Who Sees the Crescent Alone and the Judge Rejects His Testimony
Stating the ruling for a person who sees the crescent alone and the judge rejects his testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a person whose personal crescent sighting is rejected by the judge and distinguish the consequences (qada vs kaffarah) in Ramadan and Shawwal.

Core Idea
Personal certainty does not override public ruling; breaking still requires qada but no kaffarah.

Imagine you are perfectly certain you have seen the new moon, but the judge does not accept your testimony. How do you act? This lesson addresses that precise scenario: a reliable individual who sees the crescent of Ramadan or Shawwal alone, then reports it, but the judge rejects his testimony. We will explore the legal obligations and penalties according to the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that this ruling applies when a reliable person sees the crescent of Ramadan or Shawwal alone, reports it to the qadi, and the qadi rejects his testimony. The qadi's rejection does not change the person's personal certainty.
The person's uprightness (adalah) is assumed; if the qadi had accepted, the rulings differ.
2
State the ruling for Ramadan
The person must fast Ramadan because he is certain of its entry. The public not knowing does not remove his individual obligation.
3
State the ruling for Shawwal
He is not allowed to break his fast on the day of doubt (the 30th of Ramadan) even though he knows it is Shawwal. He must continue fasting until the public announcement or until the end of the month.
This preserves the public nature of the 'Id.
4
Determine consequences if he breaks
If he breaks his fast in either scenario (Ramadan or Shawwal), he must make up the day (qada), but no kaffarah is due. This is the correct Hanafi view because the obligation of that day is disputed (mukhtalaf fih).
If the qadi had accepted his testimony, kaffarah would apply for breaking a Ramadan fast without excuse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن رأى هلال رمضان أو الفطر وحده وردّ قوله لزمه الصيام، ولا يجوز له الفطر بتيقنه هلال شوال وإن أفطر في الوقتين قضى ولا كفارة عليه، ولو كان فطره قبل ما ردّه القاضي في الصحيح
And whoever sees the crescent of Ramadan or of breaking the fast (Shawwal) alone and his statement is rejected, fasting is incumbent upon him, and it is not permissible for him to break the fast despite his certainty of the Shawwal crescent. And if he breaks the fast in either time, he makes it up (qada) and there is no kaffarah (expiation) upon him, even if his breaking was before the judge rejected his testimony, according to the correct view.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage addresses a person who is reliable and sees the crescent moon of Ramadan or of Shawwal all by himself—that is, no one else saw it with him. If he reports this to the qadi (judge) but the qadi rejects his testimony (perhaps because he is not considered a competent witness or the conditions for acceptance are not met), the ruling is as follows: For Ramadan: He must fast because he is certain that Ramadan has begun, even though the community does not know. For Shawwal: He is not allowed to break his fast (i.e., he must continue fasting) despite his personal certainty that Shawwal has begun, because the 'Id cannot be announced based on one person's testimony unless the judge accepts it. If he breaks his fast in either case—meaning if he breaks his Ramadan fast early based on his own sighting of Shawwal, or if he breaks his Shawwal fast by not fasting the next day—he must make up that day (qada), but he is not required to pay kaffarah (expiation, which is the severe penalty for deliberately breaking a Ramadan fast without excuse). This is the correct position (sahih) even if he broke before the judge rejected his testimony, because his personal certainty does not lift the obligation of community adherence. The commentary clarifies that there is no kaffarah because the day is disputed (mukhtalaf fih) regarding its obligation. If he were upright (adl) and the judge accepted his testimony, then if he broke, kaffarah would be due. This ruling applies to both scenarios: seeing Ramadan crescent alone and later breaking, or seeing Shawwal crescent alone and breaking.

Key Terms
الكفارة (Kaffarah): A severe expiation for deliberately breaking a Ramadan fast without a valid excuse; it involves freeing a slave, or fasting two consecutive months, or feeding sixty poor people.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone sees the Shawwal crescent alone and the qadi rejects his testimony, what is the ruling on him breaking his fast?
A) He must pay kaffarah
B) He must only make up (qada) the day
C) He may break without any penalty
D) He must continue fasting and cannot break at all

Key Takeaway
Personal certainty of a moon sighting does not override the judge's rejection; breaking the fast still requires qada but no kaffarah because the day is disputed.

Acceptance of Testimony for Moon Sighting in Ramadan
Stating conditions for accepting witness testimony for Ramadan and Shawwal crescent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for accepting witness testimony for Ramadan crescent sighting and identify when a mastur witness is acceptable.

Core Idea
When the sky is clear, only an upright (adl) witness is accepted; when cloudy, even a mastur witness is accepted in the correct Hanafi view.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rules for establishing the start of Ramadan through witness testimony. The text from Nur al-Idah specifies different conditions depending on whether the sky is clear or cloudy, and also introduces the concept of secondary testimony (shahada 'ala shahada). We will explore the preferred view (sahih) and the intra-madhhab difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its conditions
The act is testifying for crescent sighting. Two scenarios: clear sky vs. cloudy/dusty sky. Determine the witness requirement for each.
2
Distinguish adl vs. mastur
An adl witness is known to be upright; a mastur witness has unknown character. Note the difference and its legal effect.
3
Recognize the internal disagreement
Zahir al-riwayah requires adl in all cases; the correct view (sahih) accepts mastur when there is an impediment. Note which view is mufta bihi.
4
Apply to secondary testimony
Shahada 'ala shahada is accepted for crescent sighting: one witness can testify on another's testimony. Understand the exceptional nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان بالسماء علة من غيم أو غبار ونحوه قبل خبر واحد عدل أو مستور في الصحيح، ولو شهد على شهادة واحد مثله
And if there is in the sky an impediment like clouds or dust or the like, then the report of one upright (adl) person or even one whose condition is unknown (mastur) is accepted according to the correct view. And if he testifies upon the testimony of one person like him, it is also accepted.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage deals with the conditions for accepting a witness's testimony to establish the visibility of the crescent moon for Ramadan. The general rule in Hanafi fiqh is that for moon sighting, the testimony of one upright (adl) Muslim is sufficient if the sky is clear. However, when the sky is cloudy or dusty (an impediment like ghaym or ghubar), the jurists differ: the correct view (sahih) is that even a person whose character is unknown (mastur) is accepted for Ramadan. 'Mastur' means one whose condition is not known—neither proven to be fasiq nor upright. However, there is a counter-opinion (zahir al-riwayah) that only an upright person is accepted. The text clarifies that in the correct view, the mastur is accepted. Furthermore, for Ramadan, the testimony upon testimony (shahada 'ala shahada) is accepted: meaning one person can testify on the basis of another person's testimony that he saw the crescent, and that suffices. This is an exception from other legal matters where usually two witnesses (or one man and two women) are required to testify upon testimony. The reason is the need for ease in establishing the start of Ramadan.

Key Terms
مستور (Mastur): A person whose inner state of religiosity is unknown; not known to be fasiq (sinful) nor proven to be adl (upright).
شهادة على شهادة (Shahada 'ala Shahada): Testimony based on the testimony of another witness, where the second witness attests that the first witness heard or saw something.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When the sky is cloudy, who is accepted as a witness for the Ramadan crescent in the correct Hanafi view?
A) Only a just (adl) witness
B) Only two witnesses
C) One witness, even if his character is unknown (mastur)
D) No one can testify in cloudy weather

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school shows leniency for Ramadan crescent testimony: accept one witness, and in cloudy weather even a person of unknown character suffices.
