The Solitary Crescent Sight: Personal Obligation vs Judicial Rejection
Ruling on solitary crescent sighting and its consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a solitary crescent sighting and distinguish between the obligations for Ramadan and Shawwal, and explain the consequences (qada' vs kaffara) if the person violates the ruling.

Core Idea
Personal certainty of the crescent obligates fasting or abstaining, even if the judge rejects the testimony.

This passage from Nur al-Idah addresses a nuanced situation where an individual's personal conviction about the crescent moon conflicts with the legal process of testimony. The Hanafi school clarifies that personal knowledge carries its own obligation, separate from judicial acceptance. Understanding this ruling is crucial for distinguishing between individual duty and communal evidence in matters of the lunar calendar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Recognize that the passage describes a person who sees the crescent of Ramadan or Shawwal alone (without corroborating witnesses) and whose testimony is rejected by the judge.
The rejection is due to the judge's rules of evidence, not a doubt about the person's honesty.
2
State the Obligation for Ramadan
If the person is certain that Ramadan has started, he must fast, even if the judge rejects his testimony. The personal certainty creates a binding obligation.
This is because certainty overrides judicial non-acceptance.
3
State the Obligation for Shawwal
If the person is certain that Shawwal has started, he is not permitted to break his fast (i.e., he must continue fasting) because the public ruling is that Ramadan is still ongoing.
He cannot act on his certainty publicly; he must wait for the judge's decision.
4
Determine Consequences of Violation
If he breaks the fast in either case (e.g., eats on a day he is certain is Ramadan or breaks his fast on a day he is certain is Shawwal after the judge's rejection), he must make up that day (qada') but is not liable for expiation (kaffara).
The text explicitly says 'no kaffara upon him'.
5
Note the Intra-Madhhab Detail
Even if the person breaks the fast before the judge formally rejects his testimony, the sound opinion (mufta bihi) is still that no kaffara is required.
This settles a debate among early Hanafi scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من رأى هلال رمضان أو الفطر وحده وردّ قوله مبتدأ أي وحده لم يقبل القاضي قوله لزمه الصيام، ولا يجوز له الفطر بتيقنه هلال شوال وإن أفطر في الوقتين قضى خبر ولا كفارة عليه، ولو كان فطره قبل ما ردّه القاضي في الصحيح
Whoever sees the crescent of Ramadan or Eid al-Fitr alone and his statement is rejected (beginning, i.e., alone) the judge did not accept his statement: fasting is incumbent upon him, and it is not permissible for him to break the fast with certainty of the crescent of Shawwal, and if he breaks in both times, he makes up (fast) and no expiation is upon him, even if his breaking was before the judge rejected it according to the sound opinion.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school concerning an individual who personally sights the crescent of Ramadan or Shawwal, but whose testimony is rejected by the judge (e.g., due to lack of additional witnesses or because he is not deemed upright). Despite the judge's rejection, the individual is bound by his own certainty: for Ramadan, he must fast; for Shawwal, he cannot break the fast. If he violates this and breaks the fast in either case, he must make up the day (qada') but is not liable for expiation (kaffara). The commentary notes that this applies even if he breaks the fast before the judge formally rejects his testimony, according to the sound position. The interlinear words clarify that 'alone' modifies the seer, and that the judge does not accept the statement. The word 'khabar' (خبر) is possibly a marginal note indicating that the ruling is based on report.

Key Terms
هلال (hilal): The new crescent moon that marks the beginning of a lunar month (Ramadan or Shawwal).
وحده (wahdahu): Alone, i.e., the person sees the crescent without any other witness corroborating.
ردّ قوله (radd qawlihi): Rejection of his statement by the judge, meaning his testimony is not accepted as legal proof.
قضاء (qada'): Making up a missed obligatory fast on another day.
كفارة (kaffara): Expiation, a specific penalty for breaking a fast without valid excuse; in this context, not required.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if an individual sees the crescent of Shawwal alone and the judge rejects his testimony, but the person breaks the fast out of certainty, what is required?
A) He must make up the fast (qada') and also pay kaffara.
B) He must make up the fast (qada') but no kaffara is required.
C) No make-up is required because he was certain.
D) He is not required to do anything because the judge rejected his testimony.

Key Takeaway
Personal certainty of the crescent creates a binding individual obligation, even when the judge rejects the testimony; violation requires qada' but not kaffara.

Acceptance of a Single Witness for the Crescent of Ramadan
Conditions for establishing crescent via witness when sky is cloudy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a single witness is accepted for Ramadan crescent, distinguish between 'adl and mustawir, and apply the special rule of indirect testimony for Ramadan.

Core Idea
A single trustworthy witness suffices for Ramadan crescent when sky is cloudy; even a person of unknown character is accepted in the sound opinion.

When the sky is overcast, proving the crescent of Ramadan becomes difficult. Hanafi fiqh relaxes the usual strict testimony requirements. This lesson explores the conditions for accepting a single witness, the definition of uprightness ('adl) and unknown status (mustawir), and the exceptional rule that indirect testimony is accepted with a single witness on each level.

Guided Path
1
Identify witness types
Note the two categories: 'adl (upright) and mustawir (unknown). Understand their definitions from the text.
'Adl: good deeds outweigh bad, possesses piety and dignity. Mustawir: no apparent good or bad.
2
Recognize the key condition
The leniency applies only when there is an obstruction in the sky: clouds, dust, or similar.
Clear sky requires the usual two witnesses.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute
The sound opinion (al-sahih) accepts mustawir; Zahir al-Riwayah rejects him. Note which is mufta bihi.
Later works prefer acceptance for facilitation.
4
Learn the indirect testimony exception
For Ramadan, a single witness can testify based on another single witness's testimony, unlike other cases needing two per level.
Applies only to crescent sighting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان بالسماء علة من غيم أو غبار ونحوه قبل خبر واحد عدل أو مستور في الصحيح، ولو شهد على شهادة واحد مثله، خبر واحد عدل وهو الذي حسناته أكثر من سيئاته والعدالة: ملكة تحمل على ملازمة التقوى والمروة. مستور هو مجهول الحال لم يظهر له فسق ولا عدالة. في الصحيح: مقابله ظاهر الرواية أنه لا يقبل قول المستور. ولو شهد إلخ: أي يقبل في هلال رمضان شهادة واحد عدل على شهادة واحد عدل بخلاف الشهادة على الشهادة في سائر الأحكام حيث لا تقبل ما لم يشهد على شهادة رجل واحد رجلان أو رجل وامرأتان
And if there is an obstruction in the sky, such as clouds, dust, or the like, the report of a single upright witness or a mustawir is accepted according to the sound opinion. And if he testifies upon the testimony of one like him: a single upright witness is he whose good deeds outweigh his bad deeds, and uprightness is a quality that leads to persistent piety and noble character. A mustawir is one whose condition is unknown: no immorality nor uprightness has become apparent from him. According to the sound opinion: its opposite is the apparent narration that the statement of the mustawir is not accepted. 'And if he testifies, etc.' means: for the crescent of Ramadan, the testimony of a single upright witness upon the testimony of a single upright witness is accepted, unlike testimony upon testimony in other rulings, where it is not accepted unless two men or a man and two women testify upon the testimony of a single man.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage specifies the conditions for proving the crescent of Ramadan when the sky is cloudy (or dusty, etc.). In such cases, a single trustworthy witness (adl) is accepted. Even a mustawir (a person of unknown moral standing) is accepted according to the sound position, though the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah says otherwise. The commentary defines 'adl' as one whose good deeds outweigh bad, possessing a quality that ensures piety and dignity. A mustawir is one whose state is hidden; the sound opinion accepts his report for the crescent. Additionally, when a witness testifies on the basis of another's testimony (shahāda 'alā shahāda), it is accepted for Ramadan with a single witness on each level, unlike other legal matters where two witnesses or a man and two women are required for each level. This leniency is because establishing the crescent is a matter of worship that requires facilitation.

Key Terms
عدل (adl): A witness whose uprightness is established; good deeds outweigh bad, characterized by piety (taqwa) and dignity (muru'a).
مستور (mustawir): A person of unknown moral standing; neither uprightness nor immorality is apparent.
شهادة على شهادة (shahada 'ala shahada): Testimony based on another witness's testimony (indirect testimony).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the sound opinion in the Hanafi school, what type of witness is accepted for establishing the crescent of Ramadan when the sky is cloudy?
A) Only a witness who is 'adl (upright).
B) Only a witness who is mustawir (unknown).
C) Both an 'adl and a mustawir.
D) Only two male witnesses.

Key Takeaway
When sky is cloudy, one upright or even a person of unknown character is accepted for Ramadan crescent; indirect testimony also relaxed to a single witness per level.

Conditions for Proving Shawwal and the Clear Sky Rule
Testimony requirements for Shawwal and large crowd when sky clear

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the testimony requirements for Shawwal under cloudy and clear skies and apply the rule that completing 30 days of Ramadan does not permit breaking when the sky is clear.

Core Idea
When the sky is clear on the 30th night, fasting continues until the crescent is sighted — mere completion of 30 days does not permit breaking.

In determining the start of Shawwal, the Hanafi school distinguishes between cloudy and clear skies. This lesson unpacks the conditions for accepting testimony and the special rule that on a clear night, even after 30 days, one must continue fasting until the moon is sighted. The source passage from Nur al-Idah lays out these rulings with precision.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the rulings covered
The passage covers two main issues: (a) who is acceptable to testify for crescent sighting for Ramadan vs Shawwal, and (b) the obligation to break fast on the 30th night when the sky is clear or cloudy.
Focus on the distinction between Ramadan and Shawwal conditions.
2
2. Distinguish between cloudy and clear sky conditions
For Shawwal, if the sky is cloudy (بعلة), the requirement is lafz al-shahada from two free men or one free man and two free women. If clear, a large crowd (جمع عظيم) is required for both Ramadan and Shawwal.
The large crowd gives certain knowledge. No fixed number; delegated to imam.
3
3. Apply the completion-of-30 rule
When 30 days of Ramadan are completed (by any valid testimony), but the crescent is not seen on a clear night, it is not permissible to break the fast. This is because the month cannot be confirmed to have ended by sighting.
The phrase 'لا يحل له الفطر' indicates prohibition.
4
4. Understand the exception for cloudy sky
If the sky is cloudy, all scholars agree that after 30 days one may break the fast, even if the crescent was not seen. This is because the obstruction prevents certainty.
The text says 'لا خلاف في حل الفطر إذا كان بالسماء علة'.
5
5. Note the ikhtilaf about two just witnesses
When two just witnesses testify to sighting the crescent on a clear night, there is a difference in preponderance (ترجيح) among Hanafi scholars. The text states there is ikhtilaf but does not resolve it here.
This ikhtilaf is captured in the madhhab dispute map.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان أنثى أو رقيقا أو محدودا في قذف تاب لرمضان، ولا يشترط لفظ الشهادة ولا الدعوى، وشرط لهلال الفطر إذا كان بالسماء علة لفظ الشهادة من حرين أو حر وحرتين بلا دعوى، وإن لم يكن بالسماء علة فلابد من جمع عظيم لرمضان والفطر، ومقدار الجمع العظيم مفوض لرأي الإمام في الأصح، وإذا تم العدد بشهادة فرد ولم ير هلال الفطر والسماء مصحيّة لا يحل له الفطر، واختلف الترجيح فيما إذا كان بشهادة عدلين ولا خلاف في حل الفطر إذا كان بالسماء علة ولو ثبت رمضان بشهادة الفرد
And if it is a woman, slave, or one who was flogged for slander and repented, it is not accepted for Ramadan. And the wording of testimony and a lawsuit are not required. And a condition for the crescent of Shawwal, if there is an obstruction in the sky, is the wording of testimony from two free men or a free man and two free women, without a lawsuit. And if there is no obstruction in the sky, then a large gathering is necessary for Ramadan and Shawwal. The measure of the large gathering is delegated to the opinion of the imam in the most sound view. And if the number (of Ramadan) is completed by the testimony of an individual and the crescent of Shawwal is not seen while the sky is clear, it is not permissible for him to break the fast. And there is a difference in preponderance regarding if it is by the testimony of two upright witnesses; but there is no difference in the permissibility of breaking the fast if there is an obstruction in the sky, even if Ramadan is established by the testimony of an individual.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage covers several conditions for establishing the crescent. First, for Ramadan, the testimony of women, slaves, or those who were flogged for false accusation of adultery (qadhf) and repented is not accepted (though repentance may restore credibility elsewhere). Second, a formal statement of testimony (lafz al-shahada) and a lawsuit (da'wa) are not required for Ramadan; knowledge of certainty suffices. For Shawwal when the sky is cloudy, formal testimony is required, from two free men or one free man and two free women, without a lawsuit. When the sky is clear, a large crowd (jam' 'azim) is required for both Ramadan and Shawwal; its size is left to the imam's discretion in the sound view. Lastly, if Ramadan reaches 30 days (based on a single witness or earlier sighting) and the crescent of Shawwal is not seen on a clear night, fasting remains obligatory; breaking is not allowed. However, if the sky is cloudy, all are agreed that breaking is permitted after 30 days. When two just witnesses testify to sighting, but sky is clear, there is difference; the sound view permits breaking.

Key Terms
جمع عظيم (jamm' 'azim): A large gathering of people whose report brings overwhelming knowledge (ghalabat al-zann).
عدد (adad): The number of days of Ramadan; when completed to 30, the month is complete.
لفظ الشهادة (lafz al-shahada): Uttering the specific words of testimony, e.g., 'I witness that I saw the crescent'.
دعوى (da'wa): A formal legal claim or lawsuit; not required for establishing the crescent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When the sky is clear on the 30th day of Ramadan and the crescent of Shawwal is not seen, what is the ruling according to the text?
A) It is permissible to break the fast because the month is complete.
B) It is not permissible to break the fast until the crescent is sighted.
C) It is obligatory to break the fast because 30 days have passed.
D) It is recommended to break but not obligatory.

Key Takeaway
The conditions for proving the crescent differ based on sky condition; on a clear night, a large crowd is required for both Ramadan and Shawwal, and completing 30 days does not permit breaking the fast without sighting.

Conditions for Establishing the New Moon and the Daytime Sighting Dispute
Explaining conditions for witnessing hilal and intra-madhhab ikhtilaf on daytime sighting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the testimony requirements for Ramadan and other months, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a daytime sighting belongs to the past or coming night, along with the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
A crescent seen during the day refers to the coming night, not the past — this is the relied-upon view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Determining the start of Ramadan or Shawwal hinges on reliable sighting of the crescent moon. Hanafi fiqh sets specific testimony conditions and addresses edge cases such as sighting during the day. This passage from Nur al-Idah presents the rules and an intra-madhhab dispute on whether a daytime sighting shifts the month boundary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the testimony requirements
Note the conditions for establishing the crescent for Ramadan and Dhul-Hijjah (like Shawwal): in cloudy weather, two just men or one man and two women are needed. For other months, two just men are always required. In clear weather, a large crowd suffices.
The matn explicitly mentions that the crescent of al-Adha (Dhul-Hijjah) is like al-Fitr, requiring increased testimony under clouds.
2
Understand the horizon-binding issue
If the crescent is sighted in one region, does it bind all people? The apparent madhhab says yes, but most mashayikh hold that each region is addressed by its own sighting. The commentary considers the latter more appropriate.
The story of Abu Musa al-Darir illustrates that even if one sees the sun after local sunset, he may not break his fast because his obligation is tied to his own horizon.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
The core dispute: if the crescent is seen during the day (especially before noon on the 30th), does it belong to the past night (making that day the first of the month) or the coming night?
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say it belongs to the coming night. Abu Yusuf distinguishes: before noon, it belongs to the past night; after noon, to the coming night.
4
Apply the mufta bihi
The preferred view (al-mukhtar) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: a daytime sighting is always for the coming night, so the day of sighting is still part of the previous month.
This ruling is confirmed by al-Hidayah and is the basis for fatwa. The thamarah al-khilaf: in Abu Yusuf's view, if sighted before noon, that day becomes the first of Ramadan or Shawwal, requiring immediate fasting or breaking fast.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يثبت به الهلال كتاب الصوم وهلال الأضحى كالفطر، ويشترط لبقية الأهلة شهادة رجلين عدلين، أو حر وحرتين غير محدودين في قذف، وإذا ثبت في مطلع قطر لزم سائر الناس في ظاهر المذهب، وعليه الفتوى وأكثر المشايخ، ولا عبرة برؤية الهلال نهارا، سواء كان قبل الزوال أو بعده، وهو الليلة المستقبلة في المختار. وهلال الأضحى إلخ أي هلال ذي الحجة كهلال شوال، فلا يثبت بالغيم إلا برجلين أو رجل وامرأتين، وأما حالة الصحو فالكل سواء، لابد من زيادة العدد البحر الرائق: ٤٢٤/٢] ويشترط لبقية إلخ: أطلقه وهو مقيد بما إذا كان بالسماء علة، وأما إذا لم تكن فجمع عظيم. وإذا ثبت إلخ معناه: إذا رأى الهلال أهل بلد و لم يره أهل بلدة أخرى، يجب أن يصوموا برؤية أولئك كيفما كان على قول من قال : لا عبرة باختلاف المطالع وعلى قول من اعتبره ينظر فإن كان بينهما تقارب بحيث لا تختلف المطالع يجب، وإن كان بحيث تختلف لا يجب، وأكثر المشايخ على أنه لا يعتبر حتى إذا صام أهل بلدة ثلاثين يوماً وأهل بلدة أخرى تسعة وعشرين يوماً، يجب عليهم قضاء يوم، والأشبه أن يعتبر؛ لأن كل قوم مخاطبون بما عندهم، وانفصال الهلال عن شعاع الشمس يختلف باختلاف الأقطار، كما أن دخول الوقت وخروجه يختلف باختلاف الأقطار، حتى إذا زالت الشمس في المشرق لا يلزم منه أن تزول في المغرب، وكذا طلوع الفجر وغروب الشمس، بل كلما تحركت الشمس درجة، فتلك طلوع فجر لقوم، وطلوع شمس لآخرين، وغروب لبعض، ونصف ليل لغيرهم، وروي أن أبا موسی الضرير الفقيه صاحب "المختصر" قدم الإسكندرية، فسئل عمن صعد على منارة الإسكندرية، فيرى الشمس بزمان طويل بعدما غربت عندهم في البلد، أيحل له أن يفطر؟ فقال: لا، ويحل لأهل البلد؛ لأن كلاً مخاطب بما عنده تبيين الحقائق: ١٦٤/٢، ١٦٥] ولا عبرة برؤية إلخ معنى عدم اعتبارها أنه لا يثبت بها حكم من وجوب صوم أو فطر، فلذا قال في "الخانية": فلا يصام له ولا يفطر (رد المحتار في المختار أي الذي هو قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد رحمها، وقال في "البدائع": فلا يكون ذلك اليوم من رمضان عندهما، وقال أبو يوسف إن كان بعد الزوال فكذلك، وإن كان قبله فهو لليلة الماضية، ويكون اليوم من رمضان وعلى هذا الخلاف هلال شوال، فعندهما يكون للمستقبلة مطلقاً، ويكون اليوم من رمضان وعنده لو قبل الزوال يكون للماضية، ويكون اليوم يوم الفطر. والحاصل: إذا رؤي الهلال الجمعة مثلاً قبل الزوال، فعند أبي يوسف هو لليلة الماضية بمعنى أ أنه أن الهلال قد يعتبر الأفق ليلة الجمعة، فغاب ثم ظهر نهاراً، فظهوره في النهار في حكم ظهوره في ليلة ثانية من ابتداء الشهر، وإذا كان لليلة الماضية يكون يوم الجمعة المذكور أول الشهر، فيجب صومه إن كان رمضان ، ويجب فطره إن كان شوالاً، - يوم وجد وإنما كان الخلاف في رؤية يوم - وهو يوم الثلاثين - ؛ لأن رؤية يوم التاسع والعشرين لم يقل أحد فيها إنه للماضية؛ لئلا يلزم أن يكون الشهر عليه بعض المحققين. [رد المحتار ٣٩٢/٢]
What the crescent moon is established by: The book of fasting. The crescent of al-Adha is like that of al-Fitr. For the remaining months, testimony of two just men is required, or one free man and two free women who have not been flogged for false accusation. If established in the horizon of a region, it is binding on all people according to the apparent madhhab, and fatwa is upon it according to most mashayikh. Sighting during the day is not considered, whether before or after noon; it belongs to the coming night in the preferred view. His statement 'and the crescent of al-Adha etc.' means the crescent of Dhul-Hijjah is like the crescent of Shawwal: it is not established under clouds except by two men or one man and two women. In clear weather, all are equal; a large number is necessary. 'And for the remaining months etc.' His statement is unrestricted but conditioned when there is an obstruction in the sky; if not, then a great crowd. 'And when established etc.' means: if the people of one town see the crescent and another town does not, they must fast by their sight according to the one who says there is no consideration of differing horizons. According to the one who considers it, it is examined: if they are close such that horizons do not differ, it is obligatory; if they differ, it is not. Most mashayikh hold that it is not considered, but the more appropriate is to consider it. The separation of the crescent from the sun's light differs by regions. A story about Abu Musa al-Darir: he was asked about one who climbs a lighthouse and sees the sun long after it set in the city. He said: no (he cannot break his fast), but the city folk may, because each is addressed by what they have. 'And no consideration of sighting...' The meaning of non-consideration: no ruling of obligation of fasting or breaking fast is established by it. In al-Khaniyyah: one does not fast or break fast because of it. According to the preferred view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. In al-Bada’i: that day is not from Ramadan according to them. Abu Yusuf said: if after noon, it is the same; if before noon, it is for the past night, and that day is from Ramadan. The same ikhtilaf applies to Shawwal. The gist: if the crescent is seen on Friday before noon, according to Abu Yusuf it is for the past night: the crescent on Friday night appeared and then set, then appeared during the day. So its appearance during the day is like an appearance on the second night of the month. Then Friday is the first day of the month; fasting is obligatory if it is Ramadan, and breaking fast is obligatory if it is Shawwal. The dispute is only about seeing it on the day of doubt (the 30th). No one says it is for the past if seen on the 29th to avoid the month being 28 days.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah with its commentary begins with the conditions for establishing the new moon for Ramadan and Dhul-Hijjah. The matn states that for Ramadan and Dhul-Hijjah, testimony of one just man is sufficient in cloudy weather due to necessity? No, the matn says for other months two witnesses are needed. The commentary explains that for Dhul-Hijjah it is like Shawwal—requires two men or one man and two women in cloudy weather, but in clear weather a large number is needed. Then the issue of differing horizons: if one city sights the moon, are others obligated? The preferred view according to most mashayikh is that they are not bound, but the more appropriate is that they are, because each region is addressed by its own sighting. This is supported by a story of Abu Musa al-Darir in Alexandria. Then the ruling on daytime sighting: it is not considered for the past night in the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; it belongs to the coming night. Abu Yusuf differentiates: if sighted before noon, it belongs to the past night. The ikhtilaf is restricted to the day of doubt (30th). This is a classic intra-Hanafi dispute with practical consequences.

Key Terms
الهلال (al-hilal): The new crescent moon, especially for determining the start of Islamic months.
مطلع (matla'): The place of rising (horizon) of the moon; used in the context of differing horizons (ikhtilaf al-matali').
قطر (qutr): A region or country; the ruling of sighting in one qutr is binding on all people according to the apparent madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view (al-mukhtar) in Nur al-Idah, if the crescent is seen before noon on the 30th of Shaban, what does it indicate?
A) It indicates the past night was the first of Ramadan, so one must fast that day.
B) It indicates the upcoming night will be the first of Ramadan, so fasting begins the next morning.
C) It indicates that the month is 30 days and no change is needed.
D) It indicates that one should wait until the next sighting to be sure.

Key Takeaway
A crescent seen during the day is considered for the coming night in the mufta bihi view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, so it does not retroactively make that day the first of the month.

Acts That Do Not Invalidate the Fast: The Principle of Forgetfulness
Opening the chapter listing things that do not invalidate the fast with commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating, drinking, or having intercourse forgetfully while fasting, and distinguish it from the ruling on the mistaken person.

Core Idea
Forgetfulness does not break the fast—but only if you stop immediately upon remembering.

This passage from Nur al-Idah opens the chapter on acts that do not break the fast. The central principle is that forgetfulness (nisyan) is a valid excuse; the fast remains intact. However, the text carefully distinguishes between the forgetful person (nasi) and the mistaken person (mukhti) who remembers fasting but accidentally does something that invalidates. Understanding this distinction is key to applying the ruling correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The matn lists eating, drinking, or intercourse forgetfully. The ruling is that these acts do not invalidate the fast.
2
Understand the condition of forgetfulness
The commentary specifies 'nasiyan' to exclude the mistaken person (mukhti). The mistaken person remembers the fast but does not intend to break it, yet his action invalidates because he should have controlled it.
For example, accidentally swallowing water while rinsing the mouth.
3
Apply the sub-rule on remembering
If during the forgetful act you remember, you must stop immediately. If you continue intentionally, the fast breaks.
The text elaborates: if one remembers during intercourse and withdraws immediately, the fast is valid.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on kaffara
There is a difference of opinion: some say no kaffara if you did not move after remembering, others say kaffara if you moved. The mufta bihi leans towards kaffara if movement occurred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب مالا يفسد الصوم وهو أربعة وعشرون شيئاً ما لو أكل أو شرب أو جامع ناسياً، وإن كان للناسي قدرة على الصوم يُذَكِّره به من رآه يأكل، وكره عدم تذكيره، وإن لم يكن له قوة فاعل يذكر فالأولى عدم تذكيره، أو أنزل بنظر أو فكر وإن أدام النظر والفكر، أو ادهن أو اكتحل ولو وجد طعمه في حلقه، أو احتجم، أو اغتاب، ما لا يفسد الصوم الفساد والبطلان في العبادة سيان. ناسياً: قيد بالناسي للاحتراز عن المخطيء، وهو الذاكر للصوم غير القاصد للفطر بأن لم يقصد الأكل ولا الشرب، بل قصد المضمضة أو اختبار طعم المأكول، فسبق شيء منه إلى جوفه، أو باشر مباشرة فاحشة فتوارت حشفته، فإنه يفسد، والمكره والنائم كالمخطيء. ولو بدأ بالجماع ناسياً فتذكر إن نزع من ساعته لم يفطر، وإن دام على حتى أنزل فعليه القضاء، ثم قيل: لا كفارة عليه، وقيل: هذا إذا لم يحرك نفسه بعد التذكر حتى أنزل، فإن حرك نفسه بعده، فعليه الكفارة كما لو نزع ثم أدخل. أدام النظر : ماض من الإدامة معناه: أطال النظر إليها. أطلق في النظر فشمل ما إذا نظر إلى وجهها أو فرجها، وقيد به؛ لأنه لو قبلها بشهوة فأنزل فسد صومه؛ لوجود معنى الجماع، بخلاف ما إذا لم ينزل حيث لا يفسد؛ لعدم المنافي صورة ومعنى. أو اكتحل أفاد أنه لا يكره للصائم شم رائحة المسك والورد ونحوه مما لا يكون جوهراً متصلاً كالدخان فإنهم قالوا لا يكره الاكتحال بحال، وهو شامل للمطيب وغيره، و لم يخصوه بنوع منه. وجد طعمه إلخ وكذا لوبزق فوجد لونه في الأصح. أو اغتاب قال السيد في شرحه : الغيبة: أن تذكر أخاك بما يكره، قيل: أرأيت إن كان في أخي ما أقول؟ قال: إن كان فيه ما تقول فقد اغتبته، وإن لم يكن فيه ما تقول فقد بهته، والحاصل أن من تكلم خلف إنسان مستور بما يغمه لو سمعه إن كان صدقاً بهتاناً، وأما المتجاهر فلا غيبة له يسمى غيبة، وإن كان كذباً يسمى بهتاناً
Chapter on what does not invalidate fasting. It comprises twenty-four things: one who eats, drinks, or has intercourse forgetfully; even if the forgetful person has the strength to fast, the one who sees him eating should remind him, and it is disliked not to remind him. If he does not have strength, then it is better not to remind him. Or one who has a wet dream due to looking or thinking, even if he prolongs the look and thought; or applies oil; or applies kohl even if he tastes it in his throat; or cups; or backbites. 'What does not invalidate fasting': corruption and invalidity in worship are the same. 'Forgetfully': a condition to exclude the mistaken one, i.e., one who remembers fasting but does not intend to break it, such as intending to rinse his mouth or taste food, and something enters his stomach, or engages in obscene act and his glans disappears, then it invalidates. The coerced and the sleeping are like the mistaken. If one begins intercourse forgetfully and then remembers: if he withdraws immediately, he does not break fast; if he continues until ejaculation, he must make up. It is said there is no expiation; it is also said no expiation if he did not move after remembering, but if he moved, expiation is required. 'He prolongs the look': from idamah, meaning he looks at her continuously. It includes looking at her face or private parts; but if he kisses her with desire and ejaculates, his fast is broken due to the meaning of intercourse, contrary to if he does not ejaculate. 'Or applies kohl': indicates that it is not disliked for a fasting person to smell musk, rose, etc. that is not an attached substance like smoke; they said applying kohl in any case is not disliked, including perfumed kohl. 'He tastes it in his throat': similarly if he spits and sees its color, in the correct view. 'Or backbites': backbiting is mentioning your brother with what he dislikes. The hadith: 'If it is in him, you backbit; if not, you slandered.' The conclusion: one who speaks behind a chaste person with something that would grieve him—if truthful, it is backbiting; if false, it is slander. The openly sinful has no backbiting.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on acts that do not invalidate the fast. The matn lists twenty-four things but only lists a few here: eating/drinking/intercourse forgetfully, wet dream from looking/thinking, applying oil, applying kohl, cupping, backbiting. The commentary expands on the condition of forgetfulness: 'nasiyan' is specified to exclude the mistaken person who remembers fasting but does not intend to break it; such a person invalidates the fast. The rule about forgetting: if able, others should remind him; if weak, it is better not to. For intercourse forgetfully: if he remembers and withdraws immediately, no break; if he continues purposefully, it breaks and requires qada and possibly kaffara. Then commentary on 'prolonging the look': if it leads to ejaculation, it does not break fasting according to the texts because it is not direct contact, but kissing with desire that leads to ejaculation does break. Kohl and oil do not break fast even if taste reaches the throat in the correct view. Backbiting is considered a sin but does not invalidate the fast. The commentary defines backbiting and distinguishes it from slander. All these rulings are specific to the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
ناسياً (nasiyan): Forgetfully, i.e., one who forgets he is fasting and eats/drinks/has intercourse.
الغيبة (al-ghibah): Mentioning your brother in his absence with what he dislikes; it is a sin but does not break the fast.
إدامة النظر (idamat al-nazar): Prolonged looking, e.g., at one's spouse, which may cause ejaculation. Ruling: does not break fast if no ejaculation, but breaks if kissing leads to ejaculation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a fasting person forgets he is fasting and eats a meal, then remembers while still eating. What is the ruling?
A) The fast is broken due to the eating.
B) The fast remains valid if he stops immediately upon remembering.
C) The fast is broken only if he continues eating after remembering.
D) The fast is corrupted and requires qada.

Key Takeaway
Forgetfulness preserves the fast, but only if the act stops upon remembering; the mistaken person's act invalidates.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
8 min
Conditions for Expiation in Fasting
Conditions for expiation in fasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the eight conditions required for expiation (kaffarah) to be obligatory and apply them to distinguish cases where kaffarah applies from those where it does not.

Core Idea
Kaffarah is wajib only when all conditions are met: intent, volition, adulthood, Ramadan, and no subsequent excuse.

Expiation (kaffarah) is a severe penalty in fasting, but it is not triggered lightly. The Hanafi school enumerates several conditions that must all be present. This lesson unpacks each condition, showing how missing even one drops the obligation of kaffarah, though qada may still be required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
State that kaffarah is wajib for invalidating a Ramadan fast by specified actions, as per the opening of the passage.
The passage lists twenty-two invalidators.
2
List the conditions from the text
Extract the conditions: action by fasting person, mukallaf, niyyah from night, fast of Ramadan, no subsequent valid excuse, volition, intent, no compulsion.
Each condition is mentioned explicitly in the source.
3
Analyze each condition with examples
For each condition, identify the example given in the text, such as a child (not mukallaf), forgetfulness (no intent), or travel after breaking (excuse before invalidator).
Pay special attention to the niyyah dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference on niyyah
Abu Hanifa says if one intended before noon and then broke, no kaffarah; Sahibayn say kaffarah is due. The author follows Abu Hanifa.
This is a key thamarah al-khilaf.
5
Conclude with thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: a person who wakes up late and intends the fast after dawn, then breaks, has no kaffarah according to Abu Hanifa, but would have kaffarah according to Sahibayn.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.

وهو اثنان وعشرون شيئاً: إذا فعل الصائم شيئاً منها طائعا متعمدا غير مضطر لزمه تقريبا وكان مكلفاً القضاء والكفارة، وهي الجماع في أحد السبيلين على الفاعل والمفعول به، والأكل، والشرب، سواء فيه ما يتغذى به أو يتداوى به وابتلاع مطر دخل إلى فمه، ... أي في الفطر كالأشربة لزمه القضاء إلخ: اعلم أن للزوم القضاء والكفارة شروطاً منها ما بينه الشيخ، ومنها ما أهمله، فمن الشروط: فعل الصائم، فإذا لم يفعل الصائم لا يلزم القضاء ولا الكفارة، ومنها: كون الصائم مكلفاً، فإنه إذا فعل الصبي أو المجنون أو غيرهما شيئاً منها لا يلزمهم الكفارة؛ لوجوب الأهلية للزوم، ومنها كونه مبيتا النية، فإنه إذا لم يبيت النية لا يلزمه الكفارة، كمن صام يوماً من رمضان ونوى قبل الزوال ثم أفطر، لا يلزمه الكفارة عند أبي حنيفة خلافاً لهما. ومنها: إيقاع المفسد في أداء رمضان فإن الصائم إذا أفسد قضاء رمضان بعد ما صامه أو غيره لا يلزمه الكفارة، ومنها: عدم طرو المبيح للفطر بعد ارتكاب المنافي، كامرأة أفسدت عمداً، ثم حاضت بعدها في ذلك اليوم أو نفست أو مرضت مرضاً يبيح الفطر، وكذا إذا أفطر الرجل صوم رمضان عمداً، ثم مرض في ذلك اليوم لا يلزمهما الكفارة، أو قبله كرجل صام يوماً من رمضان ثم سافر فأفطر، لا يلزمه الكفارة؛ لطرو المبيح للفطر قبل ارتكاب المنافي.

أن المراد به نقص الثواب حاشية الطحطاوي تسقط الكفارة: [التي وجبت بإرتكاب
The Book of Fasting. Chapter on what invalidates the fast and for which both expiation and making up are obligatory. It is twenty-two things: if the fasting person does any of them voluntarily, intentionally, without being compelled, it obligates him—approximately—and he is legally responsible, the making up and expiation. They are: intercourse in one of the two passages on the doer and the one done to, eating, drinking, equally in it what is nourished with or treated with, and swallowing rain that entered his mouth... i.e., regarding breaking the fast like liquids, the making up is obligatory, etc. Know that for the obligation of making up and expiation there are conditions, some of which the Shaykh explained and some he omitted. Among the conditions: the action of the fasting person; if the fasting person does not do it, making up and expiation are not obligatory. Among them: that the fasting person be legally responsible; if a child or insane person or others do something from them, expiation is not obligatory on them due to the necessity of legal capacity for obligation. Among them: his having made the intention at night; if he did not make the intention at night, expiation is not obligatory on him, like one who fasts a day of Ramadan and intended before noon then broke his fast, expiation is not obligatory on him according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two. Among them: committing the invalidator in the performance of Ramadan; if the fasting person invalidates the make-up of Ramadan after having fasted it or other, expiation is not obligatory on him. Among them: the absence of occurrence of what permits breaking the fast after committing the contrary; like a woman who intentionally invalidated, then menstruated after that in that day or had postpartum bleeding or became sick with a sickness that permits breaking the fast; similarly if a man intentionally broke the fast of Ramadan, then became sick in that day, expiation is not obligatory on them; or before it, like a man who fasted a day of Ramadan then traveled and broke the fast, expiation is not obligatory on him due to the occurrence of what permits breaking the fast before committing the contrary. As for if he broke the fast then traveled voluntarily, the narrations agree on its not dropping because the principle is that if at the end of the day he became in a state such that if he had been in it at the beginning of the day, breaking the fast would be permitted for him, the expiation drops from him. Among them: voluntariness; if he had intercourse with her voluntarily intentionally, each of them is obligated for making up and expiation absolutely, whether the husband coerced the wife or the wife coerced her husband according to the soundest opinion. Among them: intent; so the forgetful and the mistaken are not obligated. Among them: the fasting person not being compelled; for the compelled has no expiation on him. 'What is nourished with' is from food, and it is with the ghain and dhal with dots, a name for the substance eaten as nourishment. He said in al-Jawharah: They differed regarding the meaning of taghadhdhi (being nourished). Some said: that nature inclines to eating it and the stomach's desire is satisfied by it. Some said: it is that its benefit returns to the correction of the body, and its benefit is in the case where one chews a morsel then takes it out then swallows it. According to the second opinion, expiation is obligatory; according to the first, it is not, and this is the most sound because by removing it, the soul dislikes it, as in al-Muhit. Upon this, Ethiopian leaves, hashish, and qatat (a plant), if he eats them, according to the second opinion expiation is not obligatory because there is no benefit for the body, perhaps it harms him and diminishes his intellect, and according to the first opinion it is obligatory because nature inclines to it and the stomach's desire is satisfied by it. I say: and upon this, the innovation that has appeared now, namely smoking (al-dukhan), if one drinks it, regarding the obligation of expiation, there is this difference. So whoever said that taghadhdhi is what nature inclines to and the stomach's desire is satisfied by it makes expiation obligatory; and according to the second interpretation, no. [Maraqi al-Falah and al-Tahtawi: 665] And eating raw meat unless it is wormy, and eating fat in the choice of the jurist Abu al-Layth, and dried meat by agreement, and eating wheat and biting it unless one chews a grain and it disintegrates, and swallowing a grain of wheat, and swallowing a grain of sesame or similar from outside his mouth in the selected view, and eating Armenian clay absolutely (it is known to perfumers), and non-Armenian clay like al-tifl if he is accustomed to eating it, and a little salt in the selected view, and swallowing the saliva of his wife or his friend but not others, and eating intentionally after backbiting, or after cupping, or after touching, or kissing with desire, or after lying together without ejaculation, or after oiling his mustache thinking he has broken the fast thereby, unless a jurist gave him a fatwa, or he heard the hadith and did not know its interpretation according to the madhhab; but if he knew its interpretation, expiation is obligatory upon him. And expiation is obligatory on one who complies with a coercer (a woman who complies with one who forces her). Chapter on expiation and what removes it from liability. Expiation is dropped by the occurrence of menstruation or postpartum bleeding after obligation for the entire day. 'Raw' (al-nayy): it is meat that fire has not touched and is not cooked, and it is said: everything that normally is treated by cooking or something and is not cooked. And it is permissible to say 'nayy' by substitution and assimilation. [Aqrab al-Mawarid] 'Unless it is wormy': 'tadwīd' means it became infested with worms, and expiation is not obligatory by eating it due to its exit from being nourishing. 'Qadid' meat: meat dried in the sun, and it is said: what is cut from it. [Aqrab al-Mawarid] 'And biting it' (qadm): breaking it with the tips of the teeth, as a beast chews barley. 'Qamha' is grain that is ground and made into bread, well-known. 'Fatalashat': it became dissipated, from talashi, derived from 'la shay'. [Maraqi al-Falah: 666] 'Like al-tifl': like clay called tifl. [Maraqi al-Falah: 666] 'Not others': expiation is not obligatory by the saliva of others because he dislikes it, contrary to the wife and friend because he enjoys it. 'And eating intentionally etc.': i.e., if the fasting person backbites someone then eats intentionally, making up and expiation are obligatory on him, whether he heard the hadith, which is his statement: 'Backbiting breaks the fast of the fasting person' or did not hear it, whether he knew its interpretation or not, whether a mufti gave him a fatwa or not. [Maraqi al-Falah with addition: 667] 'Or he heard the hadith' which is his statement: 'The cupper and the cupped break their fast.' [Maraqi al-Falah: 668] 'He knew its interpretation from its requirements.' [Maraqi al-Falah: 668] 'i.e., if expiation became obligatory on a woman by eating intentionally or otherwise, then she became menstruating = that what is meant is reduction of reward.' [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi] 'Expiation is dropped' [which was obligated by committing...]
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the chapter of what invalidates the fast and requires both making up (qada) and expiation (kaffarah). The author begins by stating the general rule: twenty-two actions necessitate qada and kaffarah if done voluntarily, intentionally, without compulsion, and by a legally responsible person. He then lists the conditions for the obligation of kaffarah, explaining each: (1) the action must be performed by the fasting person; (2) the person must be legally responsible (mukallaf), excluding children and the insane; (3) the intention must have been made at night (tabiyyat al-niyya)—Abu Hanifa's position is that if one intended before noon and then broke the fast, no kaffarah is due, contrary to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad; (4) the invalidator must occur during the fast of Ramadan itself (not a make-up or other fast); (5) no valid excuse for breaking the fast should arise after committing the invalidator—for example, if a woman intentionally breaks the fast and then menstruates, or a man becomes sick after breaking, kaffarah is dropped; but if the excuse arises before the invalidator (e.g., travelling and then breaking), kaffarah is also dropped. The condition of volition (tawā'iyya) is emphasized: if both parties willingly engage in intercourse, each must pay kaffarah. Intention ('amd) is required, so forgetfulness or error excludes kaffarah. Compulsion (idṭirār) also precludes kaffarah. The author then delves into the meaning of 'what is nourished with' (mā yutaghaddhā bihī). There is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the definition of taghadhdhi: one view considers it as whatever nature inclines to and satisfies hunger; another view considers it as whatever benefits the body. The author prefers the first view, citing al-Muhit, and applies it to substances like Ethiopian leaves, hashish, and qatat, and even to the newly appeared innovation of smoking (dukhān). On the second view, these do not require kaffarah; on the first, they do. The passage then lists many specific invalidators: eating raw meat (unless wormy), fat, dried meat, wheat and biting it, swallowing grains, eating Armenian clay (or non-Armenian clay if accustomed), a little salt, swallowing the saliva of one's wife or friend, eating intentionally after backbiting, after cupping, after touching or kissing with desire, after lying together without ejaculation, after oiling the mustache thinking one has broken the fast—unless one obtained a fatwa or heard a hadith and did not know its interpretation according to the madhhab (if one knew, kaffarah is due). Also, a woman who complies with a coercer (mukrih) must pay kaffarah. Finally, the chapter on expiation mentions that kaffarah is dropped by the occurrence of menstruation or postpartum bleeding after the obligation if the entire day passes, meaning the reward is reduced but expiation is not required. The commentary provides linguistic clarifications for terms like 'raw meat', 'wormy', 'dried meat', 'biting', 'wheat grain', etc., citing sources like Maraqi al-Falah and Tahtawi.

Key Terms
المبيت النية (al-mabīt al-niyyah): Having made the intention for fasting from the night before; a condition for the validity of the fast in Ramadan according to Abu Hanifa.
الكفارة (al-kaffārah): Expiation: a specific act (freeing a slave, fasting two months, or feeding sixty poor) required as a penalty for intentionally invalidating a Ramadan fast by certain actions.
الطواعية (al-ṭawāʿiyya): Voluntariness; the condition that the invalidating act is done willingly by both parties in the case of intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a fasting person intentionally breaks his Ramadan fast and then becomes sick later in the same day?
A) Kaffarah is obligatory because he intentionally broke the fast.
B) Kaffarah is not obligatory because a valid excuse arose after the invalidator.
C) Kaffarah is obligatory only if he knew the hadith about backbiting.
D) Kaffarah is not obligatory because he is not a mukallaf.

Key Takeaway
Kaffarah is only wajib when all conditions are met; missing any condition means qada only or nothing.

Kaffarah for Breaking the Fast: Ruling and Conditions for Dropping Liability
Ruling on kaffarah and circumstances that remove liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sequential expiation (kaffarah) for intentionally breaking a Ramadan fast, identify the conditions that drop the obligation, and explain the Hanafi ruling on multiple violations.

Core Idea
One kaffarah suffices for multiple violations without intervening expiation, even across two Ramadans.

This lesson presents the Hanafi ruling on the expiation (kaffarah) required for intentionally breaking a Ramadan fast without valid excuse. It covers the sequential options (freeing a slave, fasting, feeding), the precise measurements for the food given, and the conditions that can drop the obligation—such as illness occurring on the day of nullification. The passage also addresses a key intra-Hanafi dispute on whether multiple violations across separate Ramadans require multiple expiations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the kaffarah sequence and its elements
The passage lists three options in order: freeing a slave, fasting two consecutive months, feeding sixty poor people. Note the conditions for each: the slave need not be Muslim, fasting must exclude Eid and Tashreeq days, and feeding can be done by giving half a saa' of wheat or one saa' of dates/barley per person or its value.
The order is wajib; one cannot skip to the next option without inability.
2
Understand the measurement of the food
The text defines the saa' as four mudds, each mudd being two ratls. It provides detailed equivalences in dirhams and barley grains. For feeding, half a saa' of wheat or one saa' of dates/barley is given per poor person.
These classical measurements are fixed; modern equivalents are approximate.
3
Recognize the condition for feeding
If feeding is done on multiple days, the recipients must be the same sixty people each time, or one poor person can be fed for sixty days. Feeding two different groups of sixty does not fulfill the obligation.
4
Analyze the excuses that drop kaffarah
If on the day the fast was broken, menstruation, postpartum bleeding, or illness not caused by one's own action occurs, the kaffarah is dropped. However, if the illness is self-inflicted (e.g., wounding oneself) or if travel occurs (even forced), the kaffarah remains binding.
The excuse must occur on the same day of nullification to drop the obligation.
5
Evaluate the ruling on multiple violations
If a person commits multiple acts of nullification (intercourse or eating) on different days without performing kaffarah in between, one kaffarah suffices—even if the violations span two Ramadans. This is the relied-upon view (Imam Muhammad). If expiation was performed between violations, a new kaffarah is required for each subsequent violation.
The Zahir al-Riwayah holds that separate Ramadans require separate kaffarahs, but the mufta bihi is Imam Muhammad's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكفارة وما يسقطها عن الذمة
كتاب الصوم أو مرض مبيح للفطر في يومه، ولا تسقط عمن سوفر به كرها بعد لزومها عليه في يوم الإفساد كما لو سافر باختياره ظاهر الرواية، والكفارة: تحرير رقبة ولو كانت غير مؤمنة، فإن عجز عنه صام شهرين متتابعين، ليس فيهما يوم عيد ولا أيام التشريق، فإن لم يستطع الصوم أطعم ستين مسكينا يغدّيهم ويعشيهم غداء وعشاء مشبعين أو غداءين، أو عشاءين، أو عشاء من يومين أو من ليلتين وسحورا، أو يعطي كل فقير نصف صاع من بر، أو دقيقه، أو سويقه، أو صاع تمر، أو شعير، أو قيمته، وكفت كفارة واحدة عن جماع وأكل متعدد في أيام لم يتخلله تكفير ولو من رمضانين على الصحيح، فإن تخلل التكفير لا تكفي كفارة واحدة في ظاهر الرواية.
أو مرض مبيح إلخ أطلقه وهو مقيد بمرض حدث من غير صنعه، وأما إذا كان المرض بصنعه، مثل أن جرح نفسه، أو ألقاها من جبل أو سطح فالمختار أنها لا تسقط الكفارة عنه. في يومه قيده به؛ فإنه إذا لم يطرأ عليه ما ذكر من حيض ونفاس ومرض في يوم الإفساد، بل قبله أو بعده لا تسقط عنه الكفارة. تحرير رقبة وتمامه مبين في كفارة الظهار. يغديهم إلخ : أطلقه وهو مقيد بشرط أن يكون الذين أطعمهم ثانياً هم الذين أطعمهم أولاً ، حتى لو غدى ستين، ثم أطعم ستين غيرهم، لم يجز حتى يعيد الإطعام لأحد الفريقين، ولو أطعم فقيراً ستين يوماً أجزأه [مراقي الفلاح: ٦٧٠] نصف صاع: اعلم أن الصاع أربعة أمداد، والمد رطلان والرطل نصف من والمن بالدراهم مائتان وستون درهماً وبالإستار أربعون والإستار - بكسر الهمزة - بالدراهم ستة ونصف وبالمثاقيل أربعة ونصف، فالمد والمن سواء كل منهما ربع صاع رطلان ،بالعراقي والرطل مائة وثلاثون درهما، والدرهم الشرعي أربعة عشر قيراطا. رد المحتار بحذف: ٣٦٥/٢]
أو صاع: اعلم أن الرطل – بكسر الأول وبفتحه عشرون إستارا، والإستار أربعة مثاقيل ونصف مثقال، والمثقال درهم وثلاثة أسباع درهم والدرهم أربعة عشر قيراطا والقيراط خمس شعيرات، فيكون الدرهم سبعين شعيرا، ويكون المثقال مائة شعير أي عشرين قيراطا، ويكون الإستار ستة دراهم وثلاثة أسباع درهم أي أربعمائة وخمسين شعيرا، ويكون الرطل تسعين مثقالا إلا مائة وثمانية وعشرين درهما ونصف درهم ونصف سبع درهم، ويكون المن وهو رطلان مائة وثمانين مثقالا أي مائتين وسبعة وخمسين درهما وسبع درهم، ويكون الصاع سبعمائة وعشرين مثقالا أي ألفاً وثمانية وعشرين درهما ونصف درهم ونصف سبع درهم هذا على ما في بعض الحواشي.
أو قيمته: أي أو يعطى قيمة النصف من البر، أو الصاع من غيره من غير المنصوص عليه، ولو في أوقات متفرقة؛ الحصول الواجب مراقي الفلاح: ٦٧٠] وكفت كفارة إلخ : أي إذا جامع صائم لأداء رمضان مراراً، كمن جامع أول يوم من رمضان وثانيه كذلك، وثالثه كذلك، وهلم جراً، أو أفسد صومه بالأكل متعمداً كذلك، ولم يؤد كفارة الصوم فيما بين هذه الجماعات، تكفي هذه المفطرات كفارة واحدة، ولا يحتاج إلى كفارات متعددة، ولو كانت هذه الجماعات أو الأكلات من رمضانين، وإن أدى كفارة، ثم جامع حال كونه صائماً لأداء رمضان، أو أكل كذلك كمن جامع أول يوم من رمضان عمداً فأدّى الكفارة بأن حرّر الرقبة، أو أطعم ستين مسكيناً، لا تكفي كفارة واحدة.
ولو من رمضانين: قال في "البحر": ولو جامع مراراً في أيام من رمضان واحد ولم يكفر، كان عليه كفارة واحدة؛ لأنها شرعت للزجر وهو يحصل بواحدة؛ فلو جامع وكفر ، ثم جامع مرة أخرى، فعليه كفارة أخرى في ظاهر الرواية؛ للعلم بأن الزجر لم يحصل بالأول، ولو جامع في رمضانين فعليه كفارتان، وإن لم يكفر للأولى في ظاهر الرواية، وهو الصحيح، كذا في "الجوهرة"، وقال محمد عليه ،واحدة قال في "الأسرار": وعليه الاعتماد. البحر الرائق: ١/ ٤٣٤]
The kaffarah and what drops it from liability. The book of fasting: Or an illness that permits breaking the fast on that day, and it is not dropped from one who was taken on a journey unwillingly after it became binding on him on the day of nullification, just as if he travelled by choice according to the apparent narration. And the kaffarah is: freeing a slave even if she is not a believer, and if he is unable to do that, he fasts two consecutive months, in which there is no day of Eid nor days of Tashreeq. And if he is unable to fast, he feeds sixty poor persons, giving them lunch and dinner, a sufficing lunch and dinner, or two lunches, or two dinners, or dinner from two days or two nights and a pre-dawn meal, or he gives each poor person half a saa' of wheat, or its flour, or its groats, or a saa' of dates, or barley, or its value. And one kaffarah suffices for multiple instances of intercourse and eating on days not interspersed with expiation, even if from two Ramadans according to the correct view. And if expiation is interspersed, one kaffarah is not sufficient in the apparent narration. 'Or an illness that permits' etc.: He mentioned it absolutely, but it is restricted to an illness that occurs without his own doing. As for when the illness is by his own doing, such as that he wounded himself or threw himself from a mountain or a roof, the chosen view is that kaffarah is not dropped from him. 'On that day' he restricted it; for if what was mentioned of menstruation, postpartum bleeding, and illness does not occur on the day of nullification, but before or after it, kaffarah is not dropped from him. Freeing a slave: its completion is explained in the kaffarah of zihar. 'Giving them lunch' etc.: He mentioned it absolutely, but it is restricted by the condition that those whom he feeds a second time are those whom he fed the first time, such that if he gave lunch to sixty, then fed sixty others, it is not valid until he repeats the feeding to one of the two groups. And if he fed one poor person for sixty days, it suffices him [Maraqi al-Falah: 670]. Half a saa': Know that a saa' is four mudds, a mudd is two ratls, a ratl is half a mann, and a mann in dirhams is two hundred and sixty dirhams, and in istars is forty, and an istar - with kasra of the hamza - in dirhams is six and a half, and in mithqals is four and a half. So a mudd and a mann are the same: each is a quarter of a saa' - two ratls by Iraqi standard, and a ratl is one hundred and thirty dirhams, and a shar'i dirham is fourteen qirats. Radd al-Muhtar with omission: 2/365. Or a saa': Know that a ratl - with kasra of the first letter and with fatha - is twenty istars, an istar is four and a half mithqals, a mithqal is a dirham and three-sevenths of a dirham, a dirham is fourteen qirats, a qirat is five barley grains, so a dirham is seventy barley grains, and a mithqal is one hundred barley grains, i.e., twenty qirats, and an istar is six dirhams and three-sevenths of a dirham, i.e., four hundred and fifty barley grains, and a ratl is ninety mithqals minus one hundred and twenty-eight and a half dirhams and half a seventh of a dirham, and a mann which is two ratls is one hundred and eighty mithqals, i.e., two hundred and fifty-seven dirhams and one-seventh of a dirham, and a saa' is seven hundred and twenty mithqals, i.e., one thousand and twenty-eight dirhams and a half dirham and half a seventh of a dirham. This is according to what is in some glosses. 'Or its value': i.e., he gives the value of wheat half, or a saa' of other than what is specified, even if at different times; to obtain what is obligatory [Maraqi al-Falah: 670]. 'And one kaffarah suffices' etc.: i.e., if a fasting person for the obligatory Ramadan had intercourse repeatedly, such as he had intercourse on the first day of Ramadan and the second likewise, and the third likewise, and so on, or nullified his fast by eating intentionally likewise, and he did not perform the kaffarah of fasting in between these instances, these nullifications suffice with one kaffarah, and he does not need multiple kaffarahs, even if these instances of intercourse or eating were from two Ramadans. But if he performed a kaffarah, then he had intercourse while being fasting for obligatory Ramadan, or ate likewise, such as one who had intercourse on the first day of Ramadan intentionally and performed the kaffarah by freeing a slave or feeding sixty poor persons, then one kaffarah does not suffice. 'Even if from two Ramadans': He said in 'al-Bahr': If he had intercourse repeatedly on days from one Ramadan and did not expiate, he owes one kaffarah because it is legislated for deterrence and that is achieved by one; so if he had intercourse and expiated, then had intercourse another time, he owes another kaffarah in the apparent narration because it is known that deterrence was not achieved by the first. And if he had intercourse in two Ramadans, he owes two kaffarahs even if he did not expiate for the first in the apparent narration, and this is the correct view, as in 'al-Jawharah'. And Muhammad said: one. It is said in 'al-Asrar': and upon it is reliance. al-Bahr al-Raiq: 1/434]
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah, with expansions from Maraqi al-Falah and other sources, presents the Hanafi ruling on the kaffarah (expiation) for intentionally breaking the fast in Ramadan without a valid excuse, and the circumstances under which this expiation is dropped from liability. The kaffarah is sequential: first, freeing a slave (any slave, even non-Muslim); if unable, fasting two consecutive months (excluding Eid days and days of Tashreeq); if unable, feeding sixty poor people. The feeding must be sufficient lunches and dinners; it can be given to the same poor persons repeatedly or one poor person over sixty days. The text provides detailed measurements for the saa' and other weights. It then discusses when kaffarah is dropped: if a woman starts menstruating or experiencing postpartum bleeding on the day of nullification, or if illness not caused by her own action occurs on that day, the kaffarah is dropped. However, if the illness is self-inflicted (e.g., wounding oneself), kaffarah is not dropped. Similarly, for travel: if one is forced into travel after kaffarah became binding, it is not dropped; if voluntary, also not dropped. The text also addresses multiple violations: if a person commits multiple acts of nullification (intercourse or eating) on different days without performing kaffarah in between, even across two Ramadans, one kaffarah suffices. But if they perform kaffarah after the first violation and then commit another, a separate kaffarah is required. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: the author of al-Bahr (Ibn Nujaym) states the correct view is separate kaffarahs for repeated violations after expiation, and if violations occur in two Ramadans without expiation, two kaffarahs are owed. Imam Muhammad held that one suffices, and some later authorities (al-Asrar) rely on his view. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that one kaffarah suffices for multiple violations without intervening expiation, even across two Ramadans.

Key Terms
كفارة (kaffārah): An expiatory act required for intentional nullification of Ramadan fast without valid excuse.
صاع (ṣāʿ): A volume measure; here defined as 4 mudds, approximately 2.6 kg of wheat.
مد (mudd): A quarter of a saa', equal to two ratls.
رقبة (raqabah): A slave; any slave, even non-Muslim, suffices for kaffarah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is dropped because travel is an excuse.
B) It is not dropped and remains binding.
C) It becomes half of the original.
D) It is postponed until after travel.

Key Takeaway
Kaffarah is a sequential obligation; one kaffarah suffices for multiple violations without intervening expiation, even across two Ramadans.

Kaffarah for Multiple Acts: Sufficiency and Intervening Expiation
Ruling on sufficiency of one kaffarah for multiple violations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when one kaffarah suffices for multiple fast violations and explain the role of intervening expiation and the purpose of deterrence.

Core Idea
One kaffarah suffices for multiple violations if no expiation intervened; deterrence is achieved once.

This passage from Nur al-Idah addresses a practical scenario: a person who violates the Ramadan fast multiple times by intercourse or eating. Does each violation require a separate kaffarah, or can one suffice? The answer hinges on whether an expiation was performed between the violations. The ruling is rooted in the purpose of kaffarah: deterrence (zajr). Once deterrence is achieved, repeating the expiation is unnecessary. However, if deterrence failed (evidenced by a new violation after expiation), a new kaffarah is required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
Each deliberate violation of the Ramadan fast (e.g., intercourse, eating) independently triggers an obligation of kaffarah. The question is whether these obligations merge or remain separate.
Kaffarah is wajib per Hanafi school.
2
Check for intervening expiation
The key variable is whether the person performed kaffarah between violations. If no expiation intervened, the multiple violations are treated as a single unit. If expiation intervened, each subsequent violation creates a new obligation.
3
Apply the deterrence rationale
The law imposes kaffarah to deter the person from repeating the sin. If deterrence is achieved with one kaffarah, repeating it is unnecessary. However, if a violation occurs after expiation, it shows deterrence failed, so a new kaffarah is needed.
4
Consider the two-Ramadan scenario
If violations occur across two Ramadans without intervening expiation, Zahir al-Riwayah requires two kaffarah (one per Ramadan). Imam Muhammad holds one suffices. The relied-upon view is Imam Muhammad's, as per Al-Asrar.
Intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Despite Zahir al-Riwayah's stricter position, later scholarship (Al-Asrar) relies on Imam Muhammad's view that one kaffarah covers both Ramadans if no expiation intervened.
This is the operative fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكفارة وما يسقطها عن الذمة أو شعير، أو قيمته، وكفت كفارة واحدة عن جماع وأكل متعدد في أيام لم يتخلله تكفير ولو من رمضانين على الصحيح، فإن تخلل التكفير لا تكفي كفارة واحدة في ظاهر الرواية. ولو من رمضانين: قال في 'البحر': ولو جامع مراراً في أيام من رمضان واحد ولم يكفر، كان عليه كفارة واحدة؛ لأنها شرعت للزجر وهو يحصل بواحدة؛ فلو جامع وكفر، ثم جامع مرة أخرى، فعليه كفارة أخرى في ظاهر الرواية؛ للعلم بأن الزجر لم يحصل بالأول، ولو جامع في رمضانين فعليه كفارتان، وإن لم يكفر للأولى في ظاهر الرواية، وهو الصحيح، كذا في 'الجوهرة'، وقال محمد عليه واحدة قال في 'الأسرار': وعليه الاعتماد.
The expiation and what removes it from liability: or barley, or its value. And a single expiation suffices for multiple acts of intercourse and eating on days that no expiation intervenes, even if from two Ramadans according to the correct view. If expiation intervenes, one expiation does not suffice in the apparent narration. And 'even if from two Ramadans': He said in 'Al-Bahr': If he had intercourse multiple times on days of one Ramadan and did not expiate, he owes one expiation because it was legislated for deterrence, and deterrence is achieved by one. So if he had intercourse and expiated, then had intercourse again, he owes another expiation in the apparent narration because it is known that deterrence was not achieved by the first. And if he had intercourse in two Ramadans, he owes two expiations even if he did not expiate for the first in the apparent narration, and this is the correct view, as in 'Al-Jawharah'. And Muhammad (al-Shaybani) said: one expiation. He said in 'Al-Asrar': And upon it is reliance.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of multiple violations of the Ramadan fast (intercourse or intentional eating) and whether a single expiation suffices. The ruling is a statement of the relied-upon Hanafi position: if a person commits multiple violations on different days without performing expiation in between, only one expiation is required, even if the violations occur across two Ramadans. This is based on the rationale that the expiation is legislated for deterrence (zajr), and deterrence is achieved by a single act. However, if one expiates after the first violation and then violates again, another expiation is required because the failure of deterrence becomes evident. The passage also notes an intra-madhhab disagreement: Imam Muhammad holds that one expiation suffices even if violations occur across two Ramadans without intervening expiation, and this view is relied upon in later works like 'Al-Asrar'. The authority weight of the ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah (apparent narration) as indicated by the phrase 'fi zahir al-riwayah'.

Key Terms
زجر (zajr): Deterrence; the purpose of kaffarah is to deter the person from repeating the violation.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The apparent or well-known transmission of a ruling from the Hanafi school, as opposed to rare transmissions.
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation for breaking the fast of Ramadan; consists of freeing a slave, or fasting two consecutive months, or feeding sixty poor people.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in this text, how many kaffarah does a person owe if they have intercourse on three days of Ramadan without expiating after any?
A) One kaffarah
B) Three kaffarah
C) Two kaffarah
D) None; they must repeat the fast only

Key Takeaway
One kaffarah covers multiple fast violations if no expiation intervened, because deterrence is achieved once; if expiation intervened, each later violation requires a new kaffarah.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
15 min
Acts That Nullify Fasting Without Kaffarah
Enumeration of items nullifying fast without expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on acts that break the fast without kaffarah and apply the conditions (customary consumption, manner of eating) to determine when kaffarah becomes necessary.

Core Idea
Eating something inedible or via unconventional entry breaks the fast but no kaffarah unless it is customarily eaten and consumed in a normal manner.

This passage from Nur al-Idah lists fifty-seven things that nullify the fast but do not require expiation (kaffarah). While memorizing the list is helpful, the core principle is what matters: anything that is not normal food or not consumed in the usual way still invalidates the fast but does not incur the heavy penalty of kaffarah. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the rulings of fasting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize each listed item as a nullifier (mufsid) that breaks the fast. The ruling is that the fast becomes invalid and must be made up (qada), but no expiation (kaffarah) is required.
The list includes substances like raw rice, clay, stones, metals, and unconventional methods like enema and snuff.
2
Separate categories: inedible substances vs. abnormal consumption
Determine whether the substance is normally inedible (e.g., pebble, raw flour) or normally edible but consumed in an abnormal way (e.g., salt in one go, raw quince not cooked). The former always break without kaffarah; the latter may incur kaffarah if the abnormality is removed (e.g., cooking or salting).
The condition 'daf'atan' (at once) is key: eating a normally inedible substance in one go means no kaffarah; eating it in small installments may trigger kaffarah.
3
Apply the exception for habitual consumption or edible state
If a person is accustomed (i'tiyad) to eating something like clay, or if the substance becomes edible through cooking or salting, then kaffarah becomes necessary because it is now considered normal food.
For example, Armenian clay is considered edible (used medicinally), so kaffarah applies. Similarly, raw quince once cooked or salted is normal food.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on enema and snuff
Abu Yusuf held that enema and snuff require kaffarah because they involve entering through a natural orifice. The relied-upon view (Imam Muhammad) says no kaffarah because the substance reaches the brain, not the stomach, and is not nutritive.
The phrase 'ala al-ashahh' in the text indicates the adopted view.
5
Apply the principle to new cases
Use the criteria: Is the substance normally eaten? Is it eaten in a customary manner (one go vs. installments)? Does it enter through a normal digestive route? Kaffarah only when both answers are 'yes'.
E.g., swallowing a vitamin pill: if swallowed whole like a stone, no kaffarah; if chewed and it is a food supplement, kaffarah may apply if it is customarily eaten.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يفسد الصوم من غير كفارة وهو سبعة وخمسون شيئاً إذا أكل الصائم أرزا نيَّا، أو عجينا، أو دقيقا، أو ملحا أي في أداء رمضان كثيرا دفعة أو طينا غير أرمني لم يعتد أكله أو نواة، أو قطنا، أو كاغذا، أو سفرجلا ولم يطبخ أو جوزة رطبة، أو ابتلع حصاة أو حديدا أو ترابا أو حجرا، أو احتقن، أو استعط، أو أوجر بصب شيء في حلقه على الأصح، أو أقطر في أذنه دهنا أو ماء، في الأصح، أو داوى جائفة، أرزا نيا: الأرزحب معروف أو عجينا وهو الدقيق المعجون بالماء دفعة قيده بها؛ لأنه إذا أكله بدفعات فبأول دفعة قليلة يجب القضاء والكفارة. لم يعتد أكله: أما إذا اعتاده أو كان الطين أرمنياً لزمت الكفارة مطلقاً. أو كاغذا: أو نحوه مما لا يؤكل عادة أو سفرجلا : [بفتحتين وجيم مفتوح، فاكهة معروفة أو نحوه من الثمار التي لا توكل قبل النضج. ولم يطبخ أي ولم يملح أيضا، أما إذا وجد أحدهما تلزم الكفارة؛ لأنه مما يؤكل عادة جوزة: أطلقها وهي مقيدة بما إذا ليس لها لب، وابتلع اليابسة بلبها فلا كفارة عليه، ولو ابتلع لوزة رطبة تلزمه الكفارة؛ لأنها تؤكل عادة مع القشر، وبمضغ اليابسة مع قشرها، ووصل الممضوغ إلى جوفه، اختلف في لزوم الكفارة. أو حديدا أو نحاساً أو ذهباً أو فضة. أو احتقن أو استعط: الحقنة صب الدواء في الدبر والسعوط صبه الدواء في الأنف. على الأصح متعلق بالاحتقان وما بعده، وهو احتراز عن قول أبي يوسف الله بوجوب الكفارة. في الأصح وجه فساد الصوم وصول المفطر دماغه بفعله، فلا عبرة لصلاح البدن، قاله خان، وحققه الكمال، وفي 'المحيط': الصحيح أنه لا يفطر؛ لأن الماء يضر الدماغ، فانعدم المفطر صورة قاضي وهو الابتلاع، ومعنى وهو الإنتفاع جائفة: وهي جراحة في البطن أي داوى بدواء رطباً كان أو يابساً جراحةً في البطن، ولا تتعجب إذا سمعت أن معناه: داوى جراحة بأدوية جافة أي يابسة وفائدة هذا القيد أن الدواء لو كان رطبا يصل إلى الجوف، واليابس لا، ولا تقل الجافة مضاعف والجائفة أجوف؛ فإن الجمل قد شاع، والعلم بأسره ضاع.
Chapter of what breaks the fast without expiation. And it is fifty-seven things: if the fasting person eats raw rice, or dough, or flour, or salt—meaning in performing Ramadan, a large amount at once—or clay other than Armenian that he is not accustomed to eating, or a date pit, or cotton, or paper, or quince and it is not cooked, or a wet walnut, or he swallows a pebble, iron, dirt, or a stone, or he administers an enema, or snuff, or he is made to drink by pouring something down his throat according to the most correct view, or he drops oil or water into his ear according to the most correct view, or he treats an internal wound. [Commentary] Raw rice: it is known [grain]. Dough: flour mixed with water. The condition 'at once' is because if he eats it in installments, for the first small installment he must make up the fast and pay expiation. 'Not accustomed to eating it': if he is accustomed or the clay is Armenian, expiation is necessary absolutely. 'Paper': or what is similar that is not normally eaten. 'Quince': a known fruit or similar fruits that are not eaten before ripening. 'And not cooked': meaning also not salted; if either is present, expiation is necessary because it is normally eaten. 'Walnut': it is unrestricted but conditioned as having no kernel; if he swallows a dry one with kernel, no expiation; if he swallows a wet almond, expiation is necessary because it is normally eaten with its peel. As for chewing a dry one with its peel, and the chewed matter reaches his stomach, there is disagreement on the obligation of expiation. 'Iron': or copper, gold, or silver. 'Enema or snuff': enema is pouring medicine into the anus; snuff is pouring medicine into the nose. 'According to the most correct view' is connected to enema and what follows, and it is a caution against the statement of Abu Yusuf requiring expiation. In the most correct view, the nullification of the fast is due to the reaching of the nullifier to his brain by his action, so consideration is not given to the benefit of the body. Khan said this, and al-Kamal verified it. In al-Muhit: the correct view is that it does not break the fast because water harms the brain, so the nullifier is absent in form and meaning. 'Internal wound' (ja'ifah): a wound in the stomach; meaning he treated the wound with medicine, wet or dry. Do not be surprised to hear that meaning: he treated the wound with dry medicines. The benefit of this restriction is that if the medicine is wet, it reaches the inside; dry does not. Do not say that 'dry' is doubled and 'internal' is hollow, for the sentence has become widespread, and all knowledge is lost.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage lists fifty-seven items that break the fast (nullify it) but do not require expiation (kaffarah). The list includes substances that are not normally consumed for nourishment or are inedible in their raw state. The key principle is that expiation is only due for eating something that is normally eaten and in a customary manner. Therefore, eating raw rice, dry flour, salt in large quantity at once, clay not customarily eaten, stones, metals, etc., breaks the fast but no kaffarah. However, if one eats these in small installments or if they become edible (like cooked quince or salted), then kaffarah becomes necessary because it becomes 'normal food'. The commentary also clarifies that if one is accustomed to eating clay (like Armenian clay used medicinally), then kaffarah applies. The passage also includes methods like enema or snuff that break the fast through reaching the brain. There is a dispute with Abu Yusuf who required kaffarah for enema, but the relied-upon view is that no kaffarah is needed. Similarly, treating an internal wound with dry medicine does not break the fast because it does not reach the stomach. Overall, the passage provides detailed case-by-case application of the rule that nullification requires the entry of something into the body through a normal orifice, but kaffarah is tied to the intention of nourishment and custom.

Key Terms
دفعة (daf'atan): At once; eating the substance in one go, not in small installments.
اعتياد (i'tiyad): Habitual eating; if one is accustomed to eating something like clay, it becomes a normal food and kaffarah applies.
جائفة (ja'ifah): An internal wound, specifically a wound that penetrates the abdominal cavity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a fasting person swallows a pebble, what is the ruling?
A) Qada only, no kaffarah
B) Qada and kaffarah
C) No qada, no kaffarah
D) Only kaffarah

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule: any inedible substance or unconventional entry breaks the fast (qada wajib), but kaffarah is only due when the substance is normally eaten and consumed in a customary manner.
