Conditions for Breaking the Fast: Breastfeeding Women and Travelers
Rules for breaking fast due to breastfeeding and travel

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a breastfeeding woman or traveler may break the fast, and identify the preferred ruling based on the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Breaking the fast due to breastfeeding or travel is permissible only under specific conditions of feared harm; otherwise fasting is superior.

This passage from Nur al-Idah provides key rulings on valid excuses for breaking the fast. It covers the breastfeeding woman (whether mother or wet nurse) and the traveler, detailing when it is permissible to break the fast and when it is better to fast. Understanding these conditions prevents the common error of assuming that travel or breastfeeding automatically permits breaking the fast without further consideration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of women included
Note that the text explicitly includes both the biological mother (relative) and the wet nurse (by contract). Make sure to understand the basis for each: the mother's obligation is religious, the wet nurse's is contractual.
The hashiyah clarifies that some earlier sources limited the ruling to the wet nurse, but the correct Hanafi position includes both.
2
Determine the standard of fear
The fear of harm must be based on ghalabat al-zann (overwhelming probability), established either by personal experience or by the report of a trustworthy Muslim physician who is skilled and just.
This standard applies to both the breastfeeding woman and the traveler who fears thirst or hunger.
3
Apply the conditions for the traveler
The traveler must have begun his journey before dawn. Additionally, breaking is permissible only if fasting would cause harm; if no harm, fasting is better. Also consider the group: if the majority are fasting, it is better to fast; if they are breaking or share expenses, it is better to break out of conformity.
The harm that justifies breaking must be something short of fear of destruction; if destruction is feared, breaking becomes obligatory.
4
Recognize the distinction between permissible and obligatory
The text distinguishes between cases where breaking is merely permissible (mubah) and where it becomes obligatory (wajib). The key is the level of harm: if there is a genuine fear of destruction or loss of senses, breaking is wajib; otherwise it is permissible but fasting is better.
This is a crucial nuance often missed in general discussions.
5
Note the rulings on making up fasts and bequest
Missed fasts must be made up when the excuse ends, and continuity is not required. If a person dies before the excuse is removed, no bequest is required for expiation. If some days cannot be made up due to prohibited days, ransom is given for those days.
These rules apply only to those who broke the fast with a valid excuse.

Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage opens the section on 'Concomitants of Fasting' from Nur al-Idah. It presents a series of rulings regarding permissible excuses for breaking the fast and their conditions. The first ruling concerns a breastfeeding woman—whether she is the biological mother or a wet nurse. The fear of harm must be based on strong probability from experience or a trustworthy Muslim physician's report. Similarly, one who fears severe thirst or hunger that could lead to destruction may break the fast. For a traveler, breaking the fast is permissible, but fasting is more meritorious if it causes no harm and the majority of his traveling companions are also fasting. However, if the companions share expenses or are breaking their fasts due to shared expenses, it is better for the traveler to break his fast out of conformity. The passage also clarifies that if a person with a valid excuse (like illness or travel) dies before the excuse is removed, no bequest is required for the expiation of missed fasts. Those who are able must make up missed fasts according to the days they were healthy or resident, and continuity is not required in making up missed fasts.

Key Terms
مرضع (murdi'): A woman who is breastfeeding a child, whether biological mother or wet nurse.
ظئر (zhi'r): A wet nurse hired to breastfeed a child.
غلبة الظن (ghalabat al-zann): Overwhelming probability that harm will occur, based on experience or a trustworthy physician's report.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Breaking the fast due to breastfeeding or travel is conditional on genuine fear of harm; otherwise fasting is preferred.

Making Up Missed Fasts Before the Next Ramadan
Ruling on precedence of qada over new Ramadan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule regarding missed fasts when the next Ramadan arrives and explain why no fidyah is required for delay.

Core Idea
Missed fasts must be made up before the next Ramadan; delay does not incur fidyah.

This lesson addresses a common scenario: a person has missed fasts from a previous Ramadan, and the next Ramadan arrives before they have made them up. What is the ruling? The text provides a clear answer along with a legal subtlety about intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A person has qada fasts from last Ramadan and the next Ramadan arrives.
2
Determine the ruling
The missed fasts take precedence – they must be made up before starting the new Ramadan.
The verb "قدم" (yuqaddamu) indicates priority.
3
Check consequence of delay
No fidyah (ransom) is required for delaying until the next Ramadan; the obligation remains to make them up later.
4
Understand legal nuance
If one intends a make-up fast while the current Ramadan is ongoing, the fast defaults to the current Ramadan's obligation unless specifically intended as qada.
From the commentary: "law nawāhu 'an al-qaḍā' lā yaqa'u illā 'an al-adā'"

If another Ramadan comes, (the missed fasts) take precedence over making up (i.e., must be made up before the next Ramadan), and there is no ransom for delaying it to that time. And it is permissible to break the fast for a decrepit old man and a decrepit old woman, and they are required to give a ransom for each day of half a sa' of wheat, like one who vows to fast forever and becomes weak due to preoccupation with livelihood: he breaks the fast and gives ransom. If he cannot afford the ransom due to his poverty, he seeks forgiveness from Allah and asks for His pardon. And if an expiation of an oath or killing becomes obligatory on him, and he cannot find what to expiate with—like freeing a slave—and he is a decrepit old man, or he did not fast until he became decrepit, it is not permissible for him to pay ransom because fasting here is a substitute for something else. And it is permissible for a voluntary faster to break his fast without an excuse in one narration, and hospitality is an excuse on the strongest view for both the guest and the host. 'Takes precedence over making up': i.e., legally, so that if he intends it as make-up, it does not count except as the current (Ramadan) fast, as was mentioned earlier. [Maraqi al-Falah: 688] (Hashiyah al-Tahtawi) 'And he asks for His pardon': i.e., he asks Him for forgiveness for his shortcoming in His right. [Maraqi al-Falah: 688] Expiation of an oath: it is that which is explained in His words: 'But its expiation is feeding ten poor people from the average of what you feed your families or clothing them or freeing a slave. And whoever cannot find (the means) then (he shall fast) three days.' (Al-Ma'idah: 89) Or (expiation of) killing, which is in His words: 'And never is it for a believer to kill a believer except by mistake. And whoever kills a believer by mistake (must) free a believing slave and pay blood money to his family unless they charity. But if the deceased was from a people at war with you and he was a believer, then (only) freeing a believing slave; and if he was from a people with whom you have a treaty, then blood money to his family and freeing a believing slave. And whoever cannot find (the means) then (he must fast) two consecutive months.' (An-Nisa: 92) 'Because fasting here...': Know that the ruling is dependent on establishing two premises from the evidence. The first is clear, which is that one cannot resort to fasting except when unable to expiate with wealth; the second the author did not mention, which is that ransom is only permitted for fasting which is originally obligatory in itself, not as a substitute for something else. And (this is) in a narration, and this narration is from Abu Yusuf, and the apparent position of the school is that he cannot break the fast except for an excuse, and it is corrected in al-Muhit. He confined himself to this narration because it is more preponderant from the evidence side, and therefore the muhaqqiq in Fath al-Qadir chose it. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 2/450]

Commentary
This passage continues the section on Concomitants of Fasting and addresses several distinct rulings. First, if a person has missed fasts from a previous Ramadan and the next Ramadan arrives, they must make up the missed fasts before starting the new Ramadan; delaying the make-up without reason does not require a ransom, but the obligation remains. Second, a decrepit old man or woman who is physically declining and unable to fast (with continuous incapacity until death) is permitted to break the fast and must pay a ransom of half a sa' of wheat for each missed day. This is similar to someone who vowed to fast continuously but cannot due to work. If such a person cannot afford the ransom, they simply seek Allah's forgiveness. The passage then clarifies that if a person owes an expiation (kaffarah) for an oath or accidental killing and cannot afford it, and they are elderly, they cannot simply pay a ransom for it because in those cases fasting is a substitute for another act (like freeing a slave) and only the person who is originally obligated to fast (like an elderly person) can use ransom. Finally, regarding voluntary fasting: according to one narration from Abu Yusuf, it is permissible to break a voluntary fast without any excuse, though the apparent position of the school is that breaking is only allowed for a valid excuse. Hospitality (for a guest or host) is considered a valid excuse on the strongest view.

Key Terms
قضاء (qaḍā'): Making up missed obligatory acts of worship, here specifically missed fasts.
فدية (fidyah): A ransom payment of food for each missed fast when unable to fast permanently.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if another Ramadan arrives before a person has made up missed fasts, what is the ruling?
A) The missed fasts are cancelled and no action required.
B) The missed fasts take precedence and must be made up; no fidyah for delay.
C) The person must pay fidyah for each missed day and then start the new Ramadan.
D) The missed fasts are transferred to the new Ramadan automatically.

Key Takeaway
Missed fasts must be made up before the next Ramadan; delay does not incur fidyah but the obligation remains.

The Elderly: Permission to Break Fast and Obligation of Fidyah
Ruling on elderly breaking fast and fidyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which an elderly person may break the fast and state the amount and obligation of fidyah.

Core Idea
Elderly with permanent incapacity may break fast and must pay half a sa‘ of wheat daily.

This lesson covers a special concession: a decrepit old man or woman (fani) who is in daily decline and permanently unable to fast may break the Ramadan fast. However, they must give a ransom (fidyah) for each missed day. The ruling also applies to those who have vowed to fast continuously but cannot due to work. The condition is that the incapacity lasts until death.

Guided Path
1
Define the subject
Identify who is a 'fani': a person who declines daily and is permanently unable to fast (dawam al-‘ajz hatta al-mawt).
Also includes a sick person with no hope of recovery.
2
State the permission
It is permissible (ja'iz) for such a person to break the fast during Ramadan.
3
State the obligation
Fidyah is wajib: half a sa‘ of wheat per day missed.
Same ruling applies to someone who vowed to fast continuously but cannot due to livelihood.
4
Address inability to pay fidyah
If they cannot afford the fidyah, they should seek Allah's forgiveness and ask for His pardon.
No alternative obligation; they are excused due to poverty.

Source Text

فإن جاء رمضان آخر قدم على القضاء، ولا فدية بالتأخير إليه، ويجوز الفطر لشيخ فان وعجوز فانية، وتلزمهما الفدية لكل يوم نصف صاع من بر كمن نذر صوم الأبد، فضعف عنه لاشتغاله بالمعيشة يفطر ،ويفدي فإن لم يقدر على الفدية لعسرته يستغفر الله تعالى، ويستقيله ولو وجبت عليه كفارة يمين أو قتل، فلم يجد ما يكفّر به من عتق، وهو شيخ فانٍ، أو لم يصم حتى صار فانيا لا يجوز له الفدية؛ لأن الصوم هنا بدل عن غيره، ويجوز للمتطوع الفطر بلا عذر في رواية، والضيافة عذر على الأظهر للضيف والمضيف،

لكل يوم، وإن لم يقدر على الصوم لشدة الحر أفطر، ويقضيه في الشتاء. [حاشية الطحطاوي بحذف: ٦٨٨] وتلزمهما الفدية: لو قال: وتلزمهما الفدية كالفطر لكان أخصر وأشمل. [ مراقي الفلاح: ٦٨٨] (حاشية الطحطاوي) ويستقيله أي يطلب منه العفو عن تقصيره في حقه. [مراقي الفلاح: ٦٨٨]
كفارة يمين: هي التي بينها في قوله تعالى: ﴿فَكَفَّارَتُهُ إِطْعَامُ عَشَرَةِ مَسَاكِينَ مِنْ أَوْسَطِ مَا تُطْعِمُونَ أَهْلِيكُمْ أَوْ كِسْوَتُهُمْ أَوْ تَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ فَمَنْ لَمْ يَجِدْ فَصِيَامُ ثَلاثَةِ أَيَّامٍ :(المائدة: (۸۹) أو قتل وهي التي في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا كَانَ لِمُؤْمِن أَنْ يَقْتُلَ مُؤْمِناً إِلَّا خَطَأَ وَمَنْ قَتَلَ مُؤْمِناً خَطَأَ فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُؤْمِنَةٍ وَدِيَةٌ مُسَلَّمَةٌ إِلَى أَهْلِهِ إِلَّا أَنْ يَصَّدَّقُوا فَإِنْ كَانَ مِنْ قَوْمٍ عَدُوٌّ لَكُمْ وَهُوَ مُؤْمِنٌ فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُؤمِنَةٍ وَإِنْ كَانَ مِنْ قَوْمٍ بَيْنَكُمْ وَبَيْنَهُمْ مِيثَاقَ فَدِيَةٌ مُسَلَّمَةٌ إِلَى أَهْلِهِ وَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُؤْمِنَةٍ فَمَنْ لَمْ يَجِدْ فَصِيَامُ شَهْرَيْنِ مُتَتَابِعَيْنِ إلى [النساء: ٩٢]. لأن الصوم الخ: اعلم أن الحكم موقوف على إثبات مقدمتين من الدليل، والأولى بينها الشيخ، ومما يؤيدها أنه لا يجوز المصير إلى الصوم إلا عند العجز عما يكفر به من المال والثانية لم يذكرها الشيخ، وهي أنها لا تجوز الفدية إلا عن صوم هو أصل بنفسه لا بدل عن غيره. في رواية وهذه الرواية عن أبي يوسف، وظاهر الرواية أنه ليس له الفطر إلا من عذر، وصححه في "المحيط"، وإنما اقتصر على هذه الرواية؛ لأنها أرجح من جهة الدليل، ولهذا اختارها المحقق في "فتح القدير. [البحر الرائق: ٤٥٠/٢]
If another Ramadan comes, (the missed fasts) take precedence over making up (i.e., must be made up before the next Ramadan), and there is no ransom for delaying it to that time. And it is permissible to break the fast for a decrepit old man and a decrepit old woman, and they are required to give a ransom for each day of half a sa' of wheat, like one who vows to fast forever and becomes weak due to preoccupation with livelihood: he breaks the fast and gives ransom. If he cannot afford the ransom due to his poverty, he seeks forgiveness from Allah and asks for His pardon. And if an expiation of an oath or killing becomes obligatory on him, and he cannot find what to expiate with—like freeing a slave—and he is a decrepit old man, or he did not fast until he became decrepit, it is not permissible for him to pay ransom because fasting here is a substitute for something else. And it is permissible for a voluntary faster to break his fast without an excuse in one narration, and hospitality is an excuse on the strongest view for both the guest and the host. 'Takes precedence over making up': i.e., legally, so that if he intends it as make-up, it does not count except as the current (Ramadan) fast, as was mentioned earlier. [Maraqi al-Falah: 687] 'And it is permissible to break the fast': on condition that the incapacity of the decrepit man or woman continues until death. [Maraqi al-Falah (688] 'A decrepit old man': he is one who daily declines until he dies; and the sick person, if recovery to health that enables him to fast is hopeless, he must pay ransom for each day; and if he cannot fast due to severe heat, he breaks and makes it up in winter. [Hashiyah al-Tahtawi, abridged: 688] 'And the ransom is required of them': if he said: 'And the ransom is required of them like the breaking of the fast', it would be more concise and comprehensive. [Maraqi al-Falah: 688] (Hashiyah al-Tahtawi) 'And he asks for His pardon': i.e., he asks Him for forgiveness for his shortcoming in His right. [Maraqi al-Falah: 688] Expiation of an oath: it is that which is explained in His words: 'But its expiation is feeding ten poor people from the average of what you feed your families or clothing them or freeing a slave. And whoever cannot find (the means) then (he shall fast) three days.' (Al-Ma'idah: 89) Or (expiation of) killing, which is in His words: 'And never is it for a believer to kill a believer except by mistake. And whoever kills a believer by mistake (must) free a believing slave and pay blood money to his family unless they charity. But if the deceased was from a people at war with you and he was a believer, then (only) freeing a believing slave; and if he was from a people with whom you have a treaty, then blood money to his family and freeing a believing slave. And whoever cannot find (the means) then (he must fast) two consecutive months.' (An-Nisa: 92) 'Because fasting here...': Know that the ruling is dependent on establishing two premises from the evidence. The first is clear, which is that one cannot resort to fasting except when unable to expiate with wealth; the second the author did not mention, which is that ransom is only permitted for fasting which is originally obligatory in itself, not as a substitute for something else. And (this is) in a narration, and this narration is from Abu Yusuf, and the apparent position of the school is that he cannot break the fast except for an excuse, and it is corrected in al-Muhit. He confined himself to this narration because it is more preponderant from the evidence side, and therefore the muhaqqiq in Fath al-Qadir chose it. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 2/450]

فدية (fidyah): Ransom of half a sa‘ of wheat per missed day.

Elderly and Kaffarah: Why Fidyah Does Not Apply
Ruling on elderly and kaffarah: no fidyah for substitute fasts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why an elderly person who owes kaffarah (expiation for oath or killing) cannot use fidyah as a substitute for fasting.

Core Idea
Fidyah only applies to fasts that are originally obligatory on the person, not to fasting as a substitute for another act.

This lesson clarifies a common confusion: if an elderly person owes an expiation (kaffarah) for breaking an oath or accidental killing, and they cannot afford the primary expiation (freeing a slave, feeding or clothing poor), they must fast as a substitute. But if they are already elderly and unable to fast, they cannot pay fidyah for this fasting because the fast here is a substitute (badal) for a material act, not an original obligation. The ruling depends on two premises explained in the commentary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A person owes kaffarah yamin (oath) or kaffarah qatl (killing) but cannot afford the primary options (freeing a slave, feeding/clothing ten poor).
The primary expiation involves wealth or physical action.
2
Check the substitute
Islam allows fasting (3 days for oath, 2 consecutive months for killing) as a substitute when unable to afford the primary.
3
Apply to elderly
If the person is elderly (fani) and cannot fast either, they cannot pay fidyah as a substitute for the fasting.
4
Understand the reason
Fidyah is only for a fast that is original and independent; here the fasting is a substitute (badal) for something else, so fidyah does not apply.
Two premises: 1) cannot resort to fasting unless unable to expiate with wealth; 2) fidyah only for original fasts, not substitutes.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

كفارة يمين (kaffārat yamīn): Expiation for breaking an oath: feed/clothe 10 poor or free slave, or fast 3 days if unable.
بدل (badal): Substitute; the fasting in kaffarah is a replacement for the material expiation.

Checkpoint
Why can't an elderly person pay fidyah for the fasting required in kaffarah?
A) Because they are already paying fidyah for Ramadan.

C) Because they must fast regardless of age.
D) Because kaffarah fasting is only three days.

Key Takeaway
Fidyah is not permissible for kaffarah fasting because that fasting is a substitute, not an original obligation.

Voluntary Fasting: Breaking Without an Excuse
Ruling on breaking voluntary fast and the excuse of hospitality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi position on breaking a voluntary fast without a valid excuse and identify the exception for hospitality.

Core Idea
The relied-upon Hanafi position: a voluntary faster may not break without a valid excuse; hospitality is a valid excuse.

This lesson addresses a difference within the Hanafi school regarding voluntary (nafl) fasting. According to one narration from Abu Yusuf, it is permissible to break a voluntary fast without any excuse. However, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that an excuse is required. The passage also mentions that hospitality (being a guest or host) is considered a valid excuse on the strongest view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
A person is performing a voluntary (nafl) fast.
2
State the narration
Abu Yusuf narrated that it is permissible to break without an excuse (fi riwayah).
This is a weaker narration (nawadir).
3
State the relied-upon position
The apparent position of the school (zahir ar-riwayah) is that breaking is not allowed without a valid excuse.
This is the mufta bihi view, as corrected in al-Muhit and selected by the muhaqqiq in Fath al-Qadir.
4
Note the exception
Hospitality is considered a valid excuse for both the guest and the host, on the strongest view (ala al-azhar).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن جاء رمضان آخر قدم على القضاء، ولا فدية بالتأخير إليه، ويجوز الفطر لشيخ فان وعجوز فانية، وتلزمهما الفدية لكل يوم نصف صاع من بر كمن نذر صوم الأبد، فضعف عنه لاشتغاله بالمعيشة يفطر ،ويفدي فإن لم يقدر على الفدية لعسرته يستغفر الله تعالى، ويستقيله ولو وجبت عليه كفارة يمين أو قتل، فلم يجد ما يكفّر به من عتق، وهو شيخ فانٍ، أو لم يصم حتى صار فانيا لا يجوز له الفدية؛ لأن الصوم هنا بدل عن غيره، ويجوز للمتطوع الفطر بلا عذر في رواية، والضيافة عذر على الأظهر للضيف والمضيف،
قدم على القضاء: أي شرعاً، حتى لو نواه عن القضاء لا يقع إلا عن الأداء كما تقدم. [مراقي الفلاح: ٦٨٧] ويجوز الفطر: بشرط دوام عجز الفاني أو الفانية إلى الموت. [مراقي الفلاح (٦۸۸] لشيخ فان: هو الذي كل يوم في نقص إلى أن يموت والمريض إذا تحقق اليأس عن الصحة أي صحة يقدر معها على الصوم، فعليه الفدية
لكل يوم، وإن لم يقدر على الصوم لشدة الحر أفطر، ويقضيه في الشتاء. [حاشية الطحطاوي بحذف: ٦٨٨] وتلزمهما الفدية: لو قال: وتلزمهما الفدية كالفطر لكان أخصر وأشمل. [ مراقي الفلاح: ٦٨٨] (حاشية الطحطاوي) ويستقيله أي يطلب منه العفو عن تقصيره في حقه. [مراقي الفلاح: ٦٨٨]
كفارة يمين: هي التي بينها في قوله تعالى: ﴿فَكَفَّارَتُهُ إِطْعَامُ عَشَرَةِ مَسَاكِينَ مِنْ أَوْسَطِ مَا تُطْعِمُونَ أَهْلِيكُمْ أَوْ كِسْوَتُهُمْ أَوْ تَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ فَمَنْ لَمْ يَجِدْ فَصِيَامُ ثَلاثَةِ أَيَّامٍ :(المائدة: (۸۹) أو قتل وهي التي في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا كَانَ لِمُؤْمِن أَنْ يَقْتُلَ مُؤْمِناً إِلَّا خَطَأَ وَمَنْ قَتَلَ مُؤْمِناً خَطَأَ فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُؤْمِنَةٍ وَدِيَةٌ مُسَلَّمَةٌ إِلَى أَهْلِهِ إِلَّا أَنْ يَصَّدَّقُوا فَإِنْ كَانَ مِنْ قَوْمٍ عَدُوٌّ لَكُمْ وَهُوَ مُؤْمِنٌ فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُؤمِنَةٍ وَإِنْ كَانَ مِنْ قَوْمٍ بَيْنَكُمْ وَبَيْنَهُمْ مِيثَاقَ فَدِيَةٌ مُسَلَّمَةٌ إِلَى أَهْلِهِ وَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُؤْمِنَةٍ فَمَنْ لَمْ يَجِدْ فَصِيَامُ شَهْرَيْنِ مُتَتَابِعَيْنِ إلى [النساء: ٩٢]. لأن الصوم الخ: اعلم أن الحكم موقوف على إثبات مقدمتين من الدليل، والأولى بينها الشيخ، ومما يؤيدها أنه لا يجوز المصير إلى الصوم إلا عند العجز عما يكفر به من المال والثانية لم يذكرها الشيخ، وهي أنها لا تجوز الفدية إلا عن صوم هو أصل بنفسه لا بدل عن غيره. في رواية وهذه الرواية عن أبي يوسف، وظاهر الرواية أنه ليس له الفطر إلا من عذر، وصححه في "المحيط"، وإنما اقتصر على هذه الرواية؛ لأنها أرجح من جهة الدليل، ولهذا اختارها المحقق في "فتح القدير. [البحر الرائق: ٤٥٠/٢]
If another Ramadan comes, (the missed fasts) take precedence over making up (i.e., must be made up before the next Ramadan), and there is no ransom for delaying it to that time. And it is permissible to break the fast for a decrepit old man and a decrepit old woman, and they are required to give a ransom for each day of half a sa' of wheat, like one who vows to fast forever and becomes weak due to preoccupation with livelihood: he breaks the fast and gives ransom. If he cannot afford the ransom due to his poverty, he seeks forgiveness from Allah and asks for His pardon. And if an expiation of an oath or killing becomes obligatory on him, and he cannot find what to expiate with—like freeing a slave—and he is a decrepit old man, or he did not fast until he became decrepit, it is not permissible for him to pay ransom because fasting here is a substitute for something else. And it is permissible for a voluntary faster to break his fast without an excuse in one narration, and hospitality is an excuse on the strongest view for both the guest and the host. 'Takes precedence over making up': i.e., legally, so that if he intends it as make-up, it does not count except as the current (Ramadan) fast, as was mentioned earlier. [Maraqi al-Falah: 687] 'And it is permissible to break the fast': on condition that the incapacity of the decrepit man or woman continues until death. [Maraqi al-Falah (688] 'A decrepit old man': he is one who daily declines until he dies; and the sick person, if recovery to health that enables him to fast is hopeless, he must pay ransom for each day; and if he cannot fast due to severe heat, he breaks and makes it up in winter. [Hashiyah al-Tahtawi, abridged: 688] 'And the ransom is required of them': if he said: 'And the ransom is required of them like the breaking of the fast', it would be more concise and comprehensive. [Maraqi al-Falah: 688] (Hashiyah al-Tahtawi) 'And he asks for His pardon': i.e., he asks Him for forgiveness for his shortcoming in His right. [Maraqi al-Falah: 688] Expiation of an oath: it is that which is explained in His words: 'But its expiation is feeding ten poor people from the average of what you feed your families or clothing them or freeing a slave. And whoever cannot find (the means) then (he shall fast) three days.' (Al-Ma'idah: 89) Or (expiation of) killing, which is in His words: 'And never is it for a believer to kill a believer except by mistake. And whoever kills a believer by mistake (must) free a believing slave and pay blood money to his family unless they charity. But if the deceased was from a people at war with you and he was a believer, then (only) freeing a believing slave; and if he was from a people with whom you have a treaty, then blood money to his family and freeing a believing slave. And whoever cannot find (the means) then (he must fast) two consecutive months.' (An-Nisa: 92) 'Because fasting here...': Know that the ruling is dependent on establishing two premises from the evidence. The first is clear, which is that one cannot resort to fasting except when unable to expiate with wealth; the second the author did not mention, which is that ransom is only permitted for fasting which is originally obligatory in itself, not as a substitute for something else. And (this is) in a narration, and this narration is from Abu Yusuf, and the apparent position of the school is that he cannot break the fast except for an excuse, and it is corrected in al-Muhit. He confined himself to this narration because it is more preponderant from the evidence side, and therefore the muhaqqiq in Fath al-Qadir chose it. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 2/450]
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage continues the section on Concomitants of Fasting and addresses several distinct rulings. First, if a person has missed fasts from a previous Ramadan and the next Ramadan arrives, they must make up the missed fasts before starting the new Ramadan; delaying the make-up without reason does not require a ransom, but the obligation remains. Second, a decrepit old man or woman who is physically declining and unable to fast (with continuous incapacity until death) is permitted to break the fast and must pay a ransom of half a sa' of wheat for each missed day. This is similar to someone who vowed to fast continuously but cannot due to work. If such a person cannot afford the ransom, they simply seek Allah's forgiveness. The passage then clarifies that if a person owes an expiation (kaffarah) for an oath or accidental killing and cannot afford it, and they are elderly, they cannot simply pay a ransom for it because in those cases fasting is a substitute for another act (like freeing a slave) and only the person who is originally obligated to fast (like an elderly person) can use ransom. Finally, regarding voluntary fasting: according to one narration from Abu Yusuf, it is permissible to break a voluntary fast without any excuse, though the apparent position of the school is that breaking is only allowed for a valid excuse. Hospitality (for a guest or host) is considered a valid excuse on the strongest view.

Key Terms
متطوع (mutaṭawwi'): One who performs a voluntary (nafl) fast.
ضيافة (ḍiyāfah): Hospitality; considered a valid excuse to break a voluntary fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, under what condition may a voluntary faster break his fast?
A) At any time without reason.
B) Only if he has a valid excuse, such as hospitality.
C) Only if he is sick.
D) He can never break it.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi view: a voluntary faster may only break with a valid excuse; hospitality is one such excuse.

Fidyah for the Elderly Who Cannot Fast
Ruling on who must pay fidyah for missed fasts due to incapacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for an elderly person who cannot fast, including the obligation of fidyah and its amount.

Core Idea
Elderly with permanent incapacity must pay fidyah; delaying qada is not penalized.

Fasting in Ramadan is an obligation, but Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity. For those who are elderly and continually declining in health, the Hanafi school provides a concession: they may break their fast and instead pay a compensatory payment called fidyah. This lesson clarifies who qualifies, what the fidyah entails, and how it differs from making up missed fasts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the eligible person
Recognize that the ruling applies to a 'shaykh fān' or 'ajūz fāniyah'—an elderly person whose strength is permanently declining, not just temporarily ill.
Permanent incapacity is key.
2
Determine the obligation
Once identified, the ruling is that they may break the fast and must pay fidyah for each missed day.
Fidyah is wajib, not optional.
3
Know the amount of fidyah
The fidyah is half a sa' of wheat (or equivalent in dates or barley).
Approximately 1.5–2 kg of wheat.
4
Understand the exception
If the person cannot afford the fidyah, they are not held liable; they should seek forgiveness from Allah and ask for pardon.
Hardship removes the obligation.
5
Distinguish from qada
This fidyah is for permanent incapacity. For temporary illness, qada (making up fasts later) is required. Also, if a new Ramadan arrives, it takes precedence over qada from the previous year, and no fidyah is due for delaying.
No fidyah for delaying qada.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عوارض الصوم فإن جاء رمضان آخر قدم على القضاء، ولا فدية بالتأخير إليه، ويجوز الفطر لشيخ فان وعجوز فانية، وتلزمهما الفدية لكل يوم نصف صاع من بر كمن نذر صوم الأبد، فضعف عنه لاشتغاله بالمعيشة يفطر ويفدي فإن لم يقدر على الفدية لعسرته يستغفر الله تعالى ويستقيله
If another Ramadan comes, it takes precedence over making up (missed fasts), and there is no fidyah for delaying until it. It is permissible to break the fast for an elderly man who is failing and an elderly woman who is failing, and both are obligated to pay fidyah for each day, half a sa' of wheat, like one who vowed to fast forever but became weak due to preoccupation with livelihood; he breaks his fast and pays fidyah. If he cannot afford the fidyah due to hardship, he seeks forgiveness from Allah and asks Him for pardon.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This is a ruling from the chapter on impediments to fasting (عوارض الصوم). The text states that if a person has outstanding missed fasts (qada) from a previous Ramadan and a new Ramadan arrives, the new Ramadan must be observed first; the qada is postponed. No fidyah is required for delaying the qada to the next Ramadan. Then it addresses those who are physically incapable of fasting due to old age and constant decline (الشيخ الفان والعجوز الفانية): they are permitted to break their fast and must pay fidyah for each missed day, which is half a sa' of wheat (approximately 1.5-2 kg). This ruling also applies to one who vowed to fast continuously but cannot due to work necessities. If such a person cannot afford fidyah, they should seek Allah's forgiveness and ask for remission, as the obligation is beyond their capacity.

Key Terms
شيخ فان (shaykh fān): An elderly person whose strength and health are continuously declining until death.
فدية (fidyah): A compensatory payment of food (half sa' of wheat or equivalent) for each missed fast.
عوارض الصوم (awāriḍ al-ṣawm): Impediments that affect the obligation or validity of fasting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the fidyah for each missed fast for an elderly person who cannot fast?
A) One full sa' of wheat
B) Half a sa' of wheat
C) One mudd of wheat
D) No fidyah, only qada

Key Takeaway
Elderly with permanent inability to fast must pay fidyah of half a sa' per day; if unable to pay, seek forgiveness.

No Fidyah for Substitute Fasting in Kaffarah
Clarifying that fidyah is not allowed for obligatory fasts that are substitutes for other obligations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that fidyah is not permitted for obligatory fasting that serves as a substitute (badal) for another obligation, and explain the rationale (that the fasting is a substitute, not the original act).

Core Idea
Fidyah is only for original acts of worship; a substitute act does not qualify for fidyah.

When we say 'fasting,' it can mean two different things: the original fasting of Ramadan, or fasting that stands in for something else, like kaffarah. The rules are not the same. This passage tackles a common question: if an elderly person can't fast the kaffarah days, can they just pay fidyah like they would for Ramadan? The answer is no—and the reason lies in the nature of the obligation as a substitute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage discusses an elderly person who owes kaffarah (for oath or killing) but cannot free a slave and has not yet performed the substitute fasting. The ruling is: it is not permissible (la yajuz) to pay fidyah.
2
Pinpoint the operative condition
The key condition is that the person becomes 'fani' (incapable of fasting) after the obligation has been established. The original obligation was to free a slave; fasting is a substitute (badal) for that.
The text mentions two scenarios: the person is already elderly before fasting, or they became elderly after not having fasted yet.
3
Extract the reason (talil)
The author explicitly gives the reason: 'Because the fasting here is a substitute for something else.' This means that the ruling of fasting as a badal inherits the characteristics of the original obligation, not of an independent act of worship.
In Hanafi usul, a badal takes the same legal status as the original in terms of compensation and excuses.
4
Contrast with original fasting
In Ramadan, fasting is the original act. If one is permanently unable to fast, fidyah is allowed. But here, the original is freeing a slave; fasting is only a replacement. The inability to perform the replacement does not create a new option of fidyah.
This distinction is crucial: fidyah is tied to the act itself, not its substitute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وجبت عليه كفارة يمين أو قتل، فلم يجد ما يكفّر به من عتق، وهو شيخ فانٍ، أو لم يصم حتى صار فانيا لا يجوز له الفدية؛ لأن الصوم هنا بدل عن غيره
And if an expiation for an oath or killing becomes obligatory upon him, and he cannot find what to expiate with, such as freeing a slave, while he is an elderly person failing, or he did not fast until he became failing, it is not permissible for him to pay fidyah because the fasting here is a substitute for something else.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between two types of fasting: original fasting (like Ramadan) and substitute fasting (like kaffarah). Fidyah is only for those who cannot perform original fasting due to permanent incapacity. For kaffarah, the obligation is to free a slave; if unable, then fasting is an alternative. Since the fasting itself is a substitute, if one becomes unable to fast, one cannot pay fidyah because the original obligation (freeing a slave) is no longer possible. The ruling is that the person must seek forgiveness and it remains a debt until they are able.

Key Terms
كفارة يمين (kaffarat yamīn): Expiation for breaking a valid oath, which includes freeing a slave, feeding/clothing ten poor, or fasting three days.
بدل (badal): A substitute; in this context, fasting is a substitute for freeing a slave in kaffarah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is fidyah not permitted for the elderly person who cannot fast the kaffarah days?
A) Because fidyah is only for children
B) Because the fasting is a substitute (badal) for another obligation
C) Because the person should have fasted earlier
D) Because fidyah is only for voluntary fasts

Key Takeaway
Fidyah is not permitted for substitute fasting (badal); it only applies to original acts like Ramadan fasts.

Breaking a Voluntary Fast: Excuses and Qada
Ruling on breaking a voluntary fast and the exception for prohibited days

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on breaking a voluntary fast, identify the valid excuse of hospitality, and apply the exception of the five prohibited days to the qada requirement.

Core Idea
Breaking a voluntary fast requires an excuse; hospitality suffices, and qada is due unless on Eid or Tashreeq.

Voluntary fasting is a meritorious act, but circumstances may arise that necessitate breaking the fast. This passage from Nur al-Idah clarifies the Hanafi ruling: while one narration allows breaking without any excuse, the authoritative view (zahir al-riwaya) requires a valid reason, such as hospitality. Moreover, even when the fast is broken, qada must be observed unless the fast was on one of the five days when voluntary fasting is forbidden—the two Eids and the three days of Tashreeq. Understanding these nuances ensures your practice aligns with the school's relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is breaking a voluntary fast (nafl sawm). Determine the general permissibility and the conditions that affect it.
The text mentions two narrations: one permissive, one requiring an excuse.
2
Locate the valid excuse
Find the specific excuse mentioned: hospitality (diyafah). Note that it applies to both guest and host.
This is the authoritative excuse per zahir al-riwaya.
3
Determine the consequence of breaking
The text states that breaking generally requires qada (making up the day). Identify the exception: days when voluntary fasting is prohibited (two Eids and days of Tashreeq).
On those five days, no qada is due even if the fast was started and then nullified.
4
Distinguish between narrations
The passage notes a difference within the Hanafi school: one narration (from Abu Yusuf) allows breaking without any excuse, but the most apparent view (zahir al-riwaya) requires an excuse. Recognize which is the mufta bihi (operative) position.
The relied-upon view is that hospitality is a valid excuse, and breaking without an excuse is not approved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز للمتطوع الفطر بلا عذر في رواية، والضيافة عذر على الأظهر للضيف والمضيف. وله البشارة بهذه الفائدة الجليلة، وإذا أفطر على أي حال عليه القضاء إلا إذا شرع متطوعا في خمسة أيام: يومي العيدين، وأيام التشريق، فلا يلزمه قضاؤها بإفسادها في ظاهر الرواية، والله أعلم.
It is permissible for the voluntary faster to break his fast without an excuse in one narration, and hospitality is an excuse according to the most apparent view for both the guest and the host. And he is given glad tidings of this great benefit. And if he breaks his fast in any case, qada is required, except if he began a voluntary fast on five days: the two Eids and the days of Tashreeq, then qada is not required for spoiling them in the apparent narration. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage concerns voluntary fasting (صوم التطوع). According to one narration (from Abu Yusuf), a voluntary faster may break his fast without any excuse, but the zahir al-riwaya (the more authoritative transmission) holds that an excuse is necessary. However, hospitality (ضيافة) is considered a valid excuse, meaning a guest or host may break their voluntary fast to honor the guest. The text also mentions that generally, if one breaks a voluntary fast, qada is required unless the fast was on the two Eids and the days of Tashreeq (11-13 Dhul Hijja), in which case no qada is needed—even if nullified intentionally—according to the apparent narration.

Key Terms
متطوع (mutatawwi'): One who performs a voluntary act of worship, here specifically a voluntary fast (nafl).
ضيافة (diyāfah): Hospitality; considered a valid excuse to break a voluntary fast.
ظاهر الرواية (zāhir al-riwāyah): The apparent/authoritative transmission of the Hanafi school from the six core books of Imam Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the most apparent view (ظاهر الرواية), what happens if you break a voluntary fast on a day other than the prohibited days?
A) No qada needed
B) Qada is required
C) Fidyah is required
D) Only if broken with excuse

Key Takeaway
Breaking a voluntary fast generally requires qada unless the fast was started on one of the five prohibited days (Eids and Tashreeq), and the relied-upon view is that a valid excuse like hospitality is needed for breaking.

Rulings on Breaking Voluntary Fasts and Qada
Hanafi rulings on breaking voluntary fasts and qada requirements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qada when breaking a voluntary fast on prohibited days and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute between Zahir al-Riwayah and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Breaking a voluntary fast on the five prohibited days does not require qada in the relied-upon Hanafi view.

This passage addresses the general principle that breaking a voluntary fast triggers qada, but carves out an exception for the five days when voluntary fasting is invalid from the start. It also presents a proof from hadith and notes an internal disagreement among the early Hanafi masters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The matn states that qada is obligatory ('عليه القضاء') for breaking a fast in any case, whether voluntary or obligatory, intentional or unintentional, with or without excuse.
This establishes the default: qada is wajib for any broken fast.
2
Note the exception for prohibited days
The exception is 'إلا إذا شرع متطوعا في خمسة أيام: يومي العيدين، وأيام التشريق' — if one begins a voluntary fast on the two Eids or the three days of Tashriq, then qada is not required upon breaking it.
These days are haram for fasting; the fast is invalid ab initio, so spoiling it has no effect.
3
Understand the hadith proof
The hadith from al-Tajnīs wa al-Mazīd permits breaking a voluntary fast for a brother's sake, promising reward for doing so and greater reward for making it up. This indicates permissibility and that qada is not sinful to omit, but the hadith does not address the exception directly.
The hadith is zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalalah; it supports the general permissibility but is not the primary proof for the exception.
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The commentator notes: 'في ظاهر الرواية' — according to Zahir al-Riwayah, no qada; but 'عن أبي يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله عليه القضاء وإن وجب الفطر' — Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that qada is required even if breaking was obligatory.
The Sahibayn argue that the general rule of qada applies regardless of the validity of starting.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The phrase 'في ظاهر الرواية' signals that the relied-upon position is the Zahir al-Riwayah view: no qada for breaking a voluntary fast on the five prohibited days.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله البشارة بهذه الفائدة الجليلة، وإذا أفطر على أي حال عليه القضاء إلا إذا شرع متطوعا في خمسة أيام: يومي العيدين، وأيام التشريق، فلا يلزمه قضاؤها بإفسادها في ظاهر الرواية، والله أعلم.
وله البشارة: قال في "التجنيس والمزيد": رجل أصبح صائماً متطوعاً فدخل على أخ من إخوانه فسأله أن يفطر، لا بأس بأن يفطر؛ لقول النبي الله : " من أفطر لحق أخيه يكتب له ثواب صوم ألف يوم، ومتى قضى يوماً يكتب له ثواب صوم ألفي يوم". [مراقي الفلاح: ۲۹۰، ۲۹۱]
على أي حال إلخ أي سواء كان الفطر لعذر أم لا، وسواء أفسده قصداً أم لا، وهذا إذا شرع قصداً، فلو شرع فيه ظناً أنه عليه فتذكر أنه ليس عليه شيء فأفطر فوراً فلا قضاء عليه، أما لو مضى ساعة لزمه القضاء؛ لأنه مضيها صار كأنه نوى في هذه الساعة. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٦٩١] في ظاهر الرواية وعن أبي يوسف ومحمد رحمها عليه القضاء وإن وجب الفطر. [مراقي الفلاح: ٦٩١]
And he has glad tidings through this magnificent benefit. And if he breaks the fast in any case, qada is obligatory upon him except if he began voluntarily on five days: the two Eids and the days of Tashriq, then qada is not required for spoiling them according to the apparent narration, and Allah knows best.
And he has glad tidings: It is said in al-Tajnīs wa al-Mazīd: A man became fasting voluntarily and entered upon a brother of his who asked him to break the fast—there is no harm in his breaking it because of the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): "Whoever breaks the fast for the sake of his brother, there is recorded for him the reward of fasting a thousand days, and whenever he makes up a day, there is recorded for him the reward of fasting two thousand days." [Marāqī al-Falāḥ: 290, 291]
"In any case" etc. means whether the breaking was due to an excuse or not, and whether he spoiled it intentionally or not. And this applies if he began intentionally. But if he began thinking it was obligatory upon him and then remembered it was not upon him and broke immediately, then no qada is upon him. But if an hour passed, qada becomes obligatory because by passing that hour, he becomes as if he intended (the fast) during that hour. [Hāshiyah al-Taḥṭāwī: 691] According to the apparent narration, and from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them): qada is upon him even if breaking the fast was obligatory. [Marāqī al-Falāḥ: 691]
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on voluntary fasting (sawm al-tatawwu') and the consequences of breaking it. The matn first gives a general rule: whoever breaks a fast—whether voluntary or obligatory—must make it up (qada), except if he started the fast voluntarily on the five prohibited days: the two Eids (al-Adha and al-Fitr) and the three days of Tashriq. On these days, voluntary fasting is not valid to start, so breaking it does not require qada. This is the position of Zahir al-Riwayah, the relied-upon transmission from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani. The commentator then brings a hadith from al-Tajnīs wa al-Mazīd showing permissibility of breaking a voluntary fast for a brother's request, with immense reward. The commentary from al-Tahtawi clarifies that qada is not required if one realizes immediately after starting that it was not obligatory and breaks right away; delay makes it as if he intended the fast. Finally, an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is noted: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that qada is required even if breaking was obligatory (e.g., due to an excuse), contrary to the Zahir al-Riwayah position. The mufta bihi (operative position) is the Zahir al-Riwayah, as indicated by the phrase 'fi zahir al-riwayah'.

Key Terms
متطوع (mutatawwi‘): One who performs a supererogatory (voluntary) act of worship.
قضاء (qaḍā’): Making up a missed or broken obligatory or voluntary fast on another day.
ظاهر الرواية (ẓāhir al-riwāyah): The apparent or well-known transmission from the early Hanafi masters (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) as recorded in the six books of Imam Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Zahir al-Riwayah, if someone begins a voluntary fast on the day of Eid al-Fitr and then breaks it, is qada required?
A) Yes, qada is always required for breaking a voluntary fast.
B) No, qada is not required because the fast was invalid from the start.
C) It depends on the intention.
D) Only if the fast was broken intentionally.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that no qada is due for breaking a voluntary fast on the five prohibited days (Eid and Tashriq).

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
8 min
Conditions for Valid Vows and Ruling on Fasting on Prohibited Days
Explains conditions for binding vows and specific applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for a binding vow and apply them to determine the validity of vowing to fast on Eid days.

Core Idea
A valid vow must be of an inherently obligatory type, intended for its own sake, and not already obligatory.

Vows (nathr) create a personal obligation upon the one who makes them. But not all acts can be validly vowed. This passage from Nur al-Idah lays down three conditions that a vowed act must meet to be binding. It then explores specific cases, including the contested issue of vowing to fast on the Eid days. Understanding these conditions helps you distinguish between a valid vow that must be fulfilled and one that is legally inconsequential.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions for a valid vow
The passage states three conditions: 1) The vowed act must be of a type that is inherently obligatory (wajib li dhatihi). 2) It must be intended for its own sake (maqsud). 3) It must not already be obligatory (like the five daily prayers).
These conditions ensure the vow creates a new obligation, not a redundant one.
2
Apply the conditions to the negative examples
Wudu' is a means to prayer, not an end in itself, so condition 2 fails. Sajdat al-tilawah is already obligatory by Allah, so condition 3 fails. Visiting the sick is not of a type that is inherently obligatory, so condition 1 fails.
Acts that are obligatory as a consequence (wajib bi ghayrihi) are not valid to vow.
3
Apply the conditions to the positive examples
Freeing slaves: its type is obligatory in expiations. I'tikaf: its type (staying with worship) is analogous to the final sitting in prayer, which is wajib. Supererogatory prayer and fasting: their type is wajib (prayer and fasting are obligatory in essence).
The qiyas for i'tikaf shows the illa is 'staying with intention of worship'.
4
Analyze the dispute on Eid fasts
Though fasting on Eid is prohibited, the Hanafi chosen position (mukhtar) holds the vow valid. The prohibition is due to an external attribute (turning away from Allah's hospitality), so the vow attaches to the essence (which is wajib). Thus the vow is valid, but fasting is sinful; one must break and make up.
An intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf exists; some say the vow is invalid.
5
Apply the rule that specification is null
Specifying a particular time, place, or person in a vow is considered null (laghw). One may substitute a different time or recipient, as long as the act itself is performed.
This facilitates fulfillment of vows without undue restriction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم
باب ما يلزم الوفاء به من منذور الصوم والصلاة ونحوهما
إذا نذر شيئاً لزمه الوفاء به إذا اجتمع فيه ثلاثة شروط: أن يكون من جنسه لذاته لا لغيره واجب، وأن يكون مقصودا، وأن يكون ليس واجبا فلا يلزم الوضوء بنذره، ولا سجدة التلاوة، ولا عيادة المريض، ولا الواجبات بنذرها، ويصح بالعتق والاعتكاف والصلاة غير المفروضة والصوم فإن نذر نذرا مطلقا أو معلقا بشرط مما يصح نذره غير مقيد بوجود شيء ووجد، لزمه الوفاء به وصح نذر صوم العيدين وأيام التشريق في المختار، ويجب فطرها وقضاؤها، وإن صامها أجزأه مع الحرمة وألغينا تعيين الزمان والمكان، لأنه أداه كما التزم والتصدق بدرهم عن درهم عيّنه له، والصرف لزيد الفقير بنذره لعمرو، وإن علق النذر بشرط لا يجزئه عنه ما فعله قبل وجود شرطه.
اعلم أن الأصل في صحة النذر أن لا يكون المنذور واجباً، ولكن من جنسه واجب لله تعالى واجب قصداً لا تبعاً؛ لأن الأصل في العبادة: الدوام لتواتر نعمه في كل لحظة، وتتابع إحسانه في كل لمحة إلا أن الله تعالى اكتفى بإيجاب خمس صلوات في كل يوم وليلة تيسيراً للأمر على عباده والعبد بنذره يريد أن يتمسك بالعزيمة، ويلحق المنذور بما هو الواجب، ومن شرط إلحاق الشيء بالشيء أن يتحقق ذلك الشيء. وقولنا: قصداً لا تبعاً وهذا؛ لأن ما يكون واجباً تبعاً يكون مباحاً لعينه، فلم يكن النذر به إلحاقاً بالواجب، بل يكون نذراً بالمباح، والنذر بالمباح لا يصح، فلذا لا يصح النذر بعيادة المريض؛ واجب، ولا بالوضوء ولا بقراءة القرآن؛ لأنهما وجبا للصلاة وليس من جنسهما واجب لعينه ولا يلزم صحة النذر بالاعتكاف؛ لأن من جنسه وهو اللبث واجبا على العباد لعينه، وهو الوقوف في الصلاة، والثاني أن النذر بالاعتكاف إنما صح؛ لكونه إدامة للصلاة، وإنها واجبة لعينها، ولهذا لم يصح الاعتكاف في غير المسجد. (الكفاية بزيادة) ثلاثة شروط: زيد شرط رابع أن لا يكون المنذور محالاً كقوله: علي صوم أمس اليوم؛ إذ لا يلزمه، وكذا لو قال: اليوم وكان بعد الزوال. مراقي الفلاح واجب فإن قلت: فكيف يصح النذر بصوم يوم النحر، وهو حرام؟ قلت أراد أن يكون واجبا بأصله وإن حرم إرتكابه لوصفه، فإن الصوم من جنسه فرض، ولكن بوصفه، وهو الإعراض عن ضيافة الله تعالى حرام. ليس واجبا أي لا يكون واجبا قبل نذره بإيجاب الله تعالى كالصلوات الخمس. (مراقي الفلاح) فلا يلزم الوضوء إلخ : أما عدم لزوم الوضوء؛ فلكونه ليس مقصوداً بالذات؛ لأنه شرع شرطاً لغيره كحل الصلاة، وأما عدم لزوم سجدة التلاوة؛ فلأنها واجبة بإيجاب الشارع، وأما عدم لزوم عيادة المريض؛ فلأنه ليس من جنسها واجب، وإيجاب العبد معتبر بإيجاب الله تعالى، فما كان من جنسه عبادة أوجبها الله تعالى صح نذره، وإلا لا؛ إذ له الاتباع لا الابتداع وأما عدم صحة نذر الواجبات؛ فلأن إيجاب الواجب محال. ويصح بالعتق إلخ: أما صحة النذر بالعتق فلافتراض التحرير في الكفارات نصاً ، وأما صحته بالاعتكاف؛ فلأن له أصلاً واجباً، وهو القعدة الأخيرة في الصلاة، فأصل المكث بهذه الصفة له نظير في الشرع، والاعتكاف انتظار للصلاة فهو كالجالس في الصلاة، فإذن صح نذره، وأما صحته بالصلاة غير المفروضة والصوم فظاهر.
ثم قال: فإن نذر نذرا مطلقا أو معلقا إلخ: يريد كونه كقوله: إن رزقني الله غلاماً فعلي إطعام عشرة مساكين. [مراقي الفلاح] مع الحرمة الورود النهي عن صوم هذه الأيام والأصل في هذا: مطلق النذر يتناول الكامل، فلا يخرج عن عهدة النذر فيه بالناقص، وأما إذا كان نذره مضافاً إلى الناقص، فيؤدى به؛ لأنه ما التزم إلا هذا القدر، وقد أدى كما التزم كمن قال الله علي أن أعتق هذه الرقبة وهي عمياء خرج عن نذره بإعتاقها، وإن كان مطلق النذر أو شيء من الواجبات لا يتأدى بها كمن نذر أن يصلي عند طلوع الشمس، فعليه أن يصلي في وقت آخر، وإن صلى في ذلك الوقت خرج عن نذره كذا في المبسوط. [الكفاية] والتصدق أي يجزئه التصدق بدرهم إلخ؛ لكون تعيين الدرهم لغوا والصرف أي يجزئه الصرف إلخ؛ لكون تعيين الفقير لغوا وعلق النذر بشرط كقوله: إن قدم زيد فلله علي أن أتصدق بكذا، فتصدق قبل قدوم زيد. [مراقي الفلاح بزيادة]
Book of Fasting. Chapter: What must be fulfilled from vowed fasts, prayers, and similar. If one vows something, fulfilling it becomes obligatory if three conditions are met: that it is of a type that is obligatory in itself not due to something else, that it is intended (for its own sake), and that it is not already obligatory. So wudu' is not binding by vow, nor prostration of recitation, nor visiting the sick, nor obligatory acts by vowing them. It is valid (the vow) regarding freeing slaves, i'tikaf, non-obligatory prayer, and fasting. If one vows an absolute vow or one conditional on a condition that is valid but not linked to the existence of something and it occurs, fulfilling it becomes obligatory. In the chosen position, the vow to fast on the two Eids and the days of Tashriq is valid, and it is obligatory to break them and make them up. If one fasts them, it suffices with sinfulness. And we nullify the specification of time and place, because one performed as one committed. And donating a dirham (is valid) for a dirham he specified, and giving to Zayd the poor for his vow to 'Amr. If the vow is conditional on a condition, what was done before the condition's existence does not suffice him. Know that the basis for the validity of a vow is that the vowed thing not be obligatory, but something of which a type is obligatory for Allah, obligatory in intent not as a consequence; because the basis in worship is continuity due to the succession of His favors every moment and the sequence of His bounty every glance, except that Allah sufficed with obligating five prayers every day and night to ease matters for His servants. The servant by his vow intends to adhere to determination and to attach the vowed thing to what is obligatory. A condition of attaching something to something is that that thing be realized. Our saying: 'in intent not as a consequence' is because what is obligatory as a consequence is permissible in itself, so the vow would not be an attachment to the obligatory, but rather a vow of the permissible, and a vow of the permissible is not valid. Hence the vow to visit the sick is not valid (since it is obligatory as a consequence? actually it is not of a type that is obligatory in itself). Similarly for wudu' and reciting Quran, because they became obligatory for prayer, and their type is not obligatory in itself. The validity of the vow of i'tikaf is because its type, staying, is incumbent upon servants in itself, namely standing in prayer. Second, the vow of i'tikaf is valid because it is a continuation of prayer, and prayer is obligatory in itself. For this reason, i'tikaf is not valid outside a mosque. (Al-Kifaya with addition: three conditions, a fourth condition was added: that the vowed thing not be impossible, like saying 'upon me is fasting yesterday today' – it is not binding, and similarly if he said 'today' after noon.) Maraqi al-Falah: 'obligatory' – if you say: how is the vow to fast on the day of Nahr valid while it is prohibited? I say: it intended to be obligatory in its origin even though its performance is prohibited due to an attribute. For fasting is of a type that is obligatory, but with the attribute of turning away from Allah's hospitality, it is prohibited. 'Not obligatory' meaning it is not obligatory before the vow by Allah's obligation like the five prayers. (Maraqi al-Falah) So wudu' is not binding etc.: As for wudu' not being binding, it is because it is not intended in itself; it was legislated as a condition for something else like the validity of prayer. As for prostration of recitation not being binding, it is because it is obligatory by the Legislator's obligation (already). As for visiting the sick not being binding, it is because its type is not obligatory. The servant's obligation is considered by Allah's obligation: what is of a type that is an act of worship that Allah obligated, his vow is valid; otherwise not, for he is to follow, not innovate. As for the invalidity of vowing obligatory acts, it is because obligating what is already obligatory is absurd. 'And it is valid by freeing slaves etc.': As for the validity of the vow to free slaves, it is because freeing is prescribed in expiations explicitly. As for its validity regarding i'tikaf, it is because it has an obligatory origin, which is the final sitting in prayer. So the origin of staying with this quality has a parallel in the Law, and i'tikaf is waiting for prayer, so it is like one sitting in prayer. Thus his vow is valid. As for its validity regarding non-obligatory prayer and fasting, it is obvious. Then he said: 'If he vows an absolute vow or conditional etc.': he means it like saying 'If Allah grants me a son, upon me is feeding ten poor people.' [Maraqi al-Falah] 'With sinfulness' refers to the prohibition of fasting these days. The principle in this: an absolute vow includes the perfect, so one does not discharge its obligation with the deficient. But if his vow was specific to the deficient, it is performed with it because he only committed to that amount and performed as committed, like one who says 'Upon me is to free this slave' and she is blind; he discharges his vow by freeing her, even though if it were an absolute vow or something of obligations, it would not be discharged by it. For example, one who vows to pray at sunrise must pray at another time, and if he prays at that time, he discharges his vow. This is from al-Mabsut. [Al-Kifaya] 'And donating' means it suffices him to donate a dirham etc. because specifying the dirham is null. 'And giving' means it suffices him to give etc. because specifying the poor person is null. And if the vow is conditional on a condition, like saying 'If Zayd comes, upon me is to give such-and-such in charity', and he gives charity before Zayd's arrival (it does not suffice). [Maraqi al-Falah with addition]
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage from the book of fasting addresses the legal rulings of vows (nathr), specifically what vows are binding and must be fulfilled. It begins by stating three essential conditions for a vow to be binding: 1) The vowed act must be of a type that is inherently obligatory (wajib li dhatihi), not obligatory because of something else. 2) It must be intended for its own sake (maqsud). 3) It must not already be an obligatory act (like the five daily prayers). The text then applies these conditions: vowing to perform wudu' is not binding because wudu' is a means (wasilah) to prayer, not an end in itself. Similarly, prostration of recitation (sajdat al-tilawah) is already obligatory by divine command, so vowing it is superfluous. Visiting the sick, though recommended, is not of a type that is inherently obligatory, so vowing it is invalid. Acts already obligatory, like the five prayers, cannot be vowed because one cannot render obligatory what is already obligatory. The passage then lists acts whose vow is valid: freeing slaves (because it is a prescribed expiation), i'tikaf (seclusion in mosque for worship), supererogatory prayers, and fasting. It explains the validity of i'tikaf through analogy (qiyas): the final sitting (qa'dah akhirah) in prayer is obligatory, and i'tikaf shares the quality of staying (lubth) with the intention of worship, so it becomes valid by analogy. A fourth condition is mentioned: the vowed act must not be impossible, e.g., 'I vow to fast yesterday.' The text then addresses a key issue: vowing to fast on Eid days (Eid al-Fitr, Eid al-Adha, and days of Tashriq). In the relied-upon Hanafi position (al-mukhtar), such a vow is valid, but fasting on these days is haram. Therefore, one must break the fast and make it up later. If one fasts, it technically fulfills the vow (with sin). The specification of time and place in vows is considered null (laghw), so one can substitute a different time or place. Similarly, specifying a particular coin or poor person is null; one can give any dirham to any poor person. Finally, if the vow is conditional (e.g., 'If Allah gives me a son, I will feed ten poor people'), the action must be performed after the condition is met; doing it before does not fulfill the vow.

Key Terms
نذر (nathr): A verbal commitment to perform a specific act of worship for Allah, making it obligatory upon oneself.
واجب (wajib): An act that is obligatory; here it distinguishes original wajib (by Allah) from wajib by vow.
لغو (laghw): Null, irrelevant; specification in a vow (of time, place, object, or person) is ignored and does not affect validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following vows is NOT binding according to the Hanafi conditions mentioned in the passage?
A) Vowing to fast on a Monday
B) Vowing to perform wudu'
C) Vowing to pray two rak'ahs at night
D) Vowing to free a slave

Key Takeaway
A valid vow requires the vowed act to be of an inherently obligatory type, intended for its own sake, and not already obligatory; specification of time, place, or person is null.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
8 min
Rulings on Vows (Nadhr) Concerning Fasting
Rulings on what is binding in vows with focused cases on fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on vows related to fasting and apply the principle that designations of time, place, and recipient are void in absolute vows.

Core Idea
In vows, designations of time, place, and recipient are void; only the act itself matters.

This lesson examines what makes a vow binding and how to fulfill it, especially when fasting is involved. You will learn that even a vow to fast on Eid is valid but the fast must be broken, and that substitutive fulfillment is allowed due to the nullification of specifications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the vow's validity
Determine whether the vow is absolute or conditional, and ensure the condition (if any) is valid. The vow becomes binding when the condition occurs or if absolute, immediately.
A vow for an act of worship that is not inherently obligatory is binding.
2
Apply the ruling for Eid fasting
Even though fasting on Eid is prohibited, the vow to fast on those days is valid in the chosen opinion. The person must break the fast; if he fasts, it suffices but he commits a sin.
There is also a narration that the vow is not valid, but the chosen opinion holds otherwise.
3
Nullify designations
In an absolute vow, any specification of time, place, or recipient is void. So one may fulfill the vow by performing the act at a different time or giving charity to another poor person.
Example: fasting Rajab suffices for a vow to fast Sha'ban.
4
Handle conditional vows
If a vow is tied to a condition (e.g., 'If Zayd arrives, I must give charity'), the condition must occur before the fulfillment is valid. Acting before the condition does not discharge the vow.
This is because the vow is contingent upon the condition.
5
Understand the completeness principle
An absolute vow requires the complete act. But if the vow itself specifies a deficient act (e.g., freeing a blind slave), that deficient act suffices because it is what was committed.
The maxim: the absolute vow encompasses the complete, but a restricted vow adheres to its restriction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يلزم الوفاء به والاعتكاف والصلاة غير المفروضة ،والصوم فإن نذر نذرا مطلقا أو معلقا بشرط مما يصح نذره غير مقيد بوجود شيء ووجد، لزمه الوفاء به وصح نذر صوم العيدين وأيام التشريق في المختار، ويجب أي الفطر والأضحى وفي رواية لا يصح فطرها وقضاؤها، وإن صامها أجزأه مع الحرمة وألغينا تعيين الزمان والمكان، لأنه أداه كما التزم والدرهم والفقير، فيجزئه صوم رجب عن نذره صوم شعبان ويجزئه صلاة ركعتين لكون تعيين الزمان لغواً لكون تعيين المكان لغوا بمصر نذر أداءهما بمكة، والتصدق بدرهم عن درهم عيّنه له، والصرف لزيد الفقير بنذره لعمرو، وإن علق النذر بشرط لا يجزئه عنه ما فعله قبل وجود شرطه. أو معلقاً : : يريد كونه كقوله: إن رزقني الله غلاماً فعلي إطعام عشرة مساكين. [مراقي الفلاح: ٦٩٤] مع الحرمة [الورود؟] النهي عن صوم هذه الأيام والأصل في هذا: مطلق النذر يتناول الكامل، فلا يخرج عن عهدة النذر فيه بالناقص، وأما إذا كان نذره مضافاً إلى الناقص، فيؤدى به؛ لأنه ما التزم إلا هذا القدر، وقد أدى كما التزم كمن قال الله علي أن أعتق هذه الرقبة وهي عمياء خرج عن نذره بإعتاقها، وإن كان مطلق النذر أو شيء من الواجبات لا يتأدى بها كمن نذر أن يصلي عند طلوع الشمس، فعليه أن يصلي في وقت آخر، وإن صلى في ذلك الوقت خرج عن نذره كذا في "المبسوط". [الكفاية: ٢٩٩/٢] والتصدق أي يجزئه التصدق بدرهم إلخ؛ لكون تعيين الدرهم .لغوا والصرف أي يجزئه الصرف إلخ؛ لكون تعيين الفقير لغوا علق النذر بشرط كقوله : إن قدم زيد فلله علي أن أتصدق بكذا ، فتصدق قبل قدوم زيد. مراقي الفلاح بزيادة : ٦٩٨]
What is binding to fulfill: and i'tikaf, and non-obligatory prayer, and fasting. For if one makes a vow, absolute or conditional upon a valid condition, not restricted to the existence of something, and it occurs, it becomes binding to fulfill it. And the vow of fasting on the two Eids and the days of Tashreeq is valid in the chosen opinion, and he must break them (i.e., on Fitr and Adha), and in one narration it is not valid to break them and make them up; but if he fasts them, it suffices him despite the prohibition, and we nullify the designation of time and place, because he performed it as he committed. And the dirham and the poor person: so fasting Rajab suffices for his vow of fasting Sha'ban, and praying two rak'ahs suffices because the designation of time is void, and because the designation of place is void: if he vowed to perform them in Makkah while in Egypt, and giving a dirham for a specific dirham he designated, and spending for Zayd the poor by his vow for 'Amr. And if he makes the vow conditional upon a condition, what he does before the existence of its condition does not suffice him. Or conditional: meaning like his saying: If Allah grants me a son, then I must feed ten poor people. [Maraqi al-Falah: 694] With the prohibition [of?] the prohibition against fasting these days, and the principle in this: An absolute vow encompasses the complete, so he does not discharge the vow with the deficient. But if his vow is attached to the deficient, then he performs it with it; because he did not commit except this amount, and he performed as he committed, like one who says: By Allah, I must free this slave, and she is blind; he is discharged from his vow by freeing her. And if the vow is absolute or something of obligations, it is not performed by it, like one who vows to pray at sunrise: he must pray at another time, and if he prays at that time, he is discharged from his vow, as in al-Mabsut. [Al-Kifayah: 2/299] And giving charity: i.e., giving charity with a dirham suffices him, etc.; because the designation of the dirham is void. And spending: i.e., spending suffices him, etc.; because the designation of the poor person is void. He made the vow conditional upon a condition, like his saying: If Zayd arrives, I must give charity with such-and-such, and he gave charity before Zayd's arrival. Maraqui al-Falah with addition: 698
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of Nur al-Idah, expanded by commentary, regarding what is binding in vows, focusing on fasting. The author first states that vows concerning i'tikaf, non-obligatory prayer, and fasting are binding if made absolutely or upon a valid condition. Then specific cases are given: a vow to fast on Eid or Tashreeq days is valid in the chosen opinion, but the fast must be broken; if fasted, it suffices but with sin. The principle is that designations of time, place, or specific recipient are void in an absolute vow, so substitution is valid. For example, fasting Rajab suffices for a vow to fast Sha'ban. However, if the vow itself specifies a deficiency (like a blind slave), that is what must be fulfilled. The commentary also clarifies that if a vow is conditional, the condition must occur first. The reasoning is based on the principle that an absolute vow requires the complete, so the deficient does not discharge it, unless the vow was attached to the deficient.

Key Terms
نذر (nadhr): A vow: an obligation to perform an act of worship not inherently obligatory, made by saying 'For Allah upon me to...'
تعيين (ta'yin): Designation: specifying a particular time, place, or recipient in a vow.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone vows to fast the month of Sha'ban but instead fasts Rajab, what is the ruling?
A) It does not fulfill the vow because the month is different.
B) It fulfills the vow because the designation of time is void.
C) He must make up the fast in Sha'ban.
D) The vow is invalid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, absolute vows are flexible: designations of time, place, and recipient are void, allowing substitution while still fulfilling the obligation.
